P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma _ { + } } } \cap P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma _ { - } } } = P _ { \varphi _ { \rho } } .
$$

Definition 2.4 (Monomial ideals).
A (monoid) ideal of a monoid $P$ is a subset $I \subset P$ such that $p \in I , q \in P$ implies $p + q \in I$ .
An ideal determines a monomial ideal in the monoid ring $\mathbb { k } \lfloor P \rfloor$ , generated by monomials $z ^ { p }$ for $p \in { I }$ .
We also denote this ideal by $I$ , hopefully with no confusion.
As a consequence, we shall sometimes write certain ideal operations either additively or multiplicatively, i.e., for $J \subset P$ ,

$$
k J = \{ p _ { 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + p _ { k } | p _ { i } \in J , 1 \leq i \leq k \} ,
$$

and the corresponding monomial ideal is $J ^ { k }$ .

Let ${ \mathfrak { m } } = P \setminus P ^ { \times }$ .
This is the unique maximal ideal of $P$ , defining a monomial ideal ${ \mathfrak { m } } \subset \mathbb { k } [ P ]$ .
Note $\mathbb { k } [ P ] / { \mathfrak { m } } \cong \mathbb { k } [ P ^ { \times } ]$ .

We say an ideal $I \subset P$ is $\mathrm { m }$ -primary if

$$
\mathfrak { m } = \sqrt { I } : = \{ p \in P | \mathrm { t h e r e ~ e x i s t s ~ a ~ p o s i t i v e ~ i n t e g e r ~ } k \mathrm { ~ s u c h ~ t h a t ~ } k p \in I \} ,
$$

in which case the same holds for the associated monomial ideal $I \subset \mathbb { k } \lfloor P \rfloor$ .

Recall from §1.3 that we are only considering toric monoids $P$ , i.e., monoids which are the intersection of rational polyhedral cones $\sigma _ { P }$ with lattices.
Such monoids are always finitely generated, so that $\mathbb { k } [ P ]$ is Noetherian.
If $\sigma _ { P }$ is strictly convex, then $\mathfrak { m }$ is the maximal ideal corresponding to the unique torus fixed point of $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk | P |$ .

Fix an ideal $I \subset P$ , and recalling that we write $R = \Bbbk [ P ]$ , set

$$
R _ { I } : = \mathbb { k } [ P ] / I .
$$

We define for $\tau \in \Sigma$ , $\dim \tau > 0$ , the ring

$$
R _ { \tau , I } : = \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } } ] \otimes _ { R } R _ { I } ,
$$

noting that $P$ acts naturally on $P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } }$ by addition.
So $\mathrm { S p e c } R _ { \tau , I }$ is a base-change of the Mumford degeneration induced by $\varphi _ { \tau }$ on the localized fan $\tau ^ { - 1 } \Sigma$ .

One observes

Proposition 2.5. Let $v _ { i }$ denote the primitive generator of the tangent ray to $\rho _ { i }$ , for each $i$ .
Then viewing $z ^ { \kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } } \in \mathbb { k } [ P ]$ as determining an element in $R _ { I }$ , we have

$$
{ \frac { R _ { I } [ X _ { i - 1 } , X _ { i } ^ { \pm } , X _ { i + 1 } ] } { ( X _ { i - 1 } X _ { i + 1 } - z ^ { \kappa _ { \rho _ { i } , \varphi } } X _ { i } ^ { - D _ { \rho _ { i } } ^ { 2 } } ) } } \cong R _ { \rho _ { i } , I }
$$

via the map

$$
X _ { j } \mapsto z ^ { \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } ( v _ { j } ) } , \quad j \in \{ i - 1 , i , i + 1 \} .
$$

Furthermore, there are natural maps

$$
\psi _ { \rho _ { i } , - } : R _ { \rho _ { i } , I }  R _ { \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , I } , \quad \psi _ { \rho _ { i } , + } : R _ { \rho _ { i } , I }  R _ { \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I }
$$

induced by the inclusions $P _ { \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } } \subseteq P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma _ { \pm } } }$ which induce isomorphisms

$$
( R _ { \rho _ { i } , I } ) _ { X _ { i - 1 } } \cong R _ { \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , I } , \quad ( R _ { \rho _ { i } , I } ) _ { X _ { i + 1 } } \cong R _ { \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } .
$$

Proof.
We need to check that the ideal on the left-hand side is mapped to zero, as the rest is obvious.
Note by construction of $B$ , $v _ { i - 1 } + D _ { \rho _ { i } } ^ { 2 } v _ { i } + v _ { i + 1 } = 0$ as elements of $\Lambda _ { \rho _ { i } }$ , so one sees in fact that $\varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } ( v _ { i - 1 } ) + \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } ( v _ { i + 1 } ) = \kappa _ { \rho _ { i } , \varphi } - D _ { \rho _ { i } } ^ { 2 } \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } ( v _ { i } )$ .
The result then follows easily.


Remark 2.6. Since Spec $R _ { \rho _ { i } , I }  \mathrm { S p e c } R _ { I }$ is a base-change of the Mumford degeneration, we can in fact say what a fibre of this morphism is over a closed point $x$ in the smallest toric stratum of $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P ]$ , i.e., a point in Spec $R _ { \mathfrak { m } }$ .
This depends on whether $\kappa _ { \rho _ { i } , \varphi } \in \mathcal Ḋ P Ḍ$ is invertible or not.
If it is not invertible, then the fibre is $\mathrm { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ \rho _ { i } ^ { - 1 } \Sigma ] \cong$ $\mathrm { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ X _ { i - 1 } , X _ { i + 1 } , X _ { i } ^ { \pm 1 } ] / ( X _ { i - 1 } X _ { i + 1 } )$ .
If $\kappa _ { \rho _ { i } , \varphi }$ is invertible, then the fibre is $\mathrm { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ \mathbb { Z } ^ { 2 } ]$ .
In this latter case, if $\rho _ { i } ~ \subset ~ \sigma$ , in fact the map $R _ { \rho _ { i } , I } \to R _ { \sigma , I }$ induced by the inclusion $P _ { \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } } \subseteq P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma } }$ is an isomorphism.

Somewhat more generally, if $J \subset P$ is a radical ideal with $\kappa _ { \rho _ { i } , \varphi } \in J$ , then in fact $R _ { \rho _ { i } , J } \cong \operatorname { S p e c } R _ { J } [ \rho _ { i } ^ { - 1 } \Sigma ]$ .

For $\tau \in \Sigma$ , $\dim \tau \geq 1$ , set

$$
U _ { \tau , I } : = \mathrm { S p e c } R _ { \tau , I } .
$$

The maps $\psi _ { \rho _ { i } , \pm }$ induce open immersions $U _ { \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , I } \hookrightarrow U _ { \rho _ { i } , I }$ and $U _ { \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } } \hookrightarrow U _ { \rho _ { i } , I }$ .
Denoting the image of each of these immersions as $U _ { \rho _ { i } , \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , I }$ and $U _ { \rho _ { i } , \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I }$ respectively, we note that

$$
\begin{array} { r } { U _ { \rho _ { i } , \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , I } \cap U _ { \rho _ { i } , \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } \cong \operatorname { S p e c } ( R _ { \rho _ { i } , I } ) _ { X _ { i - 1 } X _ { i + 1 } } \cong ( \mathbb { G } _ { m } ) ^ { 2 } \times \operatorname { S p e c } ( R _ { I } ) _ { z ^ { \kappa _ { \rho _ { i } } , \varphi } } } \end{array}
$$

Note that if $\kappa _ { \rho _ { i } , \varphi } \in \sqrt { I }$ then the localization $( \mathbb { k } [ P ] / I ) _ { z ^ { \kappa \rho _ { i } , \varphi } }$ is zero, and the intersection is empty.

We can now define our analogue of the Mumford degeneration.

Construction 2.7. Suppose that the number of irreducible components $n$ of $\boldsymbol { D }$ satisfies $n \geq 3$ , that $\varphi$ is a PL multivalued function, and $I \subset P$ an ideal such that $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in \sqrt { I }$ for all rays $\rho \in \Sigma$ .
Then there are canonical identifications of open subsets

$$
U _ { \rho _ { i } , I } \supset U _ { \rho _ { i } , \sigma _ { i } , _ { i + 1 } , I } \cong U _ { \sigma _ { i } , _ { i + 1 } , I } \cong U _ { \rho _ { i + 1 } , \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } \subset U _ { \rho _ { i + 1 } , I }
$$

which generate an equivalence relation on $\textstyle \operatorname { L l } _ { i } U _ { \rho _ { i } , I }$ , and the quotient by this equivalence relation defines a scheme $X _ { I } ^ { o }$ over ${ \mathrm { S p e c } } R _ { I }$ .

One checks easily that the canonical isomorphisms of

$$
U _ { \rho _ { i } , \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } \subseteq U _ { \rho _ { i } , I } { \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } } U _ { \rho _ { i + 1 } , \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } \subseteq U _ { \rho _ { i + 1 } , I }
$$

satisfy the requirements for gluing data for schemes along open subsets, see e.g., [H77], Ex. II 2.12.

Remark 2.8. $X _ { I } ^ { o }$ only depends on the equivalence class of $\varphi$ , since the monoids $P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } }$ are canonically defined, independently of the choice of representative for $\varphi$ .

We first analyze this construction in the purely toric case:

Lemma 2.9. For $( Y , D )$ toric and $\varphi$ a single-valued convex function on $B = M _ { \mathbb { R } }$ , $X _ { I } ^ { o }$ is an open subscheme of the Mumford degeneration Spec $\mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi } ] / I \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi } ]$ , and

$$
H ^ { 0 } ( X _ { I } ^ { o } , { \mathcal O } _ { X _ { I } ^ { o } } ) = \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi } ] / I \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi } ] .
$$

Proof.
Note that for $\tau \in \Sigma$ , $\tau \neq \{ 0 \}$ , the monoid $P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } }$ is isomorphic to the localization of $P _ { \varphi }$ along the face $\{ ( m , \varphi ( m ) ) | m \in \tau \cap M \}$ .
Thus $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } } ]$ is an open subset of $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P _ { \varphi } ]$ and $\mathrm { \backslash p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } } ] \otimes _ { \mathbb { k } [ P ] } \mathbb { k } [ P ] / I$ is an open subset of Spec $\mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi } ] / I \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi } ]$ .
Furthermore, the gluing procedure constructing $X _ { I } ^ { o }$ is clearly compatible with these inclusions, so $X _ { I } ^ { o }$ is an open subscheme of $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P _ { \varphi } ] / I \Bbbk [ P _ { \varphi } ]$ .
Next, looking at the fibre over a closed point, one sees easily that the underlying topological space of these fibres is obtained just by removing the zero-dimensional torus orbit from the corresponding fibre of the Mumford degeneration.
The closed fibres of the Mumford degeneration are $S _ { 2 }$ by [A02], 2.3.19. Thus by Lemma 2.10, the result follows.


Lemma 2.10. Let $\pi : \mathcal { X }  S$ be a flat family of surfaces such that the fibre $\mathcal { X } _ { s }$ satisfies Serre’s condition $S _ { 2 }$ for each $s \in S$ .
Let $i \colon \mathcal { X } ^ { o } \subset \mathcal { X }$ be the inclusion of an open subset such that the complement has finite fibres.
Then $i _ { * } { \mathcal { O } } _ { \mathcal { X } ^ { o } } = { \mathcal { O } } _ { \mathcal { X } }$ .
Similarly, if $\mathcal { F }$ is a coherent sheaf on $S$ then $i _ { * } ( { \mathcal { O } } _ { { \mathcal { X } } ^ { 0 } } \otimes \pi ^ { * } { \mathcal { F } } ) = { \mathcal { O } } _ { { \mathcal { X } } } \otimes \pi ^ { * } { \mathcal { F } }$ .

Proof.
For the first statement see, e.g., [H04], Lemma A.3, (the assumption that the fibres are semi log canonical is not used).
The second statement follows from the first by d´evissage.


Definition 2.11. Let $B _ { 0 } ( \mathbb { Z } )$ denote the set of points of $B _ { 0 }$ with integral coordinates in an integral affine chart.
We also write $B ( \mathbb { Z } ) = B _ { 0 } ( \mathbb { Z } ) \cup \{ 0 \}$ .

Given the description of Remark 2.6, the following lemma is obvious.

Lemma 2.12. Suppose $n \geq 3$ and we are given a convex multivalued piecewise linear function $\varphi$ and a radical monomial ideal $J \subset P$ such that $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in J$ for all rays $\rho \in$ $\Sigma$ .
Then if $x \in \operatorname { S p e c } R _ { J }$ is a closed point, the fibre of $X _ { J } ^ { o } ~  ~ \mathrm { S p e c } \ : R _ { J }$ over $x$ is $\left( \mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ \Sigma ] \right) \setminus \{ 0 \}$ .
Here, $\mathbb { k } [ \Sigma ]$ denotes the k-algebra with a k-basis $\{ z ^ { m } | m \in B ( \mathbb { Z } ) \}$ with multiplication given exactly as in (1.6), and 0 is the closed point whose ideal is generated by $\{ z ^ { m } \mid m \neq 0 \}$ .
In particular, the fibre is isomorphic to $\mathbb { V } _ { n } ^ { o }$ .
Furthermore, with $R _ { J } [ \Sigma ] : = R _ { J } \otimes _ { \Bbbk } \Bbbk [ \Sigma ]$ ,

$$
X _ { J } ^ { o } \cong ( \mathrm { S p e c } R _ { J } [ \Sigma ] ) \setminus ( \mathrm { S p e c } R _ { J } ) \times \{ 0 \} .
$$

2.2. Scattering diagrams on $B$ .
Next we translate into algebraic geometry the instanton corrections.
To construct our mirror family we will use the canonical scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathtt { c a m } }$ defined in §3.1, (which is the translation of the instanton corrections associated to Maslov index zero disks), but as the regluing process works for any scattering diagram (and we will make use of this greater generality in [K3]), we carry it out for an arbitrary scattering diagram.

We continue with the notation of the previous sections, with $( Y , D )$ , $( B , \Sigma )$ , $P$ an arbitrary toric monoid, and $\varphi$ given.
We also fix a monomial ideal $J \subset P$ such that $J = { \sqrt { J } }$ .
Denote by $\widehat { R }$ the completion of $\mathbb { k } [ P ]$ with respect to the ideal $J$ , and for any $\tau \in \Sigma$ , $\tau \neq 0$ , denote by

$$
\widehat { \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } } ] }
$$

the completion of the ring $\mathbb { k } | P _ { \varphi \tau } |$ with respect to the ideal $J \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi \tau } ]$ .

We will now define a scattering diagram, which encodes a modification of the construction of $X _ { I } ^ { o }$ .
Unlike the previous subsection, where we assumed $n \geq 3$ for ease of exposition throughout, in this subsection we can allow any number of irreducible components of $D$ except where noted.

Definition 2.13. A scattering diagram for the data $( B , \Sigma )$ , $P , \varphi$ , and $J$ is a set

$$
\mathfrak { D } = \{ ( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \}
$$

where

(1) $\mathfrak { d } \subset B$ is a ray in $B$ with endpoint the origin with rational slope.
$\mathfrak { o }$ may coincide with a ray of $\Sigma$ , or lie in the interior of a two-dimensional cone of $\Sigma$ .

(2) Let $\tau _ { 0 } \in \Sigma$ be the smallest cone containing d.
Then $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ is a formal sum

$$
f _ { \mathfrak { d } } = 1 + \sum _ { p } c _ { p } z ^ { p } \in \widehat { \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \tau _ { \mathfrak { d } } } } ] }
$$

for $c _ { p } \in \mathbb { k }$ and $p$ running over elements of $P _ { \varphi _ { \tau _ { \mathfrak { d } } } }$ such that $r ( p ) \neq 0$ and $r ( p )$ is tangent to $\mathfrak { d }$ .
Here $r$ is defined by (2.1).
We further require that $\mathfrak { o }$ satisfy one of the following two properties:

(a) For those $p$ with $c _ { p } \neq 0$ , $r ( p )$ , viewed as a tangent vector at an interior point of $\mathfrak { o }$ , points towards the origin, in which case we say that $\mathfrak { d }$ is an outgoing ray.  
(b) For those $p$ with $c _ { p } \neq 0$ , $r ( p )$ points away from the origin, in which case we say that $\mathfrak { o }$ is an incoming ray.

(3) If $\dim \tau _ { \mathfrak { d } } = 2$ or if $\dim \tau _ { \mathfrak { d } } = 1$ and $\kappa _ { \tau _ { 0 } , \varphi } \notin J$ , then $f _ { \mathfrak { d } } \equiv 1 \ \mathrm { ~ m o d ~ } J$ .

(4) For any ideal $I \subset P$ with $\sqrt { I } = J$ , there are only a finite number of $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \in \mathfrak { D }$ such that $f _ { \mathfrak { d } } \not \equiv 1 \ \mathrm { ~ m o d ~ } I \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \tau _ { 0 } } } ]$ .

Construction 2.14. We now explain how a scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D }$ is used to modify the construction of $X _ { I } ^ { o }$ , as given in Construction 2.7. Suppose we are given a scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D }$ for the data $( B , \Sigma )$ , $P$ , $\varphi$ and $J$ , and an ideal $I$ with $\sqrt { I } = J$ .
We assume that $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in J$ for all rays $\rho \in \Sigma$ and that $n \geq 3$ as in Construction 2.7.

We will use the scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D }$ to modify both the definition of the rings $R _ { \rho _ { i } , I }$ as well as the gluings of the schemes defined by these rings.
First, we modify the definition of $R _ { \rho _ { i } , I }$ , setting

$$
R _ { \rho _ { i } , I } : = \frac { R _ { I } [ X _ { i - 1 } , X _ { i } ^ { \pm 1 } , X _ { i + 1 } ] } { ( X _ { i - 1 } X _ { i + 1 } - z ^ { \kappa _ { \rho _ { i } , \varphi } } X _ { i } ^ { - D _ { \rho _ { i } } ^ { 2 } } f _ { \rho _ { i } } ) }
$$

Here $f _ { \rho _ { i } }$ is an element of $R _ { I } [ X _ { i } ^ { \pm 1 } ]$ defined by

$$
f _ { \rho _ { i } } = \prod _ { { ( \mathfrak { o } , f _ { \mathfrak { o } } ) \in \mathfrak { D } } \atop { \mathfrak { o } = \rho _ { i } } } f _ { \mathfrak { o } } \mod I \Bbbk [ P _ { \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } } ] ,
$$

identifying $X _ { i }$ with $z \mathcal { S } \rho _ { i } \left( v _ { i } \right)$ as in Proposition 2.5. Note this is a generalization of the old definition of $R _ { \rho _ { i } , I }$ , which we obtain if $f _ { \rho _ { i } } = 1$ .
Thus we continue to use the same notation.

Retaining the definition $R _ { \sigma , I } = \mathbb { k } | P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma } } | \otimes _ { R } R _ { I }$ for $\dim \sigma = 2$ from the previous subsection, we note that there are maps

$$
\psi _ { \rho _ { i } , - } : R _ { \rho _ { i } , I }  R _ { \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , I } , \quad \psi _ { \rho _ { i } , + } : R _ { \rho _ { i } , I }  R _ { \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } ,
$$

given by

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \psi _ { \rho _ { i } , - } ( X _ { i } ) = z ^ { \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } ( v _ { i } ) } , \quad \psi _ { \rho _ { i } , - } ( X _ { i - 1 } ) = z ^ { \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } ( v _ { i - 1 } ) } , \quad \psi _ { \rho _ { i } , - } ( X _ { i + 1 } ) = f _ { \rho _ { i } } z ^ { \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } ( v _ { i + 1 } ) } , } \\ & { \psi _ { \rho _ { i } , + } ( X _ { i } ) = z ^ { \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } ( v _ { i } ) } , \quad \psi _ { \rho _ { i } , + } ( X _ { i - 1 } ) = f _ { \rho _ { i } } z ^ { \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } ( v _ { i - 1 } ) } , \quad \psi _ { \rho _ { i } , + } ( X _ { i + 1 } ) = z ^ { \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } ( v _ { i + 1 } ) } . } \end{array}
$$

Furthermore, $\psi _ { \rho _ { i } , \pm }$ induce isomorphisms

$$
\psi _ { \rho _ { i } , + } : ( R _ { \rho _ { i } , I } ) _ { X _ { i + 1 } }  R _ { \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } , \quad \psi _ { \rho _ { i } , - } : ( R _ { \rho _ { i } , I } ) _ { X _ { i - 1 } }  R _ { \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , I } .
$$

Set for $\tau \in \Sigma \setminus \{ 0 \}$

$$
U _ { \tau , I } : = \mathrm { S p e c } R _ { \tau , I } .
$$

One checks easily that the natural map $U _ { \rho , I } \to \operatorname { S p e c } R _ { I }$ is flat.
The maps $\psi _ { \rho _ { i } , \pm }$ induces canonical embeddings $U _ { \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , I } , U _ { \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } \hookrightarrow U _ { \rho _ { i } , I }$ , and we denote their image by $U _ { \rho _ { i } , \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , I }$ and $U _ { \rho _ { i } , \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I }$ respectively.
Note that (2.6) continues to hold.

Next, consider $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \in \mathfrak { D }$ with $\tau _ { \mathfrak { d } } = \sigma \in \Sigma _ { \mathrm { m a x } }$ .
Let $\gamma$ be a path in $B _ { 0 }$ which crosses $\mathfrak { o }$ transversally at time $t _ { 0 }$ .
Then define

$$
\theta _ { \gamma , 0 } : R _ { \sigma , I }  R _ { \sigma , I }
$$

by

$$
\theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { d } } ( z ^ { p } ) = z ^ { p } f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ^ { \langle n _ { \mathfrak { d } } , r ( p ) \rangle }
$$

where $n _ { 0 } \in \Lambda _ { \sigma } ^ { * }$ is primitive and satisfies, with $m$ a non-zero tangent vector of $\mathfrak { o }$ ,

$$
\langle n _ { \mathsf { 0 } } , m \rangle = 0 , \quad \langle n _ { \mathsf { 0 } } , \gamma ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) \rangle < 0 .
$$

If $\gamma$ is not differentiable at $t _ { 0 }$ , which might occur for broken lines, see Definition 2.16, this inequality is interpreted to mean that $n _ { 0 }$ is positive at $\gamma ( t _ { 0 } - \epsilon )$ and negative at $\gamma ( t _ { 0 } + \epsilon )$ for $\epsilon > 0$ small.
Note that $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ is invertible in $R _ { \sigma , I }$ since $f _ { 0 } \equiv 1 \mod J \mathbb { k } | P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma } } |$ , so $f _ { \mathfrak { d } } - 1$ is nilpotent in $R _ { \sigma , I }$ .

Let $\mathfrak { D } _ { I } \subset \mathfrak { D }$ be the finite set of rays $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } )$ with $f _ { \mathfrak { d } } \not \equiv 1 \ \mathrm { ~ m o d ~ } I \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \tau _ { 0 } } } ]$ .
For a path $\gamma$ wholly contained in the interior of $\sigma \in \Sigma _ { \mathrm { m a x } }$ and crossing elements of ${ \mathfrak { D } } _ { I }$ transversally, we define

$$
\theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { D } } : = \theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { d } _ { n } } \circ \cdot \cdot \cdot \circ \theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { d } _ { 1 } } ,
$$

where $\gamma$ crosses precisely the elements $( \mathfrak { d } _ { 1 } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } _ { 1 } } ) , \dotsc , ( \mathfrak { d } _ { n } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } _ { n } } )$ of ${ \mathfrak { D } } _ { I }$ , in the given order.
Note that if two rays $\mathbf { \delta } _ { 0 } ( \mathbf { \delta } _ { i } , \mathbf { \delta } _ { 0 _ { i + 1 } }$ in fact coincide as subsets of $B$ , then $\theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { d } _ { i } }$ and $\theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { d } _ { i + 1 } }$ commute, so the ordering is not important for overlapping rays.

To construct $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ , we modify the gluings of the sets $U _ { \rho , I }$ along the open subsets $U _ { \rho , \sigma , I }$ .
For each $i$ , we have canonical identifications of open subsets

$$
U _ { \rho _ { i } , I } \supset U _ { \rho _ { i } , \sigma _ { i } , _ { i + 1 } , I } \cong U _ { \sigma _ { i } , _ { i + 1 } , I } \cong U _ { \rho _ { i + 1 } , \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } \subset U _ { \rho _ { i + 1 } , I }
$$

We can modify this identification via any automorphism of $U _ { \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } }$ .
We do this by choosing a path $\gamma _ { i } : [ 0 , 1 ] \to B$ whose image is contained in the interior of $\sigma _ { i , i + 1 }$ , with

![](images/cb19c8b100898de86f52502a9fc17803383e62b53b89de17b1c483f7756d5cd3.jpg)  
Figure 2.1. The path $\gamma _ { i }$ .
The solid lines indicate the fan, the dotted lines are additional rays in $\mathfrak { D }$ .
The solid lines may also support rays in $\mathfrak { D }$

$\gamma _ { i } ( 0 )$ a point in $\sigma _ { i , i + 1 }$ close to $\rho _ { i }$ and $\gamma _ { i } ( 1 ) \in \sigma _ { i , i + 1 }$ close to $\rho _ { i + 1 }$ , chosen so that $\gamma _ { i }$ crosses every ray $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } )$ of ${ \mathfrak { D } } _ { I }$ with $\tau _ { \mathfrak { d } } = \sigma _ { i , i + 1 }$ exactly once, see Figure 2.1.

We then obtain an automorphism

$$
\theta _ { \gamma _ { i } , \mathfrak { D } } : R _ { \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } \to R _ { \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } ,
$$

hence, after taking Spec, an isomorphism

$$
\theta _ { \gamma _ { i } , \mathfrak { D } } : U _ { \rho _ { i + 1 } , \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } \to U _ { \rho _ { i } , \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } .
$$

We now define $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ by dividing out $\textstyle \operatorname { L l } _ { i } U _ { \rho _ { i } , I }$ by the equivalence relation given by identifying $x \in U _ { \rho _ { i + 1 } , \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } \subseteq U _ { \rho _ { i + 1 } , I }$ with $\theta _ { \gamma _ { i } , \mathfrak { D } } ( x ) \in U _ { \rho _ { i } , \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } \subseteq U _ { \rho _ { i } , I }$ .
$\boxed { \begin{array} { r l } \end{array} }$

2.3. Broken lines.
We continue to fix a rational surface with anti-canonical cycle $( Y , D )$ as usual, $\boldsymbol { D }$ having an arbitrary number of irreducible components, giving $( B , \Sigma )$ , as well as a monoid $P$ , a multivalued $P$ -convex function $\varphi$ on $B$ , $J \subset P$ an ideal with $\sqrt { J } = J$ , and a scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D }$ for this data.
Broken lines were introduced in [G09] and their theory was further developed in [CPS].

Definition 2.15. Let $B$ be an integral affine manifold.
An integral affine map $\gamma :$ $( t _ { 1 } , t _ { 2 } ) ~  ~ B$ from an open interval $( t _ { 1 } , t _ { 2 } )$ is a continuous map such that for any integral affine coordinate chart $\psi : U \to \mathbb { R } ^ { n }$ of $B$ , $\psi \circ \gamma : \gamma ^ { - 1 } ( U ) \to \mathbb { R } ^ { n }$ is integral affine, i.e., is given by $t \mapsto t v + b$ for some $v \in \mathbb { Z } ^ { n }$ and $b \in \mathbb { R } ^ { n }$ .

Note that for an integral affine map, $\gamma ^ { \prime } ( t ) \in \Lambda _ { B , \gamma ( t ) }$

Definition 2.16. A broken line $\gamma$ in $( B , \Sigma )$ for $q \in B _ { 0 } ( \mathbb { Z } )$ with endpoint $Q \in B _ { 0 }$ is a proper continuous piecewise integral affine map $\gamma : ( - \infty , 0 ] \to B _ { 0 }$ with only a finite number of domains of linearity, together with, for each $L \subset ( - \infty , 0 ]$ a maximal connected domain of linearity of $\gamma$ , a choice of monomial $m _ { L } = c _ { L } z ^ { q _ { L } }$ where $c _ { L } \in \mathbb { k } ^ { \times }$ and $q _ { L } \in \Gamma ( L , \gamma ^ { - 1 } ( \mathcal { P } ) | _ { L } )$ , satisfying the following properties.

(1) For the unique unbounded domain of linearity $L$ , $\gamma | _ { L }$ goes off to infinity in a cone $\sigma \in \Sigma _ { \mathrm { m a x } }$ as $t \to - \infty$ , and $q \in \sigma$ .
Furthermore, using the identification of the stalk $\mathcal { P } _ { x }$ for $x \in \sigma$ with $\mathcal { P } _ { \sigma }$ , $m _ { L } = z ^ { \varphi _ { \sigma } ( q ) }$ .  
(2) For each $L$ and $t \in L$ , $- r ( q _ { L } ) \ = \ \gamma ^ { \prime } ( t )$ , where $r$ is defined in (2.1).
Also $\gamma ( 0 ) = Q \in B _ { 0 }$ .  
(3) Let $t \in ( - \infty , 0 )$ be a point at which $\gamma$ is not linear, passing from domain of linearity $L$ to $L ^ { \prime }$ .
If $\gamma ( t ) \in \tau \in \Sigma$ , then $\mathcal { P } _ { \gamma ( t ) } = \mathcal { P } _ { \tau }$ , so that we can view $q _ { L } \in \mathcal { P } _ { \tau }$ and $r ( q _ { L } ) \in \Lambda _ { \tau }$ .
Let $\mathfrak { d } _ { 1 } , \dots , \mathfrak { d } _ { p } \in \mathfrak { D }$ be the rays of $\mathfrak { D }$ that contain $\gamma ( t )$ , with attached functions $f _ { \mathfrak { d } _ { j } }$ .
Then we require that $\gamma$ passes from one side of these rays to the other at time $t$ , so that $\theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { d } _ { j } }$ is defined.
Let $\boldsymbol { n } = \boldsymbol { n } _ { \mathfrak { d } _ { j } }$ be the primitive element of ${ \boldsymbol { \Lambda } } _ { \tau } ^ { * }$ used to define $\theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { d } _ { j } }$ .
Expand

$$
\prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { p } f _ { \varnothing _ { j } } ^ { \langle n , r ( q _ { L } ) \rangle }
$$

as a formal power series in $\mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } } ]$ .
Then there is a term $c z ^ { s }$ in this sum with

$$
m _ { L ^ { \prime } } = m _ { L } \cdot ( c z ^ { s } ) .
$$

Remark 2.17. Using the notation of item (3) above, by item (2) of the definition,

$$
\langle n , r ( q _ { L } ) \rangle > 0 .
$$

This is vital to interpret (2.9).
Indeed, if $\tau$ is a ray, $f _ { \mathfrak { d } _ { i } }$ need not be invertible in $\mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } } ]$ , so (2.10) tells us that (2.9) makes sense in this ring.

Example 2.18. We give a first example of broken lines, in the case where $B$ is as given in Example 1.10 and ${ \mathfrak { D } } = \emptyset$ , so that there is no possibility of bending.
Nevertheless, there is quite non-trivial behaviour.
For an example including bending, see Example 3.7 after the introduction of the canonical scattering diagram.

Given $q \in B _ { 0 } ( \mathbb { Z } )$ , $Q \in B _ { 0 }$ general, we can choose lifts $\tilde { q } , \tilde { Q }$ to the universal cover $\tilde { B } _ { 0 } = | \tilde { \Sigma } | \setminus \{ 0 \}$ of $B _ { 0 }$ .
Let $\pi : \tilde { B } _ { 0 } \to B _ { 0 }$ be the covering map.
Fixing the lift $\tilde { Q }$ , for any lift $\tilde { q }$ we obtain a broken line $\gamma : ( - \infty , 0 ]  B _ { 0 }$ given by $\gamma ( t ) = \pi ( \tilde { Q } - t \tilde { q } )$ .
As this has one domain of linearity $L$ , we decorate $L$ with the monomial $z ^ { \varphi _ { \sigma } ( q ) }$ , where $q \in \sigma \in \Sigma$ .
Note there are an infinite number of such broken lines, one for each lift of $q$ .
Dealing with this non-finiteness is a key part of the proof of Looijenga’s conjecture in §7.

The next lemma and corollary are crucial for interpreting the monomials $m _ { L }$ :

Lemma 2.19. Let $\sigma _ { - } , \sigma _ { + } \in \Sigma _ { \mathrm { m a x } }$ be the two maximal cones containing the ray $\rho \in \Sigma$ .
If $q \in P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma _ { - } } }$ with $- r ( q ) \in \mathrm { I n t } ( \rho ^ { - 1 } \sigma _ { + } ) \subset \Lambda _ { \rho } \otimes _ { \mathbb { Z } } \mathbb { R }$ , then

$$
q \in P _ { \varphi _ { \rho } } = P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma _ { - } } } \cap P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma _ { + } } } .
$$

Proof.
By the definitions there exist $p , \kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in P$ and $n _ { \rho } \in \Lambda _ { \rho } ^ { * }$ annihilating the tangent space to $\rho$ and positive on $\sigma _ { + }$ such that

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { q = \varphi _ { \sigma _ { - } } ( r ( q ) ) + p } } \\ { { \varphi _ { \sigma _ { + } } ( - r ( q ) ) = \varphi _ { \sigma _ { - } } ( - r ( q ) ) + \langle n _ { \rho } , - r ( q ) \rangle \kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } . } } \end{array}
$$

Since $\langle n _ { \rho } , - r ( q ) \rangle > 0$ ,

$$
q = \varphi _ { \sigma _ { + } } ( r ( q ) ) + p + \langle n _ { \rho } , - r ( q ) \rangle \kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in P _ { \varphi _ { + } } .
$$

An immediate consequence of this lemma is

Corollary 2.20. (1) Let $\gamma : [ t _ { 1 } , t _ { 2 } ]  B _ { 0 }$ be integral affine.
Suppose that $\gamma ( t _ { 1 } ) \in$ $\tau _ { 1 }$ , $\gamma ( t _ { 2 } ) \in \ \tau _ { 2 }$ .
Suppose also we are given a section $q \in \Gamma ( \gamma ^ { - 1 } \mathcal { P } )$ such that $- r ( q ) = \gamma ^ { \prime } ( t )$ for each $t$ .
If

$$
q ( t _ { 1 } ) \in P _ { \varphi _ { \tau _ { 1 } } } \subset \mathcal P _ { \tau _ { 1 } } = \mathcal P _ { \gamma ( t _ { 1 } ) } ,
$$

then

$$
q ( t _ { 2 } ) \in P _ { \varphi _ { \tau _ { 2 } } } \subset \mathscr { P } _ { \tau _ { 2 } } = \mathscr { P } _ { \gamma ( t _ { 2 } ) } .
$$

(2) If $\gamma$ is a broken line, $t \in L$ a maximal domain of linearity with $\gamma ( t ) \in \tau$ , then

$$
q _ { L } \in P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } } \subset \mathcal { P } _ { \tau } = \mathcal { P } _ { \gamma ( t ) } .
$$

Proof.
The first item follows immediately from the lemma.
The second item follows from the fact that if $t \ll 0$ lies in the unbounded domain of linearity with $\gamma ( t ) \in \sigma$ , then $m _ { L } = z ^ { \varphi _ { \tau } ( q ) } \in P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma } }$ by construction.
Then this holds for all $t$ by item (1) and Definition 2.16, (3).


The convexity of $\varphi$ puts further restrictions on the monomial decorations of a broken line.

Definition 2.21. Let $J \subset P$ be a proper monoid ideal.
For $p \in J$ there exists a maximal $k \geq 1$ such that $p = p _ { 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + p _ { k }$ with $p _ { i } \in J$ .
We define ord $\boldsymbol { \mathbf { \mathit { \Pi } } } _ { J } ( \boldsymbol { \mathbf { \mathit { p } } } )$ to be this maximum, and define $\mathrm { o r d } _ { J } ( p ) = 0$ if $p \in P \setminus J$ .

For $x \in \tau$ , $q \in P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } }$ , define ${ \mathrm { o r d } } _ { J , x } ( q ) : = { \mathrm { o r d } } _ { J } ( q - \varphi _ { \tau } ( r ( q ) ) )$ .
This measures how high $q$ is above the graph of $\varphi _ { \tau }$ .
If $\gamma$ is a broken line and $t \in L$ a maximal domain of linearity, define

$$
\mathrm { o r d } _ { J , \gamma } ( t ) = \mathrm { o r d } _ { J , \gamma ( t ) } ( q _ { L } ) ,
$$

using $\gamma ( t ) \in \tau$ and $q _ { L } \in P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } } \subset \mathcal P _ { \gamma ( t ) }$ .

Lemma 2.22. Let $\gamma$ be a broken line.
Then if $t < t ^ { \prime }$ ,

$$
\mathrm { o r d } _ { J , \gamma } ( t ) \leq \mathrm { o r d } _ { J , \gamma } ( t ^ { \prime } ) ,
$$

with strict inequality if either $t$ and $t ^ { \prime }$ lie in different domains of linearity or for some $t ^ { \prime \prime }$ with $t < t ^ { \prime \prime } < t ^ { \prime }$ , $\gamma ( t ^ { \prime \prime } )$ lies in a ray $\rho \in \Sigma$ with bending parameter $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in J$ .

Proof.
This is immediate from the definitions and the proof of Lemma 2.19.

Definition 2.23. For $I$ an ideal in $P$ with $\sqrt { I } = J$ , let

$$
\operatorname { S u p p } _ { I } ( { \mathfrak { D } } ) : = \bigcup _ { \mathfrak { d } } \mathfrak { d }
$$

where the union is over all $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \in \mathfrak { D }$ such that $f _ { \mathfrak { d } } \not \equiv 1$ mod $I \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \tau _ { 0 } } } ]$ .
By Definition 2.13, (4), this is a finite union.

Definition 2.24. Let $I$ be an ideal of $P$ with $\sqrt { I } = J$ , and let $Q \in B \setminus \operatorname { S u p p } _ { I } ( \mathfrak { D } )$ , $Q \in \tau \in \Sigma$ .
For $q \in B _ { 0 } ( \mathbb { Z } )$ , define

$$
{ \mathrm { L i f t } } _ { Q } ( q ) : = \sum _ { \gamma } { \mathrm { M o n o } ( \gamma ) } \in \Bbbk [ P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } } ] / I \cdot \Bbbk [ P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } } ] ,
$$

where the sum is over all broken lines $\gamma$ for $q$ with endpoint $Q$ , and $\operatorname { M o n o } ( \gamma )$ denotes the monomial attached to the last domain of linearity of $\gamma$ .
The word “Lift” is used to indicate that this is, as we shall show, a lifting of a monomial $z ^ { q }$ on $X _ { J , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ to $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ .
The fact that Lift $_ Q ( q )$ lies in the stated ring follows from:

Lemma 2.25. Let $Q \in \sigma \in \Sigma _ { \operatorname* { m a x } }$ , $q \in B _ { 0 } ( \mathbb { Z } )$ .
Let $I$ be an ideal with $\sqrt { I } = J$ .
Assume that $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in J$ for at least one ray $\rho \in \Sigma$ .
Then the following hold:

(1) The collection of $\gamma$ in Definition 2.24 with

$$
\mathrm { M o n o } ( \gamma ) \not \in I \cdot \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma } } ]
$$

is finite.

(2) If one boundary ray of the connected component of $B \backslash \mathrm { S u p p } _ { I } ( \mathfrak { D } )$ containing $Q$ is a ray $\rho \in \Sigma$ , then $\mathrm { M o n o } ( \gamma ) \in \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \rho } } ]$ , and the collection of $\gamma$ with

$$
\mathrm { M o n o } ( \gamma ) \notin I \cdot \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi \rho } ]
$$

is finite.

Proof.
Note there is some $k$ such that $J ^ { k } \subset I$ because $\mathbb { k } [ P ]$ is Noetherian.
If $\gamma$ is a broken line with $\mathrm { M o n o } ( \gamma ) \not \in I \cdot \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma } } ]$ , then $\gamma$ crosses the rays of $\Sigma$ in a cyclic order.
Indeed, this follows from condition (3) of Definition 2.16, as a broken line must cross from one side to the other of each ray of $\mathfrak { D }$ it intersects.
From this, the hypotheses on the $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi }$ imply that in any set of at least $n$ consecutive rays of $\Sigma$ that it crosses, there is at least one ray $\rho$ with $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in J$ .
By Lemma 2.22, $\mathrm { o r d } _ { J , \gamma }$ increases every time $\gamma$ crosses such a ray, and also every time $\gamma$ bends at a ray $\mathfrak { o }$ not contained in a ray of $\Sigma$ .
Once $\mathrm { o r d } _ { J , \gamma } \geq k$ , $\mathrm { M o n o } ( \gamma ) \in I \cdot \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma } } ]$ .
Hence there is an absolute bound on the number of rays of $\Sigma$ that $\gamma$ can cross, and the number of times $\gamma$ can bend.
When $\gamma$ crosses a ray, there are a finite number of terms in (2.9) modulo $I \cdot \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma } } ]$ , as the exponent is always positive, see Remark 2.17. Thus there are a finite number of possible choices of bend, and hence only a finite number of possible choices for the exponent of Mono( $\mathbf { \chi } ^ { \prime } \mathbf { \chi } \big >$ ) modulo $I \cdot \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma } } ]$ once the initial monomial of $\gamma$ is fixed.
Given any prescribed sequence of bends and initial direction, one sees that there is only one possibility for the underlying map $\gamma$ with endpoint a fixed point $Q$ by tracing the broken line back from $Q$ .
Each such underlying map $\gamma$ supports only a finite number of broken lines modulo $I \cdot \mathbb { k } | P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma } } ]$ .
This yields the finiteness of (1).

The argument for the finiteness statement in (2), once the first part of (2) is established, is the same.
For the first part of (2), consider a broken line $\gamma$ contributing to Lift $_ Q ( q )$ .
We take $Q \in \sigma _ { + }$ , in the notation of Lemma 2.19. Write $\mathrm { M o n o } ( \gamma ) = c _ { L } z ^ { q _ { L } }$ .
If $r ( q _ { L } ) \in \rho ^ { - 1 } \sigma _ { + }$ then the statement follows from Lemma 2.19. Otherwise (by the definition of broken line) $\gamma$ crosses $\rho$ , which is the last ray of $\Sigma$ and the last ray of $\operatorname { S u p p } _ { I } ( { \mathfrak { D } } )$ it crosses before reaching $Q$ .
Now the result follows from Lemma 2.19 and the definition of broken line.


Definition 2.26. Assume that $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in J$ for at least one ray $\rho \in \Sigma$ .
We say a scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D }$ is consistent if for all ideals $I \subset P$ with $\sqrt { I } = J$ and for all $q \in B _ { 0 } ( \mathbb { Z } )$ , the following holds.
Let $Q \in B _ { 0 }$ be chosen so that the line joining the origin and $Q$ has irrational slope, and $Q ^ { \prime } \in B _ { 0 }$ similarly.
Then:

(1) If $Q , Q ^ { \prime } \in \sigma \in \Sigma _ { \operatorname* { m a x } }$ , then we can view Lift $_ Q ( q )$ and Lift $Q ^ { \prime } \displaystyle \left( q \right)$ as elements of $R _ { \sigma , I }$ , and as such, we have

$$
\mathrm { L i f t } _ { Q ^ { \prime } } ( q ) = \theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { D } } ( \mathrm { L i f t } _ { Q } ( q ) )
$$

for $\gamma$ a path contained in the interior of $\sigma$ connecting $Q$ to $Q ^ { \prime }$ .

(2) If $Q _ { - } ~ \in ~ \sigma _ { - }$ and $Q _ { + } ~ \in ~ \sigma _ { + }$ with $\sigma _ { \pm } ~ \in ~ \Sigma _ { \operatorname* { m a x } }$ and $\rho ~ = ~ \sigma _ { + } \mathrm { ~ } ^ { \prime } { } ^ { \mathrm { ~ ~ } } ^ { \sigma _ { - } }$ a ray, and furthermore $Q _ { - }$ and $Q _ { + }$ are contained in connected components of $B \backslash \mathrm { S u p p } _ { I } ( \mathfrak { D } )$ whose closures contain $\rho$ , then Lift $_ { Q \pm } ( q ) \in R _ { \sigma \pm , I }$ are both images under $\psi _ { \rho , \pm }$ o f a single element

$$
\operatorname { L i f t } _ { \rho } ( q ) \in R _ { \rho , I } .
$$

Of course the definition is introduced so that the following construction works:

Construction 2.27 (Construction of $\vartheta _ { q }$ ).
Suppose $\textit { D } \subset \textit { Y }$ has $n \geq 3$ irreducible components, and that $\mathfrak { D }$ is a consistent scattering diagram for data $( B , \Sigma )$ , $P$ , $\varphi$ and $J$ .
Assume further that $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in J$ for all $\rho \in \Sigma$ , so that we may apply Construction 2.14. We now construct for any $I$ with $\sqrt { I } = J$ a function $\vartheta _ { q } \in \Gamma ( X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o } , \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o } } )$ for $q \in B ( \mathbb { Z } ) = B _ { 0 } ( \mathbb { Z } ) \cup \{ 0 \}$ .

We define $\vartheta _ { 0 } = 1$ .
Next, let $q \in B _ { 0 } ( \mathbb { Z } )$ .
For each ray $\rho \in \Sigma$ contained in $\sigma _ { \pm } \in$ $\Sigma _ { \mathrm { m a x } }$ , choose two points $Q _ { \rho } ^ { \pm } \in B$ , one each in the two connected components of $B \ \backslash$ $( \operatorname { S u p p } _ { I } ( { \mathfrak { D } } ) \cup \rho )$ which are adjacent to $\rho$ , with $Q _ { \rho } ^ { + } \in \sigma _ { + }$ and $Q _ { \rho } ^ { - } \in \sigma _ { - }$ .

We first note that $\mathrm { L i f t } _ { Q _ { \rho } ^ { \pm } } ( q )$ is a well-defined element of $R _ { \sigma _ { \pm } , I }$ , independent of the particular choice of $Q _ { \rho } ^ { \pm }$ : given a choice say of $Q \ = \ Q _ { \rho } ^ { + }$ and another choice $Q ^ { \prime }$ , we take a path $\gamma$ connecting $Q$ and $Q ^ { \prime }$ wholly contained in the connected component of $B \setminus ( \operatorname { S u p p } _ { I } ( \mathfrak { D } ) \cup \rho )$ containing $Q$ and $Q ^ { \prime }$ .
By Definition 2.26, (1), it then follows that $\mathrm { L i f t } _ { Q } ( q ) = \mathrm { L i f t } _ { Q ^ { \prime } } ( q )$ .

By Definition 2.26, (2), we have an element $\mathrm { L i f t } _ { \rho } ( q ) \in R _ { \rho , I }$ whose image under $\psi _ { \rho , \pm }$ is $\mathrm { L i f t } _ { Q _ { \rho } ^ { \pm } } ( q )$ .
It then follows via another application of Definition 2.26, (1), applied to the path of Figure 2.1, that if $\rho$ , $\rho ^ { \prime }$ are adjacent rays in $\Sigma$ , then $\operatorname { L i f t } _ { \rho } ( q )$ and $\operatorname { L i f t } _ { \rho ^ { \prime } } ( q )$ glue under the identification of open subsets of $U _ { \rho , I }$ and $U _ { \rho ^ { \prime } , I }$ given by $\theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { D } }$ .
Thus all these elements of the rings $R _ { \rho , I }$ for $\rho \in \Sigma$ glue to give a regular function on $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ , by construction of this latter space.
This regular function is what we call $\vartheta _ { q }$ .

Theorem 2.28. Suppose $\boldsymbol { D }$ has $n \geq 3$ irreducible components, and let $\varphi$ be a multivalued piecewise linear function on $B$ such that $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in J$ for all rays $\rho \in \Sigma$ .
Let $\mathfrak { D }$ be a consistent scattering diagram and $I \subset P$ an ideal with $\sqrt { I } = J$ .
Set

$$
X _ { I } : = \mathrm { S p e c } \Gamma ( X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o } , \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o } } ) .
$$

Since $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ has the structure of a scheme over Spec $R _ { I }$ , so does $X _ { I }$ , which we write as

$$
f _ { I } : X _ { I } \to \operatorname { S p e c } R _ { I } .
$$

Then

(1) $X _ { I }$ contains $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ as an open subset and $f _ { I }$ is flat with fibre over a closed point $x$ of Spec $R _ { I }$ isomorphic to the $n$ -vertex $\mathbb { V } _ { n }$ .

(2) For each $q \in B ( \mathbb { Z } )$ , there is a section $\vartheta _ { q } \in \Gamma ( X _ { I } , { \mathcal { O } } _ { X _ { I } } )$ , and the set

$$
\{ \vartheta _ { q } | q \in B ( \mathbb { Z } ) \}
$$

is a free $R _ { I }$ -module basis for $\Gamma ( X _ { I } , { \mathcal { O } } _ { X _ { I } } )$ .

Proof.
Construction 2.27 constructs regular functions $\vartheta _ { q }$ on $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ , hence by definition of $X _ { I }$ , we obtain $\vartheta _ { q } \in \Gamma ( X _ { I } , { \mathcal { O } } _ { X _ { I } } )$ .

Now note that $X _ { J , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o } ~ = ~ X _ { J } ^ { o }$ as defined in §2.1.
Indeed, for any $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \ \in \ \mathfrak { D }$ with $\dim \tau _ { 0 } = 2$ we have $f _ { 0 } \equiv 1$ mod $J$ , so the open sets $U _ { \rho _ { i } , J }$ , $U _ { \rho _ { i + 1 } , J }$ are glued trivially.
Similarly, if $\dim \tau _ { \mathfrak { d } } = 1$ then since $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in J$ , the rings $R _ { \rho , I }$ as given in (2.7) and (2.5) coincide and are glued trivially.
Thus with $I \ = \ J$ , we see the gluing constructions Constructions 2.7 and 2.14 coincide.

Note that with the assumption that $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in J$ for all rays $\rho$ ,

$$
X _ { J } ^ { o } \cong ( \mathrm { S p e c } R _ { J } [ \Sigma ] ) \setminus ( \mathrm { S p e c } R _ { J } ) \times \{ 0 \}
$$

by Lemma 2.12. We see that the canonical map

$$
\bigoplus _ { q \in B ( \mathbb { Z } ) } R _ { J } \cdot \vartheta _ { q } \to \Gamma ( X _ { J } ^ { o } , { \mathcal { O } } _ { X _ { J } ^ { o } } )
$$

is an isomorphism.
Indeed, by Lemma 2.10, $\Gamma ( X _ { J } ^ { o } , { \mathcal { O } } _ { X _ { J } ^ { o } } ) \cong R _ { J } [ \Sigma ]$ .
Furthermore, under this isomorphism, $\vartheta _ { q }$ is clearly taken to $z ^ { q } \in R _ { J } | \Sigma |$ .
This is because the only broken lines contributing to Lift $_ Q ( q )$ modulo $J$ for any $Q$ is the straight line with endpoint $Q$ , and this provides a contribution only if $Q$ lies in the same maximal cone as $q$ .

It also follows that $X _ { J } : = \mathrm { S p e c } \Gamma ( X _ { J } ^ { o } , { \mathcal O } _ { X _ { J } ^ { o } } ) = \mathrm { S p e c } R _ { J } \vert   \Sigma \vert$ is flat over ${ \mathrm { S p e c } } R _ { J }$ and the fiber over a closed point $x$ is given by $\mathrm { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ \Sigma ]$ .

Now let $I$ be an ideal with $\sqrt { I } = J$ .
Let $i \colon X _ { J } ^ { o } \subset X _ { J }$ be the inclusion.
Define a ringed space $X _ { I } ^ { \prime }$ with underlying topological space $X _ { J }$ by $\mathcal { O } _ { X _ { I } ^ { \prime } } : = i _ { * } \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o } }$ .
Then the natural map $\mathcal { O } _ { X _ { I } ^ { \prime } } \to \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { J } }$ is surjective by the existence of the lifts $\vartheta _ { q }$ .
Thus $X _ { I } ^ { \prime } / \mathrm { S p e c } R _ { I }$ is a flat deformation of $X _ { J } / \operatorname { S p e c } R _ { J }$ by Lemma 2.29 below.
Now since $X _ { J }$ is affine it follows that $X _ { I } ^ { \prime }$ is also affine, so $X _ { I } ^ { \prime } = X _ { I } : = \sec \Gamma ( X _ { I , \mathfrak D } ^ { o } , { \mathcal O } _ { X _ { I , \mathfrak D } ^ { o } } )$ .

We showed above that the $\vartheta _ { q }$ form an $R _ { J }$ -module basis of $\Gamma ( X _ { J } , { \mathcal { O } } _ { X _ { J } } )$ .
Now since $X _ { I } / \mathrm { S p e c } R _ { I }$ is a flat infinitesimal deformation of $X _ { J } / \operatorname { S p e c } R _ { J }$ it follows that the $\vartheta _ { q }$ form a $R _ { I }$ -module basis of $\Gamma ( X _ { I } , { \mathcal { O } } _ { X _ { I } } )$ , see Lemma 2.30 below.
$\boxed { \begin{array} { r l } \end{array} }$

Lemma 2.29. Let $X _ { 0 } / S _ { 0 }$ be a flat family of surfaces such that the fibres satisfy Serre’s condition $S _ { 2 }$ .
Let $i \colon X _ { 0 } ^ { o } \subset X _ { 0 }$ be the inclusion of an open subset such that the complement has finite fibres.
Note that $i _ { * } \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { 0 } ^ { o } } = \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { 0 } }$ by Lemma 2.10.

Let $S _ { 0 } \subset S$ be an infinitesimal thickening of $S _ { 0 }$ and let $X ^ { o }  S$ be a flat deformation of $X _ { 0 } ^ { o } / S _ { 0 }$ over $S$ .
Define a family of ringed spaces $X  S$ by $\mathcal { O } _ { X } : = i _ { * } \mathcal { O } _ { X ^ { o } }$ .

Then $X / S$ is a flat deformation of $X _ { 0 } / S _ { 0 }$ (that is, $X / S$ is flat and $X _ { 0 } = X \times _ { S } S _ { 0 _ { . } }$ ) if and only if the map

$$
\mathcal { O } _ { X } : = i _ { * } \mathcal { O } _ { X ^ { o } }  i _ { * } \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { 0 } ^ { o } } = \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { 0 } }
$$

is surjective.

Proof.
The condition is clearly necessary.

Conversely, suppose (2.12) is surjective.
Let $\mathcal { T } \subset \mathcal { O } _ { S }$ be the nilpotent ideal defining $S _ { 0 } \subset S$ .
Let $X _ { n } ^ { o } / S _ { n }$ denote the $n$ th order infinitesimal thickening of $X _ { 0 } / S _ { 0 }$ determined by $X ^ { o } / S$ , that is, $\mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n } ^ { o } } = \mathcal { O } _ { X ^ { o } } / \mathbb { Z } ^ { n + 1 } \cdot \mathcal { O } _ { X ^ { o } }$ and ${ \mathcal O } _ { S _ { n } } = { \mathcal O } _ { S } / { \mathbb Z } ^ { n + 1 }$ .
Define $X _ { n } / S _ { n }$ by $\mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n } } : = i _ { * } \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n } ^ { o } }$ .
Note that $\mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n } } \to \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { 0 } }$ is surjective because $\mathcal { O } _ { X } \to \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { 0 } }$ is surjective by assumption.
We show by induction on $n$ that $X _ { n } / S _ { n }$ is a flat deformation of $X _ { 0 } / S _ { 0 }$ .
For $n = 0$ there is nothing to prove.
Suppose the induction hypothesis is true for $n$ .
Since $X _ { n + 1 } ^ { o } / S _ { n + 1 }$ is flat (being the restriction of the flat family $X ^ { o } / S$ to $S _ { n + 1 }$ ) we have a short exact sequence

$$
0  \mathbb { Z } ^ { n + 1 } / \mathbb { Z } ^ { n + 2 } \otimes \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { 0 } ^ { o } }  \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n + 1 } ^ { o } }  \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n } ^ { o } }  0 .
$$

Applying $i _ { * }$ we obtain an exact sequence

$$
0 \to i _ { * } ( { \mathbb { Z } ^ { n + 1 } } / { \mathbb { Z } ^ { n + 2 } } \otimes { \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { 0 } ^ { o } } } ) \to { \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n + 1 } } } \to { \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n } } } .
$$

By Lemma 2.10 the first term is equal to $\mathcal { T } ^ { n + 1 } / \mathcal { T } ^ { n + 2 } \otimes \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { 0 } }$ .
Moreover, the last arrow is surjective because $\mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n + 1 } } \to \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { 0 } }$ is surjective, $\mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n } } / \mathcal { T } \cdot \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n } } = \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { 0 } }$ by the induction hypothesis, and $\mathcal { L }$ is nilpotent, as in Theorem 8.4 of [Ma89] (where the module $M$ need not be finitely generated for the argument given there to work).
So we have an exact sequence

$$
0  \mathbb { Z } ^ { n + 1 } / \mathbb { Z } ^ { n + 2 } \otimes \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { 0 } }  \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n + 1 } }  \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n } }  0 .
$$

It follows that $\mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n + 1 } } / \mathcal { T } ^ { n + 1 } \cdot \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n + 1 } } = \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n } }$ .
(Indeed, consider the map

$$
\alpha \colon \mathcal { T } ^ { n + 1 } \otimes \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n + 1 } } \to \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n + 1 } } , \quad \alpha ( f \otimes g ) = f g .
$$

We claim that $\alpha$ is equal to the composition of the map

$$
\beta \colon  { \mathcal { T } } ^ { n + 1 } \otimes  { \mathcal { O } } _ { X _ { n + 1 } } \to  { \mathcal { T } } ^ { n + 1 } /  { \mathcal { T } } ^ { n + 2 } \otimes  { \mathcal { O } } _ { X _ { 0 } }
$$

given by the natural maps on the factors and the first map $\gamma$ of the exact sequence (2.13).
Since $\mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n + 1 } } = i _ { * } \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n + 1 } ^ { o } }$ by definition, it suffices to check the equality after restriction to $X ^ { o }$ , where it is obvious.
The map $\beta$ is surjective because $\mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n + 1 } } \to \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { 0 } }$ is surjective.
So the image of $\gamma$ is equal to the image of $\alpha$ , namely ${ \cal { T } } ^ { n + 1 } \cdot \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { n + 1 } }$ .) Now by [Ma89], Theorem 22.3, p.
174, the exact sequence (2.13) shows that $X _ { n + 1 } / S _ { n + 1 }$ is a flat deformation of $X _ { 0 } / S _ { 0 }$ .


Lemma 2.30. Let $A  B$ be a flat homomorphism of Noetherian rings and $I \subset A$ a nilpotent ideal.
Suppose given a set $S$ of elements of $B$ such that the reductions of the elements of $S$ form an $A / I$ -module basis of $B / I B$ .
Then $S$ is an $A$ -module basis of $B$ .

Proof.
Since $I$ is nilpotent and $S$ generates $B / I B$ it is clear that $S$ spans $B$ by Theorem 8.4 of [Ma89].
So we have an exact sequence

$$
0 \to K \to A ^ { S } \to B \to 0 .
$$

Tensoring with $A / I$ we obtain an exact sequence

$$
0 \to K / I K \to ( A / I ) ^ { S } \to B / I B \to 0
$$

using flatness of $B$ over $A$ .
We deduce that $K / I K = 0$ by our assumption, hence $K = 0$ because $I$ is nilpotent.


Proposition 2.31. Let $X _ { I } / S _ { I } : = \operatorname { S p e c } R _ { I }$ be the family of Theorem 2.28. Then the relative dualizing sheaf $\omega _ { X _ { I } / S _ { I } }$ is trivial.
It is generated by the global section $\Omega$ given on local patches $U _ { \rho _ { i } , I }$ by $\mathrm { d } \mathrm { l o g } X _ { i - 1 } { \wedge } \mathrm { d } \mathrm { l o g } X _ { i } = \mathrm { d } \mathrm { l o g } X _ { i } { \wedge } \mathrm { d } \mathrm { l o g } X _ { i + 1 }$ .
Here we take the rays $\rho _ { j }$ in counter-clockwise order, after choosing an orientation on $B$ , to obtain a consistent choice of signs.

Proof.
By the adjunction formula for the closed embedding

$$
U _ { \rho _ { i } } \subset \mathbb { A } _ { X _ { i - 1 } , X _ { i + 1 } } ^ { 2 } \times \mathbb { G } _ { m , X _ { i } } \times S _ { I } ,
$$

the dualizing sheaf $\omega _ { X _ { I } / S _ { I } }$ is freely generated over $U _ { \rho _ { i } }$ by the local section in the statement.
These sections patch to give a generator $\Omega$ of $\omega _ { X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o } } / S _ { I }$ because the scattering automorphisms preserve the torus invariant two-forms.
Both $\omega _ { X _ { I } / S _ { I } }$ and ${ \mathcal { O } } _ { X _ { I } }$ satisfy the relative $S _ { 2 }$ property $i _ { * } i ^ { * } { \mathcal { F } } = { \mathcal { F } }$ where $i \colon X _ { I } ^ { o } \subset X _ { I }$ is the inclusion ([H04], Appendix, where the hypothesis of slc is not needed), hence $\omega _ { X _ { I } / S _ { I } }$ is freely generated by $\Omega$ .


2.4. The algebra structure.
In the previous section, we saw that the $R _ { I }$ -algebra

$$
A _ { I } = \Gamma ( X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o } , \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o } } )
$$

defining the flat deformation $X _ { I }$ has an $R _ { I }$ -module basis of theta functions $\{ \vartheta _ { m } | m \in$ $B ( \mathbb { Z } ) \}$ .
Here we derive a description of the multiplication rule on $R _ { I }$ using the geometry of the integral affine manifold $B$ .
Besides being an attractive combinatorial description of the multiplication rule, we will use this (in the case of the canonical scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ ) in §6 to prove that our deformation extends over completions of larger strata of $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P ]$ , as well as for the case that $\boldsymbol { D }$ has $1$ or 2 irreducible components.

Definition 2.32. For a broken line $\gamma$ with endpoint $Q \in \tau \in \Sigma$ , define $s ( \gamma ) \in \Lambda _ { \tau }$ , $c ( \gamma ) \in \mathbb { k } [ P ]$ by demanding that

$$
\mathrm { M o n o } ( \gamma ) = c ( \gamma ) \cdot z ^ { \varphi _ { \tau } ( s ( \gamma ) ) } .
$$

Write Limits $( \gamma ) = ( q , Q )$ if $\gamma$ is a broken line for $q$ and has endpoint $Q \in B$ .

Remark 2.33. Recall that $B _ { 0 }$ in fact has the structure of an integral linear manifold.
One feature of such manifolds is that for any simply connected set $U \subset B _ { 0 }$ , there is is a canonical linear immersion $U \to \Lambda _ { \mathbb { R } , U }$ , compatible with parallel transport inside $U$ .

In particular, if $q$ is a point of $B _ { 0 }$ with $q \in \sigma \in \Sigma$ , and $\tau \subset \sigma$ , then the canonical embedding of a neighbourhood of $\tau \setminus \{ 0 \}$ in $\Lambda _ { \tau , \mathbb { R } }$ identifies $q$ with a point of $\Lambda _ { \tau , \mathbb { R } }$ .

Theorem 2.34. Let $q _ { 1 } , q _ { 2 } \in B ( \mathbb { Z } )$ .
In the canonical expansion

$$
\vartheta _ { q _ { 1 } } \cdot \vartheta _ { q _ { 2 } } = \sum _ { q \in B ( \mathbb { Z } ) } \alpha _ { q } \vartheta _ { q } ,
$$

where $\alpha _ { q } \in R _ { I }$ for each $q$ , we have

$$
\alpha _ { q } = \sum _ { \stackrel { \left( \gamma _ { 1 } , \gamma _ { 2 } \right) } { \operatorname { L i m i t s } ( \gamma _ { i } ) = ( q _ { i } , z ) } } c ( \gamma _ { 1 } ) c ( \gamma _ { 2 } )
$$

Here $z \in B _ { 0 }$ is a point very close to $q$ contained in a cell $\tau$ , and we identify $q$ with a point of $\Lambda _ { \tau }$ using Remark 2.33.

Proof.
To identify the coefficient of $\vartheta _ { q }$ , choose a point $z \in B$ very close to $q$ , and describe the product using the lifts of $z ^ { q 1 }$ , $z ^ { q _ { 2 } }$ at $z$ :

$$
( \mathrm { L i f t } _ { z } ( q _ { 1 } ) ) ( \mathrm { L i f t } _ { z } ( q _ { 2 } ) ) = \sum _ { q ^ { \prime } } \alpha _ { q ^ { \prime } } \mathrm { L i f t } _ { z } ( q ^ { \prime } ) .
$$

Now observe first that there is only one broken line $\gamma$ with endpoint $z$ and $s ( \gamma ) = q \in$ $\Lambda _ { \tau }$ : this is the broken line whose image is $z + \mathbb { R } _ { \geq 0 } q$ .
Indeed, the final segment of such a $\gamma$ is on this ray, and this ray meets no scattering rays, so the broken line cannot bend.
Thus the coefficient of Lift $_ z ( q )$ on the right-hand side of the above equation can be read off by looking at the coefficient (in $R _ { I }$ ) of ${ z } ^ { \varphi _ { \tau } ( q ) }$ .
This gives the desired description.


# 3. The canonical scattering diagram

3.1. Definition.
Here we give the precise definition of $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ .
As explained in the introduction, it is, roughly speaking, defined in terms of maps $\mathbb { A } ^ { 1 } \to Y \setminus D$ , which are algebro-geometric analogues of the holomorphic disks used for instanton corrections in the symplectic heuristic.
We begin by recalling necessary facts about relative GromovWitten invariants used to count these curves.

Definition 3.1. Let $( \tilde { Y } , \tilde { D } )$ be a non-singular rational surface with $\tilde { D }$ an anti-canonical cycle of rational curves, and let $C$ be an irreducible component of $\tilde { D }$ .
Consider a class $\beta \in A _ { 1 } ( \tilde { Y } , \mathbb { Z } )$ such that

$$
\beta \cdot \tilde { D } _ { i } = \left\{ k _ { \beta } \begin{array} { l l } { { \tilde { D } _ { i } = C } } \\ { { 0 } } & { { \tilde { D } _ { i } \neq C } } \end{array} \right.
$$

for some $k _ { \beta } > 0$ .
Let $F ^ { \prime }$ be the closure of $\bar { D } \backslash C$ , and let

$$
{ \tilde { Y } } ^ { o } : = { \tilde { Y } } \setminus F , \quad { C } ^ { o } : = C \setminus F .
$$

Let $\overline { { \mathfrak { M } } } ( \tilde { Y } ^ { o } / C ^ { o } , \beta )$ be the moduli space of stable relative maps of genus zero curves representing the class $\beta$ with tangency of order $k _ { \beta }$ at an unspecified point of ${ \cal C } ^ { \dag o }$ .
(See [Li00], [Li02] for the algebraic definition for these relative Gromov-Witten invariants, and [LR01], [IP03] for the original symplectic definitions.) We refer to $\beta$ informally as an $\mathbb { A } ^ { 1 }$ -class.
The virtual dimension of this moduli space is

$$
- K _ { \tilde { Y } } \cdot \beta + ( \dim \tilde { Y } - 3 ) - ( k _ { \beta } - 1 ) = 0 .
$$

Here the first two terms give the standard dimension formula for the moduli space of stable rational curves in $\tilde { Y }$ representing the class $\beta$ , and the term $k _ { \beta } - 1$ is the change in dimension given by imposing the $k _ { \beta }$ -fold tangency condition.
The moduli space carries a virtual fundamental class.
Furthermore, we have

Lemma 3.2. $\overline { { \mathfrak { M } } } ( \tilde { Y } ^ { o } / C ^ { o } , \beta )$ is proper over k.

Proof.
This follows as in the proof of [GPS09], Theorem 4.2. In brief, let $R$ be a valuation ring with quotient field $K$ , with $S = { \mathrm { S p e c } } R$ , $T = { \mathrm { S p e c } } K$ .
We would like to extend a morphism $T \to \overline { { \mathfrak { M } } } ( \tilde { Y } ^ { o } / C ^ { o } , \beta )$ to $S$ .
We know that the moduli space $\overline { { \mathfrak { M } } } ( \tilde { Y } / C , \beta )$ is proper, so we obtain a family of relative stable maps $c  S$ to $\tilde { Y }$ .
We just need to show that in fact the image of the closed fibre $\scriptstyle { \zeta _ { 0 } }$ lies in ${ \tilde { Y } } ^ { o }$ .
However, the argument in the proof of [GPS09], Theorem 4.2 shows that if the image of $\scriptstyle { \zeta _ { 0 } }$ intersects $F ^ { \prime }$ , then $\scriptstyle { \zeta _ { 0 } }$ must be of genus at least $1$ , which is not the case.


Given this, we define

$$
N _ { \beta } : = \int _ { [ \overline { { \mathfrak { M } } } ( \tilde { Y } ^ { o } / C ^ { o } , \beta ) ] ^ { v i r } } 1 .
$$

Morally, one should view $N _ { \beta }$ as counting maps from affine lines to $\tilde { Y } \backslash \tilde { D }$ whose closures represent the class $\beta$ .

In what follows, we fix as usual the pair $( Y , D )$ , with tropicalisation $( B , \Sigma )$ , and $\varphi$ the function given by Example 2.3 for some choice of $\eta : \mathrm { N E } ( Y ) \to P$ .
We can assume here that $D$ has an arbitrary number of irreducible components.

Definition 3.3. Fix a ray $\mathfrak { d } \subset B$ with endpoint the origin, with rational slope.
If $\mathfrak { o }$ coincides with a ray of $\Sigma$ , set $\Sigma ^ { \prime } : = \Sigma$ ; otherwise, let $\Sigma ^ { \prime }$ be a refinement of $\Sigma$ obtained by adding the ray $\mathfrak { o }$ and a number of other rays chosen so that each cone of $\Sigma ^ { \prime }$ is integral affine isomorphic to the first quadrant of $\mathbb { R } ^ { 2 }$ .
This gives a toric blow-up $\pi : \tilde { Y }  Y$ (the identity in the first case) by Lemma 1.6. Let $C \subset \pi ^ { - 1 } ( D )$ be the irreducible component corresponding to $\mathfrak { o }$ .

Let $\tau _ { 0 } \in \Sigma$ be the smallest cone containing $\mathfrak { o }$ .
Let $m _ { \mathfrak { d } } \in \Lambda _ { \tau _ { \mathfrak { d } } }$ be a primitive generator of the tangent space to $\mathfrak { o }$ , pointing away from the origin.
Define

$$
f _ { 0 } : = \exp \left[ \sum _ { \beta } k _ { \beta } N _ { \beta } z ^ { \eta ( \pi _ { * } ( \beta ) ) - \varphi _ { \tau _ { 0 } } ( k _ { \beta } m _ { 0 } ) } \right] .
$$

Here the sum is over all classes $\beta \in A _ { 1 } ( \tilde { Y } , \mathbb { Z } )$ satisfying (3.1).
Note that if $N _ { \beta } \neq 0$ , then necessarily $\overline { { \mathfrak { M } } } ( \tilde { Y } ^ { o } / C ^ { o } , \beta )$ is non-empty, and thus $\beta \in \operatorname { N E } ( \tilde { Y } )$ , so $\pi _ { * } ( \beta ) \in \mathrm { N E } ( Y )$ .
We note the numbers $N _ { \beta }$ do not depend on the particular choice of refinement $\Sigma ^ { \prime }$ .
Indeed, further refining $\Sigma ^ { \prime }$ does not change the pair $\tilde { Y } ^ { o } / C ^ { o }$ , and hence does not change the numbers $N _ { \beta }$ .

We define

$$
\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } : = \{ ( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) | \mathfrak { d } \subset B \mathrm { ~ a ~ r a y ~ o f ~ r a t i o n a l ~ s l o p e } \} \ : \mathrm { . }
$$

We call a class $\beta \in A _ { 1 } ( \tilde { Y } , \mathbb { Z } )$ an $\mathbb { A } ^ { 1 }$ -class if $N _ { \beta } \neq 0$ .

Note that all rays of the canonical scattering diagram are outgoing.

Remark 3.4. In theory, one should be able to use logarithmic Gromov-Witten invariants ([GS11] or [AC11]) to define $N _ { \beta }$ without the technical trick of blowing up and working on an open variety.
This would be done by working relative to $\boldsymbol { D }$ , and counting rational curves of class $\beta$ with one point mapping to the boundary with specified orders of tangency with each boundary divisor, with non-zero order of tangency with either one divisor $D _ { i }$ or two adjacent divisors $D _ { i }$ , $D _ { i + 1 }$ .
However, some additional arguments are required to compare logarithmic invariants with the invariants described above as developed in [GPS09], and we do not wish to do this here.

Lemma 3.5. Let $J \subset P$ be an ideal with $\sqrt { J } = J$ .
Suppose the map $\eta$ : NE ${ \ ' } ( Y )  P$ satisfies the following conditions:

(1) For any ray $\mathfrak { d } \subset B$ of rational slope, let $\pi : \tilde { Y }  Y$ be the corresponding blowup.
We require that if $\dim \tau _ { \mathfrak { d } } = 2$ or $\dim \tau _ { \mathfrak { d } } = 1$ and $\kappa _ { \tau _ { 0 } , \varphi } \notin J$ then for any $\mathbb { A } ^ { 1 }$ -class $\beta$ contributing to $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ , we have $\eta ( \pi _ { * } ( \beta ) ) \in J$ .  
(2) For any ideal $I$ with $\sqrt { I } = J$ , there are only a finite number of $\mathfrak { d }$ and $\mathbb { A } ^ { 1 }$ -classes $\beta$ such that $\eta ( \pi _ { * } ( \beta ) ) \not \in I$ .

Then $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ is a scattering diagram for the data $( B , \Sigma ) , P , \varphi$ , and $J$ .

Proof.
Note that

$$
z ^ { \eta ( \pi _ { \ast } ( \beta ) ) - \varphi _ { \tau _ { 0 } } ( k _ { \beta } m _ { 0 } ) } \in I \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \tau _ { 0 } } } ]
$$

if and only if $\eta ( \pi _ { * } ( \beta ) ) \in I$ .
So the hypotheses of the lemma imply conditions (2)-(4) of Definition 2.13. 

Example 3.6. Let $\sigma \subset A _ { 1 } ( Y ) \otimes _ { \mathbb { Z } } \mathbb { R }$ be a strictly convex rational polyhedral cone containing $\mathrm { N E } ( Y )$ .
(This can be obtained as the dual of a strictly convex rational polyhedral cone in $\operatorname { P i c } ( Y ) \otimes _ { \mathbb { Z } } \mathbb { R }$ which spans this latter space and is contained in the nef cone.) Let $P = \sigma \cap A _ { 1 } ( Y )$ .
Since $\sigma$ is strictly convex, $P ^ { \times } = 0$ .
For any mprimary ideal $I$ , $P \setminus I$ is a finite set.
Let $\eta : \mathrm { N E } ( Y ) \to P$ be the inclusion.
Then the finiteness hypotheses of the above Lemma hold for $J = { \mathfrak { m } }$ (note that the conditions (3.1) determine $\beta \in A _ { 1 } ( \dot { Y } )$ given $\pi _ { * } ( \beta )$ ).

Example 3.7. We return to the example $( Y , D )$ of a del Pezzo surface together with a cycle of 5 $( - 1 )$ -curves studied in Example 1.9. Let $P = \mathrm { { N E } } ( Y )$ and $\eta$ be the identity.
Let $J = { \mathfrak { m } } \subset P$ and $I \subset P$ be an ideal with $\sqrt { I } = J$ .
Then $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ consists of five rays:

$$
\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } = \{ ( \rho _ { i } , 1 + z ^ { [ E _ { i } ] - \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } ( v _ { i } ) } ) | 1 \le i \le 5 \} .
$$

Here $E _ { i }$ is the unique $( - 1 )$ -curve in $Y$ which is not contained in $D$ and meets $D _ { i }$ transversally, and $v _ { i }$ is the primitive generator of the ray $\rho _ { i }$ corresponding to $D _ { i }$ .
To derive this formula from the above definition of the canonical scattering diagram one needs to show that the only possible stable relative maps contributing to $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ are multiple covers of the $E _ { i }$ ’s, and that a $k$ -fold multiple cover contributes a GromovWitten invariant of $( - 1 ) ^ { k - 1 } / k ^ { 2 }$ .
It is easier to compute this using the main result of [GPS09], which is done by way of Theorem 3.25. See Example 3.26.

If we accept this description of $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ , then we can describe all broken lines and the multiplication law given by this diagram.

We first note that no broken line can wrap around $0 \in B$ , i.e., if a broken line leaves a cone $\sigma \in \Sigma _ { \mathrm { m a x } }$ , it will never return to that cone.
It is enough to check this for a straight line (as the bending in any broken line for ${ \mathfrak { D } } _ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ is always away from the origin), and this is easily verified, using e.g., Figure 1.1.

Next, since the only scattering rays are the rays $\rho \in \Sigma$ , if $q , Q \in \sigma \in \Sigma _ { \operatorname* { m a x } }$ , then the obvious straight line is the unique broken line for $q$ with endpoint $Q$ .
Thus if we describe $\vartheta _ { q }$ in the open subset of $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } } ^ { o }$ corresponding to $\sigma$ , $\vartheta _ { q }$ is just the monomial $z ^ { \varphi _ { \sigma } ( q ) }$ .
It follows that

$$
\vartheta _ { v _ { i } } ^ { a } \vartheta _ { v _ { i + 1 } } ^ { b } = \vartheta _ { a v _ { i } + b v _ { i + 1 } }
$$

for $a , b \geq 0$ .
In particular, the $\vartheta _ { v _ { i } }$ ’s generate the $\mathbb { k } [ P ] / I$ -algebra $\Gamma ( X _ { I } , { \mathcal { O } } _ { X _ { I } } )$ , and the algebra structure is determined once we compute $\vartheta _ { v _ { i } } \cdot \vartheta _ { v _ { i + 2 } }$ .

We consider a broken line for $v _ { i }$ .
One checks the following, using Figure 1.1 and the above description of $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ : The broken line can cross at most two rays of $\Sigma$ , and it bends at most once, at the last ray of $\Sigma$ that it crosses.
See Figure 3.1. $\begin{array} { r } { \boldsymbol { \dot { \lambda } } } \end{array}$ From this one deduces using Theorem 2.34:

$$
\vartheta _ { v _ { i - 1 } } \vartheta _ { v _ { i + 1 } } = z ^ { [ D _ { i } ] } ( \vartheta _ { v _ { i } } + z ^ { [ E _ { i } ] } ) .
$$

![](images/9ea90c2ed0935818bb55cc81af6067784e4e566253b2545e565b59173dead850.jpg)  
Figure 3.1. The different types of broken lines in Example 3.7.

The term $z ^ { [ D _ { i } ] } \cdot \vartheta _ { v _ { i } }$ corresponds to two straight broken lines for $v _ { i - 1 } , v _ { i + 1 }$ , with endpoint the point $v _ { i }$ of $\rho _ { i }$ .
The term $z ^ { \left. D _ { i } \right. } \cdot z ^ { \left. E _ { i } \right. }$ is the coefficient of $1 = \vartheta _ { 0 }$ .
To compute this we use the invariance of broken lines, and so choose a generic point $Q$ near 0 and compute the coefficient $\alpha _ { 0 }$ of $\vartheta _ { 0 }$ using pairs $\gamma _ { i }$ as in Theorem 2.34 whose final directions are opposite, i.e., $s ( \gamma _ { 1 } ) + s ( \gamma _ { 2 } ) = 0$ .
If we take $Q \in \sigma _ { i , i + 1 }$ , then there is exactly one term contributing to $\alpha _ { 0 }$ : $\gamma _ { 1 }$ will bend once where it crosses $\rho _ { i }$ , and $\gamma _ { 2 }$ is straight.
Alternatively, one can use the explicit expressions for Lift $\mathbf { \Omega } _ { Q } ( v _ { j } )$ , $j = i - 1 , i$ and $i + 1$ , and see they satisfy the relation (3.2).

One can check that the five equations (3.2) define $X _ { I }$ .
These equations are algebraic, and in fact define a flat family over Spec k[NE $( Y )$ )].
(This is always the case in the nonnegative semi-definite case, see Corollary 6.11).

Our goal now is to prove consistency of $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ , as stated in the following (the final step in the construction of our mirror family):

Theorem 3.8. Suppose that we are given a map $\eta : \mathrm { N E } ( Y ) \to P$ such that $\varphi$ is defined as in Example 2.3 by $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } = \eta ( [ D _ { \rho } ] )$ .
Suppose furthermore the following conditions hold:

(I) For any $\mathbb { A } ^ { 1 }$ -class $\beta$ , $\eta ( \pi _ { * } ( \beta ) ) \in J$ ; (II) For any ideal $I$ with $\sqrt { I } = J$ , there are only a finite number of A1-classes $\beta$ such that $\eta ( \pi _ { * } ( \beta ) ) \not \in I$ .
(III) $\eta ( \left[ D _ { i } \right] ) \in J$ for at least one boundary component $D _ { i } \subset D$ .

Then $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ is a consistent scattering diagram.

We include here an observation we will need later showing that the canonical scattering diagram only depends on the deformation class of $( Y , D )$ .

Lemma 3.9. Let $( \mathcal { V } , \mathcal { D } ) \ \to \ S$ be a flat family of pairs over a connected base $S$ , with each fibre $( \mathcal { V } _ { s } , \mathcal { D } _ { s } )$ being a non-singular rational surface with anti-canonical cycle.
Suppose further that there is a trivialization ${ \mathcal { D } } \cong D \times S$ and the restriction map $\mathrm { P i c } ( \mathcal { Y } ) \to \mathrm { P i c } ( \mathcal { Y } _ { s } )$ is an isomorphism for any $s \in S$ .
This in particular gives a canonical identification $A _ { 1 } ( \mathcal { V } _ { s } , \mathbb { Z } )$ with $A _ { 1 } ( \mathcal { V } _ { s ^ { \prime } } , \mathbb { Z } )$ for any $s , s ^ { \prime } \in S$ .
Then for any $s , s ^ { \prime } \in S$ , $( \mathcal { V } _ { s } , \mathcal { D } _ { s } )$ and $( \mathcal { V } _ { s ^ { \prime } } , \mathcal { D } _ { s ^ { \prime } } )$ induce the same canonical scattering diagram.

Proof.
It is enough to show that the numbers $N _ { \beta }$ are deformation invariants in the above sense, i.e., if we are given a family $\pi : ( \tilde { \mathcal { V } } , \tilde { \mathcal { D } } )  S$ with each fibre as in Definition 3.1, with an irreducible component $\mathcal { C } \subset \tilde { \mathcal { D } }$ , then the number

$$
N _ { \beta , s } : = \int _ { [ \overline { { \mathfrak { M } } } ( \tilde { \mathcal { Y } } _ { s } ^ { o } / \mathcal { C } _ { s } ^ { o } , \beta ) ] ^ { v i r } } 1
$$

is independent of $s$ .
Indeed, once this is shown, then if $N _ { \beta , s } \neq 0$ , necessarily $\beta$ defines a class in $\mathrm { N E } ( \mathcal { V } _ { s } )$ , as well as in N $\lceil \mathrm { E } ( \mathcal { V } _ { s ^ { \prime } } )$ , under the chosen identification.
This invariance follows from the standard argument that (relative) Gromov-Witten invariants are deformation invariants, with a little care because our target spaces are open.
For this, one considers the moduli space $\overline { { \mathfrak { M } } } ( \tilde { \mathfrak { M } } ^ { o } / \mathcal { C } ^ { o } , \beta )$ of stable maps to $\tilde { \mathcal { V } } ^ { o }$ relative to $\mathcal { C } ^ { o }$ and whose composition with $\pi$ is constant.
Then one has a map $\psi : \overline { { \mathfrak { M } } } ( \tilde { \mathcal { N } } ^ { o } / \mathcal { C } ^ { o } , \beta ) \to S$ whose fibre over $s$ is $\overline { { \mathfrak { M } } } ( \tilde { \mathcal { V } } _ { s } ^ { o } / \mathcal { C } _ { s } ^ { o } , \beta )$ .
Letting $\xi$ be the inclusion of this fibre in $\overline { { \mathfrak { M } } } ( \bar { \mathcal { V } } ^ { o } / \mathcal { C } ^ { o } , \beta )$ , deformation invariance will follow if we know that

$$
\xi ^ { ! } [ \overline { { \mathfrak { M } } } ( \tilde { y } ^ { o } / \mathcal { C } ^ { o } , \beta ) ] ^ { v i r } = [ \overline { { \mathfrak { M } } } ( \tilde { y } _ { s } ^ { o } / \mathcal { C } _ { s } ^ { o } , \beta ) ] ^ { v i r }
$$

and $\psi$ is proper.
The first statement is standard in Gromov-Witten theory, see e.g. the argument of Theorem 4.2 of [LT98] in the non-relative case, which carries over to the relative case.
The second point, the properness of $\psi$ , follows exactly as in the proof of Lemma 3.2. 

3.2. Consistency: Overview of the proof.
We will describe in detail the intuition behind each step of the proof of consistency.
In the next subsection, we will work somewhat more generally with a more general scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D }$ for certain steps, as this will be needed in [K3] for the K3 case.
However, for the discussion here let us assume we are only studying the consistency of the canonical scattering diagram.

Step I.
We can replace $( Y , D )$ with a toric blow-up of $( Y , D )$ .
This is straightforward — toric blowups just correspond to refinements of $\Sigma$ , but do not change broken lines or scattering diagrams.

Step II.
We can assume that $( Y , D )$ has a toric model and $P$ is a finitely generated submonoid of $A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { Z } )$ containing $\operatorname { N E } ( Y )$ , with $\eta$ the inclusion.
By Step I and Proposition 1.3, we can assume $( Y , D )$ has a toric model.
We can then always factor $\eta$ a s $\mathrm { N E } ( Y ) { \xrightarrow { \bar { \eta } } } { \bar { P } } { \xrightarrow { \psi } } P$ where $P$ is a finitely generated submonoid of $A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { Z } )$ containing $\operatorname { N E } ( Y )$ with $\eta$ the inclusion.
In this case there are two canonical scattering diagrams, $\mathfrak { D }$ and $\mathfrak { D }$ defined using ${ \bar { \eta } } : { \mathrm { N E } } ( Y ) \to P$ and $\eta : \mathrm { N E } ( Y ) \to P$ respectively.
Then $\mathfrak { D }$ can be obtained from $\mathfrak { D }$ essentially just by applying $\psi$ to each exponent appearing in each function $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ .

In this case we show that if consistency holds for $\bar { \mathfrak { D } }$ then it holds for $\mathfrak { D }$ .
The idea is that given a broken line $\gamma$ for $\mathfrak { D }$ , we can get something like a broken line for $\mathfrak { D }$ by applying $\psi$ to the exponents of monomials attached to $\gamma$ .
However, this isn’t necessarily a broken line for $\mathfrak { D }$ .
Indeed, there might be two different broken lines for $\mathfrak { D }$ , say $\gamma _ { 1 }$ and $\gamma _ { 2 }$ , which after we apply $\psi$ give broken lines with the same sequence of attached exponents.
These should not arise as distinct broken lines for $\mathfrak { D }$ , and we have to combine the monomials attached to these broken lines.
This requires a certain amount of book-keeping.

Step III.
Reduction to the Gross–Siebert locus.
By Step II we can assume we have a toric model $p : Y  Y$ .
Let $H$ be an ample divisor on $Y$ .
Shrinking $P$ if necessary, we can assume that $P$ has a face of the form $P \cap ( p ^ { * } H ) ^ { \perp }$ .
Let $G$ be the monomial ideal which is the complement of this face, $E$ the subgroup of $P ^ { \mathrm { g p } }$ generated by $P \setminus G$ .
The main work in this step is to show that we can replace $P$ by $P + E$ .
This requires a bit of analysis of the rays $( \rho _ { i } , f _ { \rho _ { i } } )$ of $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ .
In particular, we need to understand the contribution to $f _ { \rho _ { i } }$ coming from the exceptional curves of $p$ meeting $D _ { i }$ .

After doing this, we have $P ^ { \times } = E$ , so now $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ lives over the thickening of a torus $\chi \mathrm { { s s } }$ we call the Gross-Siebert locus.

Step IV.
Pushing the singularities to infinity.
This is the crucial step, and we explain carefully the intuition here.
In [GS07], Gross and Siebert considered a smoothing construction associated to an integral affine manifold with singularities where (in the two-dimensional case) the singularities occurred only in the interior of edges of a polyhedral decomposition of $B$ , rather than at the vertices.
The case at hand, with one singularity at the origin, does not fit into that framework.
In particular, in the GrossSiebert world, the singularities must have monodromy of the form $\left( { \begin{array} { l l } { 1 } & { k } \\ { 0 } & { 1 } \end{array} } \right)$ for some $k > 0$ , with the tangent line to the edge containing the singularity being the invariant direction; in analogy with the Kodaira classification, we call this an $I _ { k }$ singularity.
Indeed, one expects a cycle of $k$ two-spheres as fibre over such a point in the SYZ picture.

Here, we can view such a surface as being obtained by factoring the complicated singularity $0 \in B$ into $I _ { k }$ singularities along the edges of $\Sigma$ .
We should have an $I _ { k _ { i } }$ singularity on the ray $\rho _ { i }$ where $k _ { i }$ is the number of exceptional divisors of $p : Y  Y$ intersecting $D _ { i }$ .
This process can be described as follows.
Let $( B , \Sigma )$ be the fan associated to $( Y , D )$ .
There is a piecewise linear isomorphism

$$
\nu : B \to \bar { B }
$$

which identifies each cone in $\Sigma$ with the corresponding cone in $\Sigma$ .
