This is an isomorphism of integral affine manifolds outside of $\rho _ { i }$ , but it is not affine along $\rho _ { i }$ .
There is a natural one-parameter family of integral affine manifolds interpolating between the two structures by a process Kontsevich and Soibelman [KS06] call moving worms.
Precisely, choose points $y _ { i } \in \rho _ { i } \setminus \{ 0 \}$ .
Let $\Delta : = \{ y _ { i } | 1 \leq i \leq n \}$ , $B _ { 0 } ^ { \prime } : = B \setminus \Delta$ .
Put a new affine structure on $B _ { 0 } ^ { \prime }$ compatible with the affine structures on the interior of each maximal cell by defining a $\Sigma$ -piecewise linear function to be linear if its restriction to a small neighbourhood of $( y _ { i } , + \infty ) \subset \rho _ { i }$ in $B _ { 0 } ^ { \prime }$ is $B$ -linear, and its restriction to a small neighbourhood of $[ 0 , y _ { i } ) \subset \rho _ { i }$ in $B _ { 0 } ^ { \prime }$ is $B$ -linear.
Call the resulting integral affine manifold with singularities $B ^ { \prime }$ .
The map $\nu : B ^ { \prime } \to B$ is a linear isomorphism near $0$ This new manifold can be seen to have an $I _ { k _ { i } }$ singularity at $y _ { i }$ , with invariant direction $\rho _ { i }$ .

Now if we were to apply the algorithm of Gross and Siebert [GS07] to $B ^ { \prime }$ , one would find roughly that one obtains a scattering diagram which initially has two rays emanating from each singularity.
The rays emanating from $\rho _ { i }$ are initially contained in $\rho _ { i }$ ; one of these goes out to infinity and the other passes through the origin and then to infinity.
Where all these rays meet at the origin, one must follow a procedure of Kontsevich and Soibelman [KS06] and add some additional rays to ensure that the composition of automorphisms associated to the rays about a loop centered at the origin is the identity.
We then obtain a scattering diagram which can be shown is very close to the canonical scattering diagram, the only difference being the segments of the rays between the $y _ { i }$ and the origin.

We do not actually work with this affine manifold with singularities.
Rather, we instead push the singularities $y _ { i }$ to infinity.
In doing so, we replace $B ^ { \prime }$ with $B$ .
We transfer the canonical scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D }$ to a scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D }$ on $B$ , differing from $\mathfrak { D }$ essentially only by changing the rays supported on the $\rho _ { i }$ ’s in a simple way motivated by the above description.
Once this is done, we show consistency of $\mathfrak { D }$ is equivalent to consistency of $\mathfrak { D }$ .
Now we no longer have to deal with any singularities.

It is much easier to determine consistency when there are no singularities.
In particular, we appeal to a result in [CPS], which shows that $\mathfrak { D }$ is consistent provided that the composition of automorphisms associated to the rays about a loop centered at the origin is the identity.
We say such a scattering diagram is compatible.
The important point is that we can now make sense of such a statement: when we had a singularity at the origin, there was no common ring which the automorphisms associated to rays could act on.
However, without a singularity at the origin, there are such rings, as appeared in [GS07].

Step $V . { \bar { \mathfrak { D } } }$ satisfies the required compatibility condition.
This step is really the punchline, explaining why the particular choice of the canonical scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D }$ gives a diagram $\bar { \mathfrak { D } }$ which is compatible.
We make use of [GPS09] to link the enumerative definition of $\mathfrak { D }$ to the notion of compatibility.
Indeed, the definition of the canonical scattering diagram was originally obtained by working backwards from the enumerative description of [GPS09].
This connection is worked out in §3.4.

3.3. Consistency: Reduction to the Gross–Siebert locus.
We now begin the proof of Theorem 3.8, following the outline given in §3.2.
We will, however, prove a number of lemmas in a slightly more general context, as we will need some more general consistency results in [K3].
We assume we are given $( Y , D )$ , $\eta : \mathrm { N E } ( Y ) \to P$ and $\varphi$ defined as in Example 2.3, and a radical ideal $J \subseteq P$ .
Suppose we are given a scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D }$ for this data; the application in this paper will be ${ \mathfrak { D } } = { \mathfrak { D } } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ .
In particular, the hypotheses of Theorem 3.8 imply $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ is a scattering diagram for this data.

Step I.
Replacing $( Y , D )$ with a toric blowup.

Proposition 3.10. Let $p : ( \tilde { Y } , \tilde { D } )  ( Y , D )$ be a toric blowup.
Then if we take $\tilde { \eta } : = \eta \circ p _ { \ast } : \mathrm { N E } ( \tilde { Y } )  P$ , then $\mathfrak { D }$ can also be viewed as a scattering diagram for $B _ { ( \tilde { Y } , \tilde { D } ) } , P$ .
Furthermore, if $\mathfrak { D }$ is consistent for this latter data, it is consistent for the data $B _ { ( Y , D ) } , P$ .

Proof.
Decorate notation, writing for example $\tilde { B } , \tilde { \Sigma }$ for the singular affine manifold with subdivision into cones associated to $( \dot { Y } , \ddot { D } )$ .
By Lemma 1.6, we have a canonical identification of the underlying singular affine manifolds $B = \tilde { B }$ , and $\tilde { \Sigma }$ is the refinement of $\Sigma$ obtained by adding one ray for each $p$ -exceptional divisor.
We have multivalued piecewise linear functions $\varphi$ on $B$ and $\ddot { \varphi }$ on $\tilde { B }$ .
We can in fact choose representatives so that $\tilde { \varphi } = \varphi$ .
Indeed, $\kappa _ { { \rho } , \tilde { \varphi } } = \eta ( p _ { * } ( [ \tilde { D } _ { \rho } ] ) )$ where $\tilde { D } _ { \rho }$ is the irreducible component of $\tilde { D }$ corresponding to $\rho$ .
But $p _ { * } ( [ \bar { D } _ { \rho } ] ) = 0$ if $\rho \not \in \Sigma$ , and $p _ { * } ( [ \tilde { D } _ { \rho } ] ) = [ D _ { \rho } ]$ if $\rho \in \Sigma$ .
Thus $\tilde { \varphi }$ in fact has the same domains of linearity as $\varphi$ , and the same bending parameters, so we can choose representatives which agree.

As a consequence, we note that the sheaves $\mathcal { P }$ and $\tilde { \mathcal P }$ on $B _ { 0 }$ defined using $\varphi$ and $\bar { \varphi }$ coincide.
Furthermore, if $\tilde { \tau } \subset \tilde { \sigma }$ are cones in $\tilde { \Sigma }$ , with $\tau \in \Sigma$ the smallest cone containing $\tilde { \tau }$ and $\sigma \in \Sigma$ the smallest cone containing $\tilde { \sigma }$ , there is a canonical identification of $P _ { \varphi \tau }$ with $P _ { \tilde { \varphi } _ { \tilde { \tau } } }$ and a canonical isomorphism

$$
{ \tilde { R } } _ { \tilde { \tau } , I } \cong { R } _ { \tau , I } ;
$$

note the slightly non-trivial case when $\dim \tilde { \tau } = 1$ but $\dim \tau = 2$ , in which case we use the fact that $\kappa _ { \tilde { \tau } , \tilde { \varphi } } = 0$ .

Using these identifications, we can view $\mathfrak { D }$ as living on $\tilde { B }$ , and as such, one sees from the definition that $\mathfrak { D }$ is a scattering diagram for the data $\tilde { B } , P , \tilde { \varphi }$ .

Now suppose $\sqrt { I } = J$ .
One observes that the set of broken lines contributing to $\operatorname { L i f t } _ { Q } ( q )$ are the same whether we are working in $B$ or $\tilde { B }$ .
Thus if $Q \in \tilde { \sigma } \in \tilde { \Sigma } _ { \mathrm { m a x } }$ , $\mathrm { L i f t } _ { Q } ( q ) \in \tilde { R } _ { \tilde { \sigma } , I }$ , defined using $\ddot { B }$ , coincides under the isomorphism (3.3) with Lift $ _ { Q } ( q ) \in$ $R _ { \sigma , I }$ .
From this one sees easily that if $\mathfrak { D }$ is consistent for $\tilde { Y }$ , it is consistent for $Y$ .
$\boxed { \begin{array} { r l } \end{array} }$

Corollary 3.11. Given $Y , P , \eta , J$ satisfying the hypotheses of Theorem 3.8, then Theorem 3.8 holds for this data if it holds for the data $\tilde { Y } , P , \tilde { \eta } , J$ .

Proof.
By the proposition, one just needs to check that the canonical scattering diagrams defined using $Y$ or $\tilde { Y }$ are identical.
Indeed, given a ray $\mathfrak { d } \subset B$ , we can choose a refinement $\Sigma ^ { \prime }$ of $\Sigma$ which is also a refinement of $\tilde { \Sigma }$ , giving maps $\tilde { \pi } : Y ^ { \prime } \to \tilde { Y }$ and $\pi : Y ^ { \prime } \to Y$ .
Then for an $\mathbb { A } ^ { 1 }$ -class $\beta \in A _ { 1 } ( Y ^ { \prime } , \mathbb { Z } )$ , $\eta ( \pi _ { * } ( \beta ) ) = \tilde { \eta } ( \tilde { \pi } _ { * } ( \beta ) )$ , and so $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ is the same for $\tilde { Y }$ and $Y$ .


Step II.
Changing the monoid $P$ .

We would like to change the monoid $P$ , which was fairly arbitrary, to one with better properties.
For this step, assume we are given monoid homomorphisms

$$
\mathrm { N E } ( Y ) { \xrightarrow { \bar { \eta } } } { \bar { P } } { \xrightarrow { \psi } } P
$$

with $\eta = \psi \circ \bar { \eta }$ .
Then $\eta$ and $\eta$ induce multivalued piecewise linear functions $\varphi$ and $\varphi$ respectively, via Example 2.3, with $\varphi = \psi \circ \bar { \varphi }$ .
The monoids $P$ , $P$ and functions $\varphi , \varphi$ yield sheaves $\mathcal { P }$ and $\mathcal { P }$ over $B _ { 0 }$ .
The map $\psi : { \cal P }  { \cal P }$ induces a map of sheaves $\psi : \mathcal { P }  \mathcal { P }$ using $\varphi ~ = ~ \psi ~ \circ ~ \bar { \varphi }$ , and hence it also induces monoid homomorphisms $\psi : P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \tau } } \to P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } }$ for any $\tau \in \Sigma \setminus \{ 0 \}$ .

Suppose $\mathfrak { D }$ is a scattering diagram for the data $B , P , \bar { \mathfrak { m } } = P \backslash P ^ { \times }$ .
For each ray $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } )$ , $f _ { \mathfrak { d } } \in \bar { \mathbb { k } } \bar { [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \tau _ { \mathfrak { d } } } } ] }$ .
Now we can try to define $\psi ( f _ { \mathfrak { d } } )$ by applying $\psi$ to each exponent of $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ , but in general, this need not make sense even formally since $\psi$ may take an infinite number of exponents occuring in $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ to a single element of $P$ .
However, we shall write $\psi ( f _ { \mathfrak { d } } )$ for such an expression if it does make sense as an element of $\widehat { \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \tau _ { 0 } } } ] }$ .
If $\psi ( f _ { \mathfrak { d } } )$ makes sense for each $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \in \mathfrak { D }$ , we write

$$
\psi ( { \bar { \mathfrak { D } } } ) = \{ ( { \mathfrak { d } } , \psi ( f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) ) \mid ( { \mathfrak { d } } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \in { \bar { \mathfrak { D } } } \} .
$$

Proposition 3.12. In the above situation, suppose $\mathfrak { D }$ is a scattering diagram for the data $B , \bar { P } , \bar { \mathfrak { m } } = \bar { P } \setminus \bar { P } ^ { \times }$ , such that ${ \mathfrak { D } } = \psi ( { \bar { \mathfrak { D } } } )$ makes sense and is a scattering diagram for the data $B , P , J$ , where $J$ is a radical ideal in $P$ .
Assume that $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in J$ for at least one ray $\rho \in \Sigma$ .
If $\bar { \mathfrak { D } }$ is consistent for $P , { \bar { \eta } } , { \bar { \mathfrak { m } } }$ , then $\mathfrak { D }$ is consistent for $P , \eta , J$ .

Proof.
Let $q \in B _ { 0 } ( \mathbb { Z } )$ .
Then if $\bar { \gamma }$ is a broken line for $q$ with endpoint $Q$ with respect to the barred data, i.e., $P$ , $\mathcal { P }$ etc., we can construct what we shall call $\psi ( \bar { \gamma } )$ .
This will be the data required for defining a broken line for the unbarred data.
The underlying map of $\psi ( \bar { \gamma } )$ coincides with that of $\gamma$ .
For the attached monomials, we simply apply $\psi$ to the monomial $m _ { L } ( \bar { \gamma } )$ attached to a domain of linearity $L$ of $\bar { \gamma }$ to get the attached monomial for $\psi ( \bar { \gamma } )$ .
This is not a broken line for the unbarred data, as condition (3) of Definition 2.16 need not hold.
Indeed, when a broken line bends at a ray, the attached monomial will be replaced by a term in (2.9).
However, there might be several different terms $c _ { i } z ^ { s _ { i } }$ appearing in (2.9) with $\bar { s } _ { i } \in P _ { \varphi _ { \tau } }$ such that $\psi ( { \bar { s } } _ { i } )$ all coincide with some $s \in  { \mathcal { P } } _ { \varphi _ { \tau } }$ .
Each choice $c _ { i } z ^ { s _ { i } }$ leads to a different broken line $\gamma _ { i }$ , but $\psi ( \bar { \gamma } _ { i } )$ is not a broken line because $c _ { i } z ^ { \psi ( \bar { s } _ { i } ) } = c _ { i } z ^ { s }$ is not a term in the formula (2.9) for the monoid $P$ .
Rather, one needs to replace the collection of broken lines $\gamma _ { i }$ with a single one which has monomial $\textstyle \sum _ { i } c _ { i } z ^ { s }$ attached after the bend.
To deal with this, we need to do a certain amount of book-keeping.

Fix an ideal $I \subset P$ with $\sqrt { I } = J$ , $Q \in \sigma \in \Sigma$ , and let $\mathfrak { B }$ be the set of broken lines $\gamma$ for the barred data with endpoint $Q$ such that $\psi ( \mathrm { M o n o } ( \bar { \gamma } ) ) \not \in I \cdot \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma } } ]$ .
The same finiteness argument of Lemma 2.25 shows that $\mathfrak { B }$ is a finite set.
Note this uses the facts (1) at least one $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in J$ and (2) all but a finite number of monomials appearing in $\mathfrak { D }$ lie in $I$ .

We define an equivalence relation on $\mathfrak { B }$ by saying $\gamma _ { 1 } \sim \gamma _ { 2 }$ provided $\psi ( { \bar { \gamma } } _ { 1 } )$ and $\psi ( { \bar { \gamma } } _ { 2 } )$ coincide except possibly for the $\Bbbk$ -valued coefficients of the monomials attached to the domains of linearity.
Given an equivalence class $\xi \subset \mathfrak { B }$ with respect to this equivalence relation, we will show there is at most one broken line $\gamma _ { \xi }$ for the unbarred data such that

$$
\sum _ { \bar { \gamma } \in \xi } \psi ( \operatorname { M o n o } ( \bar { \gamma } ) ) = \operatorname { M o n o } ( \gamma _ { \xi } ) ,
$$

with there being no such broken line precisely if the above quantity is zero.
Furthermore, every broken line $\gamma$ for the unbarred data with $\mathrm { M o n o } ( \gamma ) \notin I \cdot \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma } } ]$ arises in this way.

Define $\gamma _ { \xi }$ to be the broken line with underlying piecewise linear map given by any element of $\xi$ , with the following attached monomials.
For any domain of linearity $\boldsymbol { L } = [ s , t ]$ for $\gamma _ { \xi }$ , choose a maximal subset $\xi _ { L } \subset \xi$ of broken lines such that the attached

monomials for $\gamma _ { 1 }$ and $\gamma _ { 2 }$ on $( - \infty , t ]$ do not coincide for any $\bar { \gamma } _ { 1 } , \bar { \gamma } _ { 2 } \in \xi _ { L }$ .
Then define

$$
m _ { L } ( \gamma _ { \xi } ) = \sum _ { \bar { \gamma } \in \xi _ { L } } m _ { L } ( \psi ( \bar { \gamma } ) ) .
$$

Assuming that the final monomial attached to $\gamma _ { \xi }$ is not zero, one checks easily that $\gamma _ { \xi }$ is a broken line, now satisfying (3) of Definition 2.16, and (3.4) is satisfied since for $L$ the last domain of linearity of $\gamma _ { \xi }$ , one takes $\xi _ { L } = \xi$ .
Furthermore, it is easy to see that any broken line for the unbarred data with the same underlying map and attached monomials at most differing by their coefficients from $\gamma _ { \xi }$ must in fact coincide with $\gamma _ { \xi }$ .
This shows the claim.

Since $\mathfrak { B }$ is finite, there is some $k > 0$ such that for any $\bar { \gamma } \in \mathfrak { B }$ , $\mathrm { M o n o } ( \bar { \gamma } ) \in \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \overline { { \varphi } } _ { \tau } } ]$ does not lie in $\bar { \mathfrak { m } } ^ { k } \cdot \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \overline { { \varphi } } _ { \tau } } ]$ .
If we take $I = \psi ^ { - 1 } ( I )$ , then it is clear from (3.4) that

$$
\psi ( \overline { { \mathrm { L i f t } } } _ { Q } ( q ) ) = \mathrm { L i f t } _ { Q } ( q ) ,
$$

where $\overline { { \mathrm { L i f t } } } _ { Q } ( q )$ is the lift defined with respect to the ideal $I$ and the other barred data, and Lift $_ Q ( q )$ is defined with respect to the unbarred data and the ideal $I$ .
Now $\mathfrak { D }$ is consistent for $\mathfrak { m }$ , which implies (1) and (2) of Definition 2.26 hold for the ideal $\bar { \mathfrak { m } } ^ { k } + I$ .
Since any monomial in $P \setminus I$ appearing in $\overline { { \mathrm { L i f t } } } _ { Q } ( q )$ is in $P \setminus ( \bar { \mathfrak { m } } ^ { k } + I )$ , we can use (3.5) to deduce consistency of $\mathfrak { D }$ from consistency of $\mathfrak { D }$ .


Step III.
Reduction to the Gross-Siebert locus.
As a consequence of Proposition 1.3 and Corollary 3.11, in order to prove Theorem 3.8 (i.e., with ${ \mathfrak { D } } = { \mathfrak { D } } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ ), we may assume we have a toric model $p : ( Y , D )  ( Y , D )$ with $D = D _ { 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + D _ { n }$ .
Furthermore, by replacing $( Y , D )$ with a deformation equivalent pair and using Lemma 3.9, we can assume that $p$ is the blowup at distinct points $x _ { i j }$ , $1 \le j \le \ell _ { i }$ , along $D _ { i }$ , with exceptional divisors $E _ { i j }$ .
Assume $D _ { i }$ is the proper transform of $D _ { i }$ , corresponding to the ray $\rho _ { i } \in \Sigma$ .

By Proposition 3.12, we can replace $P$ with a better suited choice of monoid.
We shall do this as follows in the case that $\mathfrak { D } = \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ .
As in Example 3.6, the nef cone ${ \overline { { \kappa } } } ( Y ) \subset$ $A ^ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { R } )$ contains a strictly convex rational polyhedral cone $\sigma$ , so $\sigma ^ { \vee } \subset A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { R } )$ is a strictly convex rational polyhedral cone containing NE $( Y )$ .
The map $\eta : \mathrm { N E } ( Y ) \to P$ induces a map $\eta : A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { R } ) \to P _ { \mathbb { R } } ^ { \mathrm { g p } }$ .
Since $P$ is toric, there is some rational polyhedral cone $\sigma _ { P } \subset P _ { \mathbb { R } } ^ { \mathrm { g p } }$ such that $P = \sigma _ { P } \cap P ^ { \mathrm { g p } }$ .
In addition, let $H$ be an ample divisor on $Y$ , so that $\mathrm { N E } ( Y ) \cap ( p ^ { * } H ) ^ { \perp }$ is a face of $\mathrm { N E } ( Y )$ , generated by the classes $\left[ E _ { i j } \right]$ .
Now take

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \sigma _ { \bar { P } } = \eta ^ { - 1 } ( \sigma _ { P } ) \cap \sigma ^ { \vee } \cap \{ q \in A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { R } ) | p ^ { * } H \cdot q \geq 0 \} , } \end{array}
$$

and take

$$
\bar { P } = \sigma _ { \bar { P } } \cap A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { Z } ) .
$$

As $\sigma _ { \bar { P } }$ is strictly convex, $( P ) ^ { \times } = \{ 0 \}$ , ${ \bar { \mathfrak { m } } } = P \setminus \{ 0 \}$ , and if $I$ is an $\mathfrak { m }$ -primary ideal, $P \setminus I$ is finite.
Thus the hypotheses of Theorem 3.8 trivially hold for ${ \bar { \eta } } : { \mathrm { N E } } ( Y ) \to P$ .
By Proposition 3.12, we can replace $P$ with $P$ to prove Theorem 3.8.

The above discussion shows that in order to complete a proof of consistency of $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ , (i.e., Theorem 3.8), we can operate under the following assumptions:

# Assumptions 3.13.

There is a toric model

$$
p : ( Y , D )  ( { \bar { Y } } , { \bar { D } } )
$$

which blows up distinct points $x _ { i j }$ on $D _ { i }$ , with exceptional divisors $E _ { i j }$ .

• $\eta : \mathrm { N E } ( Y )  P$ is an inclusion, and $P ^ { \times } = \{ 0 \}$ .
Via Example 2.3, this gives the function $\varphi$ .  
• There is a face of $P$ whose intersection with $\mathrm { N E } ( Y )$ is $\mathrm { N E } ( Y ) \cap ( p ^ { * } H ) ^ { \perp }$ .
Let $G$ be the prime monomial ideal given by the complement of this face.
Note that $G \neq { \mathfrak { m } }$ unless $p$ is an isomorphism.  
• $J = \mathfrak { m } = P \setminus \{ 0 \}$ .  
• $\mathfrak { D }$ is a scattering diagram for the data $P , \varphi$ and $J$ .

Definition 3.14. The Gross–Siebert locus is the open torus orbit $T ^ { \mathrm { \Delta \mathfrak { g s } } }$ of Spec $\mathbb { k } [ P ] / G$

We now want to work not with the maximal ideal $\mathfrak { m }$ but with the ideal $G$ , effectively extending the families $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ with $\sqrt { I } = \mathfrak { m }$ to infinitesimal neighbourhoods of the toric boundary stratum of $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk | P |$ associated to $G$ .
We will then find it easier to check the explicit equalities of Definition 2.26 after restricting to these neighbourhoods of the Gross–Siebert locus.
To do so requires showing that the diagram $\mathfrak { D }$ we are working with ( $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ in this paper) is also a scattering diagram for the data $P , \eta , G$ .
In the case of $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ , this requires analyzing elements of this scattering diagram supported on the $\rho _ { i }$ .

We first perform this analysis for $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ ; we will then continue our proof assuming that the elements of $\mathfrak { D }$ supported on the rays $\rho _ { i }$ take the same form as the corresponding elements of $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ modulo $G$ .

For each ray $\rho _ { i }$ in $\Sigma$ , we have a unique ray $( \rho _ { i } , f _ { \rho _ { i } } ) \in \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ with support $\rho _ { i }$ .
The following describes $f _ { \rho _ { i } }$ mod $G$ .

Lemma 3.15. Given Assumption 3.13 with $\mathfrak { D } = \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ , viewing $f _ { \rho _ { i } }$ as an element of $\mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } } ] \otimes R _ { I }$ with $\sqrt { I } = \mathfrak { m }$ , we have

$$
f _ { \rho _ { i } } = g _ { \rho _ { i } } \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } X _ { i } ^ { - 1 } )
$$

where $b _ { i j } = z ^ { \eta ( [ E _ { i j } ] ) }$ and $g _ { \rho _ { i } } \equiv 1 \mod G$ .
The $j ^ { t h }$ term of the product is the contribution from $\mathbb { A } ^ { 1 }$ -classes coming from multiple covers of the $p$ -exceptional divisor $E _ { i j }$ , and $g _ { \rho _ { i } }$ is the product of contributions from all other $\mathbb { A } ^ { 1 }$ -classes.

Proof.
Note that in defining $f _ { \rho _ { i } }$ using the definition of the canonical scattering diagram, we take $\tilde { Y } = Y$ .
Now the only terms that contribute to $f _ { \rho _ { i } }$ mod $G$ will involve classes $\beta \in \mathrm { N E } ( Y ) \subset A _ { 1 } ( Y )$ with $\eta ( \beta ) \not \in G$ , so in particular, such a $\beta$ must be a linear combination $\textstyle \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } c _ { j } [ E _ { i j } ]$ , with $k _ { \beta } = \sum c _ { j }$ .
Furthermore, if $f : C \to Y$ contributes to $N _ { \beta }$ , $f ( C )$ must be contained in $\cup _ { i , j } E _ { i j }$ .
Indeed, if $f ( C )$ has an irreducible component $C ^ { \prime }$ not contained in this set, then $\eta ( \left[ C ^ { \prime } \right] ) \in G$ , so $\eta ( f _ { \ast } ( \left[ C \right] ) ) \in G$ , as $G$ is an ideal.
But $\eta ( f _ { * } ( \left[ C \right] ) ) = \eta ( \beta )$ , which we have assumed is not an element of $G$ .

Since $f ( C )$ is connected and intersects $D _ { i }$ , we now see that the image of $f$ is $E _ { i j } ^ { \prime }$ for some $j$ , and in particular, $f$ is a degree $k _ { \beta }$ cover of $E _ { i j } ^ { \prime }$ .
Then Theorem 6.1 of [GPS09] tells us that the contribution from $k _ { \beta }$ -fold multiple covers of $E _ { i j } ^ { \prime }$ is $( - 1 ) ^ { k _ { \beta } - 1 } / k _ { \beta } ^ { 2 }$ .
From this we conclude that

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { f _ { \rho _ { i } } = \displaystyle \exp \left( h + \sum _ { k = 1 } ^ { \infty } \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } k \left( \frac { ( - 1 ) ^ { k - 1 } } { k ^ { 2 } } \right) ( b _ { i j } X _ { i } ^ { - 1 } ) ^ { k } \right) } } \\ { { = \displaystyle \exp ( h ) \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } X _ { i } ^ { - 1 } ) } } \end{array}
$$

where $h \equiv 0 \mod G$ .
We take $g _ { \rho _ { i } } = \exp ( h )$

Corollary 3.16. $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ is a scattering diagram for the data $( B , \Sigma )$ , $P$ , $\varphi$ and $G$

Proof.
Fixing an $I \subset P$ with $\sqrt { I } = G$ , there exists a bound $n$ such that $q \in P \setminus I$ implies $q \cdot p ^ { * } H < n$ , where $H$ is a fixed ample divisor on $Y$ .
Thus if $\beta$ is an $\mathbb { A } ^ { 1 }$ - class with $\eta ( \pi _ { * } ( \beta ) ) \in P \setminus I$ , there are only a finite number of choices for $p _ { \ast } \pi _ { \ast } \beta$ .
We need to examine the possible choices for $\pi _ { * } \beta$ .
Given a choice for $\alpha = p _ { * } \pi _ { * } \beta$ , we have $\pi _ { * } \beta = p ^ { * } \alpha + \textstyle \sum a _ { i j } E _ { i j }$ for some collection of $a _ { i j } \in \mathbb { Z }$ .
Clearly the $a _ { i j }$ are bounded below by the requirement that $( \pi _ { * } \beta ) \cdot D _ { i } \ge 0$ for each $i$ .
On the other hand, if $a _ { i j } > 0$ for some $i , j$ , then $( \pi _ { * } \beta ) \cdot E _ { i j } < 0$ , so if $f \colon C \to Y$ is an $\mathbb { A } ^ { 1 }$ -curve with $f _ { * } \vert C \vert = \pi _ { * } \beta$ then its reduced image $C ^ { \prime }$ must contain $E _ { i j }$ .
(Technically a relative stable map in $\tilde { Y } ^ { \circ }$ is a map to an expanded degeneration of ${ \tilde { Y } } ^ { \circ }$ , but we compose with the projection to ${ \tilde { Y } } ^ { \circ }$ and then the natural map $\tilde { Y } ^ { \circ }  Y$ .) Write $C ^ { \prime } = C ^ { \prime \prime } \cup E _ { i j }$ , with $C ^ { \prime \prime }$ a reduced divisor distinct from $E _ { i j }$ .
Suppose $C ^ { \prime \prime }$ is non-empty.
Necessarily either $C ^ { \prime \prime } \cap D$ is empty or $C ^ { \prime \prime }$ intersects $\boldsymbol { D }$ only at $E _ { i j } \cap D$ ; otherwise $C ^ { \prime }$ cannot be the image of a relative stable map with one point of tangency with $\boldsymbol { D }$ .
In either case there is an integer $k$ such that $\mathcal { O } _ { Y } ( C ^ { \prime \prime } + k E _ { i j } ) | _ { D }$ is the trivial sheaf.
However, by [GHK12], Proposition 4.1, for a general deformation $( Y ^ { \prime } , D )$ of $( Y , D )$ , the kernel of the restriction map $\operatorname* { P i c } Y ^ { \prime } \to \operatorname* { P i c } D$ is trivial.
Thus by Lemma 3.9, $N _ { \beta } ~ = ~ 0$ .
We conclude that there are only a finite number of choices of $\pi _ { * } \beta$ , except when $\pi _ { * } \beta$ is a multiple of some $E _ { i j }$ .
This shows condition (4) in Definition 2.13 of scattering diagrams, as well as condition (2).
Note that $\kappa _ { \rho _ { i } , \varphi } = [ D _ { i } ] \in G$ for each $i$ so condition (3) is vacuous for $\dim \tau _ { \mathfrak { d } } = 1$ .
If $\dim \tau _ { \mathfrak { d } } = 2$ , any contributing $\mathbb { A } ^ { 1 }$ -class $\beta$ satisfies $\pi _ { * } \beta \in G$ , so (3) holds.


Theorem 3.17. We follow the above notation.
If $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ is consistent as a scattering diagram for $( B , \Sigma )$ , $P$ , $\varphi$ , and $G$ , then Theorem 3.8 is true.

Proof.
This just follows from the series of reductions of Theorem 3.8 already made and the observation that if $I ^ { \prime }$ is an m-primary ideal, then since $G \subset { \mathfrak { m } }$ one can find some $k$ such that $k G \subset I ^ { \prime }$ .
To show consistency holds for the ideal $I ^ { \prime }$ , we use the assumed consistency to observe consistency holds for the ideal $I = k G$ , and this gives the desired result.


Remark 3.18. Given a consistent scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D }$ for $( B , \Sigma )$ , $P$ , $\varphi$ , and $G$ , and $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } \in G$ for all rays $\rho \in \Sigma$ , Theorem 2.28 shows that with ${ \sqrt { I } } = G$ ,

$$
X _ { I } : = \mathrm { S p e c } \Gamma ( X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o } , \mathcal { O } _ { X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o } } )
$$

is flat over Spec $\mathbb { k } [ P ] / I$ , and $X _ { G } = \mathbb { V } _ { n } \times \operatorname { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P ] / G$ .

Let $T ^ { \mathrm { g s } } \subset \mathrm { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P ] / G$ be the Gross–Siebert locus, Definition 3.14. Note $\chi { \mathrm { g s } }$ determines open subschemes of the thickenings $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P ] / I$ , which we will shall denote by $T _ { I } ^ { \mathrm { g s } }$ .

We can describe the subscheme $T _ { I } ^ { \mathrm { g s } }$ of $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P ] / I$ as follows.
Let $E \subset P ^ { \mathrm { g p } }$ be the lattice generated by the face $P \setminus G$ .
Then as a subset of $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P ] / G$ , $T ^ { \mathrm { g s } } \cong \operatorname { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ E ]$ .
Furthermore, if we take the localization $P + E$ of $P$ along the face $P \setminus G$ , then $T _ { I } ^ { \mathrm { g s } }$ as a subscheme of $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P ] / I$ is $\operatorname { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P + E ] / ( I + E )$ .

Note that ${ \mathfrak { m } } _ { P + E } ~ = ~ ( P + E ) \setminus E$ , and $G = P \cap { \mathfrak { m } } _ { P + E }$ , so we can write $\mathbb { k } [ E ] =$ $\mathbb { k } [ P + E ] / { \mathfrak { m } } _ { P + E }$ .


We can now view $\varphi$ as a multivalued strictly $( P + E )$ -convex function.
Then we have the following obvious

Lemma 3.19. Suppose $\mathfrak { D }$ is a consistent scattering diagram for the data $( B , \Sigma )$ , $P + E$ , $\varphi$ , $\mathbf { m } _ { P + E }$ , and a scattering diagram for the data $( B , \Sigma )$ , $P$ , $\varphi$ , and $G$ .
Then $\mathfrak { D }$ is also consistent as a scattering diagram for the latter data.
In particular, by Theorem 3.17, Theorem 3.8 holds if $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ is consistent as a scattering diagram for $P + E$ , ${ \mathfrak { m } } _ { P + E }$ .

Proof.
Since $P \cap { \mathfrak { m } } _ { P + E } = G$ , the equalities in Definition 2.26 can be tested for an ideal $I$ of $P$ with ${ \sqrt { I } } = G$ by choosing some ideal $I ^ { \prime } \subseteq P + E$ with $\sqrt { I ^ { \prime } } = { \mathfrak { m } } _ { P + E }$ and $I ^ { \prime } \cap P \subset I$ .
Then the equalities of Definition 2.26 hold for the data $P + E$ and $I ^ { \prime }$ by the assumed consistency, and hence also for $P$ and $I$ .
$\boxed { \begin{array} { r l } \end{array} }$

Let $I \subset { \mathfrak { m } } _ { P + E }$ be an ideal with $\sqrt { \bar { I } } = \mathfrak { m } _ { P + E }$ .
Set $I = I \cap P$ .
Then $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ is flat over Spec $\mathbb { k } [ P ] / I$ .
Restricting $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ to the open set $\operatorname { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P + E ] / I$ gives the flat family $X _ { \bar { I } , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ .

We now replace $P$ by $P + E$ and $J$ by ${ \mathbf { m } } _ { P + E }$ in what follows.
We now summarize our current situation with the following assumptions:

# Assumptions 3.20.

There is a toric model

$$
p : ( Y , D )  ( { \bar { Y } } , { \bar { D } } )
$$

which blows up distinct points $x _ { i j }$ on $D _ { i }$ , $1 \le j \le \ell _ { i }$ , with exceptional divisors $E _ { i j }$ .

• $\eta : \mathrm { N E } ( Y )  P$ is an inclusion.
Via Example 2.3, this gives the function $\varphi$ .
$E = P ^ { \times } = P \cap ( p ^ { * } H ) ^ { \perp }$ is generated by the classes of exceptional curves of $p$ .
Let $G = P \setminus E = \mathfrak { m } _ { P }$ .

• $\mathfrak { D }$ is a scattering diagram for the data $P , \varphi$ and $G$ .
Furthermore, for each ray $\rho _ { i } \in \Sigma$ , the unique outgoing ray $( \rho _ { i } , f _ { \rho _ { i } } ) \in \mathfrak { D }$ satisfies

$$
f _ { \rho _ { i } } = g _ { \rho _ { i } } \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } X _ { i } ^ { - 1 } )
$$

with $g _ { \rho _ { i } } \equiv 1$ mod $G$ and $b _ { i j } = z ^ { [ E _ { i j } ] }$ .

We note we have shown that ${ \mathfrak { D } } = { \mathfrak { D } } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ achieves these assumptions.

Step IV.
Pushing the singularities to infinity.
We work with Assumptions 3.20. Consider the tropicalisation $( B , \Sigma )$ of $( Y , D )$ .
By Example 1.7, $B$ in fact has no singularity at the origin, and is affine isomorphic to $M _ { \mathbb { R } } = \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 }$ (with $M = \mathbb { Z } ^ { 2 }$ ), while $\Sigma$ is precisely the fan for $Y$ .
In order to distinguish between constructions on $( Y , D )$ and $( Y , D )$ , we decorate all existing notation with bars.
For example, if $\tau \in \Sigma$ , denote the corresponding cone of $\Sigma$ by $\tau$ .
Let $\varphi$ be the multivalued $P _ { \mathbb { R } } ^ { \mathrm { g p } }$ -valued function on $B$ such that

$$
\kappa _ { \bar { \rho } , \bar { \varphi } } = p ^ { * } [ \bar { D } _ { \bar { \rho } } ] .
$$

Note that by Lemma 1.13, we can assume $\varphi$ is in fact a single-valued function on $M _ { \mathbb { R } }$ .
This single-valuedness will be important to be able to apply the method of Kontsevich and Soibelman, Theorem 3.23.

We now have sheaves $\mathcal { P }$ on $B _ { 0 }$ and $\mathcal { P }$ on $B _ { 0 }$ , induced by the two functions $\bar { \varphi }$ and $\varphi$ respectively.

Note that since $\varphi$ is single-valued and $B$ has no singularities, $\mathcal { P }$ is the constant sheaf with fibre $P ^ { \mathrm { g p } } \oplus M$ .

There is a canonical piecewise linear map

$$
\nu : B \to \bar { B }
$$

which restricts to an integral affine isomorphism $\nu | _ { \sigma } : \sigma \to \bar { \sigma }$ , where $\sigma \in \Sigma _ { \mathrm { m a x } }$ and $\bar { \sigma } \in \Sigma _ { \operatorname* { m a x } }$ is the corresponding cell of $\Sigma$ .
Note this map identifies $B ( \mathbb { Z } )$ with $B ( \mathbb { Z } )$ .

For each maximal cone $\sigma \in \Sigma _ { \mathrm { m a x } }$ , the derivative $\nu _ { * }$ of $\nu$ induces a canonical identification of $\Lambda _ { B , \sigma }$ with $\Lambda _ { \bar { B } , \overline { { \sigma } } }$ .
This then gives an induced isomorphism of monoids:

$$
\tilde { \nu } _ { \sigma } : P _ { \varphi _ { \sigma } } \to P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \bar { \sigma } } }
$$

given by

$$
\varphi _ { \sigma } ( m ) + p \mapsto \bar { \varphi } _ { \bar { \sigma } } ( \nu _ { * } ( m ) ) + p ,
$$

for $p \in P$ and $m \in \Lambda _ { \sigma }$ .
This identifies the $\mathbb { k } \lfloor P \rfloor$ -algebras $\mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi \sigma } ]$ and $\mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \bar { \sigma } } } ]$ , and the completions $\widehat { \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi \sigma } ] }$ and $\widehat { \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } \bar { \sigma } } ] }$ .

Because the map $\nu$ is only piecewise linear around rays $\rho \in \Sigma$ , there is only a piecewise linear identification of $P _ { \varphi _ { \rho } }$ with $P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \bar { \rho } } }$ and hence no identification of the corresponding rings.
However, $\nu _ { * }$ is still defined on the tangent space to $\rho$ , and there is an identification

$\tilde { \nu } _ { \rho } : \{ \varphi _ { \rho } ( m ) + p | m$ is tangent to $\rho$ , $p \in P \} \to \{ \bar { \varphi } _ { \bar { \rho } } ( m ) + p \vert m$ is tangent to $\rho$ , $p \in P \}$ given by

$$
\varphi _ { \rho } ( m ) + p \mapsto \bar { \varphi } _ { \bar { \rho } } ( \nu _ { * } ( m ) ) + p .
$$

We now explain the Kontsevich-Soibelman lemma.
This has to do with scattering diagrams on the smooth affine surface $M _ { \mathbb { R } } = \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 }$ (such as $B = B _ { ( \bar { Y } , \bar { D } ) , }$ ).
For this general discussion, we fix the data of a monoid $Q$ which comes along with a map $r : Q \to M$ .
Let ${ \mathfrak { m } } _ { Q } = Q \backslash Q ^ { \times }$ , and let $\widehat { \mathbb { k } [ Q ] }$ denote the completion of $\mathbb { k } \lfloor Q \rfloor$ with respect to the monomial ideal ${ \mathfrak { m } } _ { Q }$ .
(In our application we take $Q = P _ { \bar { \varphi } }$ as defined in (1.5).)

We can then consider a variant of the notion of scattering diagram:

Definition 3.21. We define a scattering diagram for the pair $Q$ , $r : Q \to M$ .
This is a set

$$
\mathfrak { D } = \{ ( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \}
$$

where

• ${ \mathfrak { d } } \subset M _ { \mathbb { R } }$ is given by

$$
\mathfrak { d } = - \mathbb { R } _ { \ge 0 } m _ { 0 }
$$

if $\mathfrak { d }$ is an outgoing ray and

$$
\mathfrak { d } = \mathbb { R } _ { \geq 0 } m _ { 0 }
$$

if $\mathfrak { o }$ is an incoming ray, for some $m _ { 0 } \in M \setminus \{ 0 \}$ .

• $f _ { \mathfrak { d } } \in \widehat { \mathbb { k } [ Q ] }$ .  
• $f _ { 0 } \equiv 1 \mod \mathfrak { m } _ { Q }$ .  
• $\begin{array} { r } { f _ { 0 } = 1 + \sum _ { p } c _ { p } z ^ { p } } \end{array}$ for $c _ { p } \in \mathbb { k }$ , $r ( p ) \neq 0$ a positive multiple of $m _ { 0 }$ .  
• For any $k > 0$ , there are only a finite number of rays $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \in \mathfrak { D }$ with $f _ { \mathfrak { d } } \not \equiv 1$ mod ${ \mathfrak { m } } _ { Q } ^ { k }$ .

Definition 3.22. Given a loop $\gamma$ in $M _ { \mathbb { R } }$ around the origin, we define the path ordered product

$$
\widehat { \theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { D } } : \mathbb { K } [ Q ] } \to \widehat { \mathbb { k } [ Q ] }
$$

as follows.
For each $k > 0$ , let $\mathfrak { D } [ k ] \subset \mathfrak { D }$ be the subset of rays $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \in \mathfrak { D }$ with $f _ { \mathfrak { d } } \not \equiv 1$ mod ${ \mathfrak { m } } _ { Q } ^ { k }$ .
This set is finite.
For $\mathfrak { d } \in \mathfrak { D } | k |$ with $\gamma ( t _ { 0 } ) \in \mathfrak { d }$ , define

$$
\theta _ { \gamma , 0 } ^ { k } : \mathbb { k } [ Q ] / \mathfrak { m } _ { Q } ^ { k }  \mathbb { k } [ Q ] / \mathfrak { m } _ { Q } ^ { k }
$$

by

$$
\theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { d } } ^ { k } ( z ^ { q } ) = z ^ { q } f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ^ { \langle n _ { \mathfrak { d } } , r ( q ) \rangle }
$$

for $n _ { \mathfrak { d } } \in M ^ { * }$ primitive satisfying, with $m$ a non-zero tangent vector of $\mathfrak { d }$

$$
\langle n _ { \mathsf { 0 } } , m \rangle = 0 , \quad \langle n _ { \mathsf { 0 } } , \gamma ^ { \prime } ( t _ { 0 } ) \rangle < 0 .
$$

Then, if $\gamma$ crosses the rays $\mathfrak { o } _ { 1 } , \ldots , \mathfrak { o } _ { n }$ in order with $\mathfrak { D } | k | = \{ \mathfrak { d } _ { 1 } , \dots , \mathfrak { d } _ { n } \}$ , we can define

$$
\theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { k } = \theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { d } _ { n } } ^ { k } \circ \cdot \cdot \cdot \circ \theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { d } _ { 1 } } ^ { k } .
$$

We then define $\theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { D } }$ by taking the limit as $k  \infty$ .

The following is a slight generalisation of a result of Kontsevich and Soibelman which appeared in [KS06].

Theorem 3.23. Let $\mathfrak { D }$ be a scattering diagram in the sense of Definition 3.21. Then there is another scattering diagram Scatter $( { \mathfrak { D } } )$ containing $\mathfrak { D }$ such that Scatter $( { \mathfrak { D } } ) \backslash { \mathfrak { D } }$ consists only of outgoing rays and $\theta _ { \gamma , \mathrm { S c a t t e r } ( \mathfrak { D } ) }$ is the identity for $\gamma$ a loop around the origin.

For a proof of this theorem essentially as stated here, see [GPS09], Theorem 1.4. The result is unique if $\operatorname { S c a t t e r } ( { \mathfrak { D } } ) \setminus { \mathfrak { D } }$ has at most one ray in each possible direction; we shall assume $\mathrm { S c a t t e r } ( { \mathfrak { D } } )$ has been chosen to have this property.
This can always be done.

We apply this in the following situation.
We take $Q$ to be the monoid $P _ { \bar { \varphi } }$ which yields the Mumford degeneration associated to the data $( B , \Sigma )$ , $\varphi$ (recalling $B = M _ { \mathbb { R } }$ ), defined by

$$
P _ { \bar { \varphi } } = \{ ( m , \bar { \varphi } ( m ) + p ) | m \in M , p \in P \} \subset M \times P ^ { \mathrm { g p } } .
$$

This comes with a canonical map $r : { \cal P } _ { \bar { \varphi } } \to M$ by projection.

Definition 3.24. Suppose we are in the situation of Assumptions 3.20. We define a scattering diagram $\nu ( \mathfrak { D } )$ on $B$ as follows.
For every ray $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \in \mathfrak { D }$ not equal to $( \rho _ { i } , f _ { \rho _ { i } } )$ for some $i$ , $\nu ( \mathfrak { D } )$ contains the ray $\left( \nu ( \mathfrak { d } ) , \tilde { \nu } _ { \tau _ { 0 } } ( f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \right)$ , and for each ray $( \rho _ { i } , f _ { \rho _ { i } } )$ , $\nu ( \mathfrak { D } )$ contains two rays, $\left( \bar { \rho } _ { i } , \tilde { \nu } _ { \tau _ { 0 } } ( g _ { \rho _ { i } } ) \right)$ and $( \bar { \rho } _ { i } , \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } ^ { - 1 } \bar { X } _ { i } ) )$ .

We note that $\nu ( \mathfrak { D } )$ may not actually be a scattering diagram in the sense of Definition 3.21, as it is possible that $f _ { \mathfrak { d } } \notin \widehat { \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ] }$ : if $p \in P _ { \varphi \tau }$ , then $\tilde { \nu } _ { \tau } ( p ) \in P _ { \overline { { \varphi } } _ { \tau } }$ but need not lie in $P _ { \overline { { \varphi } } }$ .

In the case of $\mathfrak { D } = \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ , we can use the Kontsevich-Soibelman lemma to describe $\nu ( { \mathfrak { D } } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } )$ .
This will both show that $\nu ( { \mathfrak { D } } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } )$ is a scattering diagram in the sense of Definition 3.21 and that it satisfies an important additional property, namely the condition that $\theta _ { \gamma , \nu ( \mathfrak { T } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } ) }$ is equal to the identity.
This will allow us to prove consistency.
Let

$$
\bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { 0 } = \{ \bigl ( \bar { \rho } _ { i } , \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } ^ { - 1 } \bar { X } _ { i } ) \bigr ) | 1 \leq i \leq n \} .
$$

Let $\mathfrak { m } _ { \bar { \varphi } } = P _ { \bar { \varphi } } \setminus P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ^ { \times }$ as usual.
Then by the strict convexity of $\varphi$ , $X _ { i } \in { \mathfrak { m } } _ { \bar { \varphi } }$ so that $\mathfrak { D } _ { 0 }$ is a scattering diagram for the pair $P _ { \bar { \varphi } }$ , $r$ in the sense of Definition 3.21. Now define

$$
\bar { \mathfrak { D } } : = \mathrm { S c a t t e r } ( \bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { 0 } )
$$

where we require $\mathfrak { D } \ \backslash \ \mathfrak { D } _ { 0 }$ to have only one outgoing ray in each direction (and no incoming rays).

The following will be Step V, which we defer until §3.4.

Theorem 3.25. ${ \mathfrak { D } = \nu ( { \mathfrak { D } } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } ) }$ .
In particular, $\nu ( { \mathfrak { D } } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } )$ is a scattering diagram in the sense of Definition 3.21 and $\theta _ { \gamma , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } } \equiv 1$ for a loop $\gamma$ around the origin.

Example 3.26. Continuing with Example 3.7, note that the pair $( Y , D )$ can be obtained from the toric pair $( Y , D )$ defined by the fan $\Sigma$ with rays generated by $( 1 , 0 )$ , $( 1 , 1 )$ , $( 0 , 1 )$ , $( - 1 , 0 )$ and $( 0 , - 1 )$ , corresponding to $D _ { 1 } , \ldots , D _ { 5 }$ , by blowing up one point on each of $D _ { 4 }$ and $D _ { 5 }$ .
This description determines $\mathfrak { D } _ { 0 }$ and hence $\mathfrak { D }$ .
One can check this description agrees with that given in Example 3.7 for $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ , see e.g. [GPS09], Example 1.6 for a similar computation.

Returning to the situation of Assumptions 3.20, suppose in addition that $\nu ( \mathfrak { D } )$ is a scattering diagram in the sense of Definition 3.21. (For example, by Theorem 3.25, $\mathfrak { D } = \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ satisfies these assumptions.) For $I \subset P$ an ideal with $\sqrt { I } = J$ , we now have deformations $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ and $X _ { I , \nu ( \mathfrak { D } ) } ^ { o }$ .
The latter scheme is glued from open sets

$$
\overline { { U } } _ { \bar { \rho } , I } = \operatorname { S p e c } \overline { { R } } _ { \bar { \rho } , I }
$$

along open sets identified with $\mathrm { S p e c } \overline { { R } } _ { \bar { \sigma } , I }$ .
Here we are decorating the rings coming from the data on $B$ with bars as before, while we maintain the notation $R _ { \rho , I }$ , etc., for those rings coming from the data on $B$ .

Lemma 3.27. Given Assumptions 3.20, assume also that $\nu ( \mathfrak { D } )$ is a scattering diagram in the sense of Definition 3.21. Then there are isomorphisms

$$
p _ { i } : R _ { \rho _ { i } , I } \to \overline { { R } } _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } , I }
$$

and

$$
p _ { i - 1 , i } : R _ { \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , I }  \overline { { R } } _ { \bar { \sigma } _ { i - 1 , i } , I }
$$

for all i such that the diagrams

$$
\begin{array} { l l } { { R _ { \rho _ { i } , I } \xrightarrow { \psi _ { \rho _ { i } , - } } R _ { \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , I } \qquad } } & { { R _ { \rho _ { i } , I } \xrightarrow { \psi _ { \rho _ { i } , + } } R _ { \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } } } \\ { { p _ { i } \bigg \downarrow } } & { { \qquad \downarrow \atop { \overline { { { R } } } _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } , I } } } } \\ { { \overline { { { R } } } _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } , I } \xrightarrow { \quad \quad } \overline { { { R } } } _ { \bar { \sigma } _ { i - 1 , i } , I } \qquad } } & { { \overline { { { R } } } _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } , I } \xrightarrow [ \quad \quad ] { p _ { i } } \overline { { { \psi } } } _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } , + 1 } } } \end{array} \bigg \downarrow _ { \bar { \sigma } _ { i , i + 1 } , I }
$$

and

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \quad R _ { \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , I } \xrightarrow [ ] { \theta _ { \gamma , \mathcal { D } } } \ R _ { \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , I } } \\ & { \quad \xrightarrow [ ] { p _ { i - 1 , i } } \Big \downarrow } \\ & { \quad \overline { { R } } _ { \bar { \sigma } _ { i - 1 , i } , I } \xrightarrow [ ] { \theta _ { \gamma , \nu ( \mathfrak { D } ) } } \overline { { R } } _ { \bar { \sigma } _ { i - 1 , i } , I } } \end{array}
$$

are commutative, where $\gamma$ is any path in $\sigma _ { i - 1 , i }$ for which $\theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { D } }$ is defined, and $\bar { \gamma } = \nu \circ \gamma$ .
Consequently, the maps $p _ { i }$ and $p _ { i - 1 , i }$ induce an isomorphism

$$
p : X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o } \to \bar { X } _ { I , \nu ( \mathfrak { D } ) } ^ { o }
$$

over Spec $\mathbb { k } [ P ] / I$ .

Proof.
Recall that

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { R _ { \rho , I } = \frac { R _ { I } [ X _ { i - 1 } , X _ { i } ^ { \pm } , X _ { i + 1 } ] } { ( X _ { i - 1 } X _ { i + 1 } - z ^ { \eta ( [ D _ { i } ] ) } X _ { i } ^ { - D _ { i } ^ { 2 } } g _ { \rho _ { i } } \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } X _ { i } ^ { - 1 } ) ) } , } \\ & { \overline { { R } } _ { \rho , I } = \frac { R _ { I } [ \bar { X } _ { i - 1 } , \bar { X } _ { i } ^ { \pm } , \bar { X } _ { i + 1 } ] } { ( \bar { X } _ { i - 1 } \bar { X } _ { i + 1 } - z ^ { \eta ( p ^ { * } [ \bar { D } _ { i } ] ) } \bar { X } _ { i } ^ { - \bar { D } _ { i } ^ { 2 } } \bar { g } _ { \rho _ { i } } \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } ^ { - 1 } \bar { X } _ { i } ) ) } . } \end{array}
$$

We simply define $p _ { i }$ to be the identity on $R _ { I }$ and $p _ { i } ( X _ { j } ) = X _ { j }$ .
This makes sense since $D _ { i } ^ { 2 } = D _ { i } ^ { 2 } - \ell _ { i }$ and $\begin{array} { r } { [ D _ { i } ] = p ^ { * } [ \bar { D } _ { i } ] - \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } E _ { i j } } \end{array}$ , so that

$$
\begin{array} { r c l } { { } } & { { } } & { { \mathrm {  ~ \gamma ~ } _ { i } \left( z ^ { \eta ( [ D _ { i } ] ) } X _ { i } ^ { - D _ { i } ^ { 2 } } \displaystyle \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } X _ { i } ^ { - 1 } ) \right) = z ^ { \eta ( p ^ { * } [ \bar { D } _ { i } ] ) } \bar { X } _ { i } ^ { - \bar { D } _ { i } ^ { 2 } } \left( \displaystyle \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } b _ { i j } ^ { - 1 } \bar { X } _ { i } \right) \left( \displaystyle \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } \bar { X } _ { i } ^ { - 1 } ) \right) } } \\ { { } } & { { } } & { { } } \\ { { } } & { { } } & { { = z ^ { \eta ( p ^ { * } [ \bar { D } _ { i } ] ) } \bar { X } _ { i } ^ { - \bar { D } _ { i } ^ { 2 } } \displaystyle \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } ^ { - 1 } \bar { X } _ { i } ) . } } \end{array}
$$

The map $p _ { i - 1 , i }$ is induced by $\tilde { \nu } _ { \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } }$ defined in (3.7).
It is then straightforward to check the commutativity of the three diagrams.


Lemma 3.28. Given Assumptions 3.20, suppose $\nu ( \mathfrak { D } )$ is a scattering diagram in the sense of Definition 3.21. For $Q \in \sigma _ { i - 1 , i }$ , we distinguish between

$$
\operatorname { L i f t } _ { Q } ( q ) \in R _ { \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , I }
$$

for the lift of $q \in B _ { 0 } ( \mathbb { Z } )$ and

$$
\mathrm { L i f t } _ { \nu ( Q ) } ( \nu ( q ) ) \in \bar { R } _ { \bar { \sigma } _ { i - 1 , i } , I }
$$

the lift of $\nu ( q )$ .
Then

(1) $p _ { i - 1 , i } ( \mathrm { L i f t } _ { Q } ( q ) ) = \mathrm { L i f t } _ { \nu ( Q ) } ( \nu ( q ) )$ .  
(2) Under the natural identifications $( P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \tau } } ) ^ { \mathrm { g p } } = ( P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ) ^ { \mathrm { g p } }$ , for $\tau \in \bar { \Sigma } \setminus \{ 0 \}$ , $P _ { \bar { \varphi } } \subset P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \tau } }$ , and for any broken line $\gamma$ for $q$ , $\mathrm { M o n o } ( \gamma ) \in \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ]$ .  
(3) $\nu$ induces a bijection between broken lines: If $\gamma : ( - \infty , 0 ]  B _ { 0 }$ is a broken line in $B _ { 0 }$ , then $\nu \circ \gamma$ is a broken line in ${ \bar { B } } _ { 0 }$ , and conversely, if $\bar { \gamma } : ( - \infty , 0 ]  \mathcal { P }$ is a broken line in $B _ { 0 }$ , then $\nu ^ { - 1 } \circ \bar { \gamma }$ is a broken line in $B _ { 0 }$ .

Proof.
(3) implies (1).
For (3), clearly it is enough to compare bending and attached monomials of broken lines near a ray $\rho _ { i }$ .

Consider a broken line $\gamma$ in $B _ { 0 }$ passing from $\sigma _ { i - 1 , i }$ to $\sigma _ { i , i + 1 }$ , and let $c { z } ^ { q }$ be the monomial attached to the broken line before it crosses over $\rho _ { i }$ , so that $q \in \boldsymbol { P } _ { \varphi _ { \sigma _ { i - 1 , i } } }$ .
Let $\theta _ { \rho _ { i } }$ , $\theta _ { \hat { \rho } _ { i } }$ be defined by

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { \theta _ { \rho _ { i } } ( z ^ { p } ) : = z ^ { p } f _ { \rho _ { i } } ^ { \langle n , r ( p ) \rangle } } } \\ { { \bar { \theta } _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } } ( z ^ { p } ) : = z ^ { p } \left( \bar { g } _ { \rho _ { i } } \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } ^ { - 1 } \bar { X } _ { i } ) \right) ^ { \langle \bar { n } , \bar { r } ( p ) \rangle } } } \end{array}
$$

where $( \bar { \rho } _ { i } , \bar { g } _ { \rho _ { i } } ) \in \nu ( \mathfrak { D } )$ is the outgoing ray with support $\rho _ { i }$ .
Here $n , n$ are primitive cotangent vectors vanishing on tangent vectors to $\rho _ { i } , \rho _ { i }$ and positive on $\sigma _ { i - 1 , i } , \sigma _ { i - 1 , i }$ respectively.
Then we need to show that

$$
p _ { i , i + 1 } ( \theta _ { \rho _ { i } } ( c z ^ { q } ) ) = \bar { \theta } _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } } ( p _ { i - 1 , i } ( c z ^ { q } ) )
$$

to get the correspondence between broken lines.

Note that

$$
p _ { i - 1 , i } ( X _ { i - 1 } ) = \bar { X } _ { i - 1 } , \quad p _ { i , i - 1 } ( X _ { i } ) = \bar { X } _ { i } , \quad p _ { i , i + 1 } ( X _ { i } ) = \bar { X } _ { i } ,
$$

but to compute $p _ { i , i + 1 } ( X _ { i - 1 } )$ , we need to use the relation (see Proposition 2.5)

$$
X _ { i - 1 } X _ { i + 1 } = z ^ { \eta ( [ D _ { i } ] ) } X _ { i } ^ { - D _ { i } ^ { 2 } }
$$

in $\mathbb { k } [ P _ { \varphi _ { \rho _ { i } } } ]$ to write

$$
X _ { i - 1 } = z ^ { \eta ( [ D _ { i } ] ) } X _ { i } ^ { - D _ { i } ^ { 2 } } X _ { i + 1 } ^ { - 1 } .
$$

On the other hand, one has the relation

$$
\bar { X } _ { i - 1 } \bar { X } _ { i + 1 } = z ^ { \eta ( p ^ { * } [ \bar { D } _ { i } ] ) } \bar { X } _ { i } ^ { - \bar { D } _ { i } ^ { 2 } }
$$

in $\mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } } } ]$ , so

$$
\begin{array} { r c l } { { } } & { { } } & { { p _ { i , i + 1 } ( X _ { i - 1 } ) = z ^ { \eta ( [ D _ { i } ] - p ^ { * } [ \bar { D } _ { i } ] ) } \bar { X } _ { i } ^ { - D _ { i } ^ { 2 } + \bar { D } _ { i } ^ { 2 } } \bar { X } _ { i - 1 } } } \\ { { } } & { { } } & { { } } \\ { { } } & { { } } & { { = \bar { X } _ { i } ^ { \ell _ { i } } \bar { X } _ { i - 1 } \displaystyle \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } b _ { i j } ^ { - 1 } . } } \end{array}
$$

Thus using Assumptions 3.20 for the form of $f _ { \rho _ { i } }$ , we have

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { \displaystyle p _ { i , i + 1 } \big ( \theta _ { \rho _ { i } } \big ( X _ { i - 1 } \big ) \big ) = p _ { i , i + 1 } \big ( X _ { i - 1 } f _ { \rho _ { i } } \big ) } & { } \\ { \displaystyle } & { = \bar { X } _ { i } ^ { \ell _ { i } } \bar { X } _ { i - 1 } \left( \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } b _ { i j } ^ { - 1 } \right) \left( \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } \bar { X } _ { i } ^ { - 1 } ) \right) \bar { g } _ { \rho _ { i } } } \\ { \displaystyle } & { = \bar { X } _ { i - 1 } \bar { g } _ { \rho _ { i } } \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } ^ { - 1 } \bar { X } _ { i } ) } \\ { \displaystyle } & { = \bar { \theta } _ { \bar { \theta } _ { i } } ( p _ { i - 1 , i } ( X _ { i - 1 } ) ) } \end{array}
$$

as desired.
Also,

$$
p _ { i , i + 1 } ( \theta _ { \rho _ { i } } ( X _ { i } ) ) = \bar { X } _ { i } = \bar { \theta } _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } } ( p _ { i - 1 , i } ( \bar { X } _ { i } ) ) .
$$

Thus (3.11) holds.
This shows (3).

For (2), the statement that $P _ { \bar { \varphi } } \subset P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \tau } }$ is obvious.
For $q \in \sigma \in \Sigma$ , by definition the monomial attached to the first domain of linearity of a broken line for $q$ is $z ^ { \varphi _ { \sigma } ( q ) }$ , which is identified under $\tilde { \nu } _ { \sigma }$ with $z ^ { ( \nu ( q ) , \bar { \varphi } ( \nu ( q ) ) ) } \in \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ]$ .
For any $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \in \nu ( \mathfrak { D } )$ , $f _ { \mathfrak { d } } \in \widehat { \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ] }$ by assumption, and hence all monomials associated to broken lines in $B _ { 0 }$ lie in $\mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ]$ , hence (2).


Definition 3.29. Let $\mathfrak { D }$ be a scattering diagram in the sense of Definition 3.21 for the pair $P , r : P \to M$ for some toric monoid $P$ .
Let $I \subset P$ be an ideal with $\sqrt { I } = \mathfrak { m } _ { P }$ .
We define for $q \in B _ { 0 } ( \mathbb { Z } )$ and $Q \in B _ { 0 }$ ,

$$
\operatorname { L i f t } _ { Q } ( q ) = \sum \operatorname { M o n o } ( \gamma ) \in \mathbb { k } [ P ] / I
$$

where the sum is over all broken lines $\gamma$ for $q$ with endpoint $Q$ in $B _ { 0 }$ with respect to the scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D }$ .
One sees easily as in Lemma 2.25 that this is a finite sum.

The last crucial result we need for consistency is the following result of [CPS].

Theorem 3.30. With the assumptions of Definition 3.29, suppose furthermore that $\theta _ { \gamma , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } } \equiv 1$ for a loop $\gamma$ around the origin.
Fix an ideal $I \subset P$ with $\sqrt { I } = { \mathfrak { m } } _ { P }$ and $q \in \bar { B } _ { 0 } ( \mathbb { Z } )$ .
If $Q , Q ^ { \prime } \in M _ { \mathbb { R } } \ \backslash \ \mathrm { S u p p } ( \bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { I } )$ are general, and $\gamma$ is a path connecting $Q$ and $Q ^ { \prime }$ for which $\theta _ { \gamma , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { I } }$ is defined, then

$$
\mathrm { L i f t } _ { Q ^ { \prime } } ( q ) = \theta _ { \gamma , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { I } } ( \mathrm { L i f t } _ { Q } ( q ) )
$$

as elements of $\mathbb { k } [ P ] / I$ .

Proof.
This is shown in [CPS] in a rather more general setup.
For a version of the argument closer to the current setup, see the proof of Theorem 5.35 of [G11].
$\boxed { \begin{array} { r l } \end{array} }$

Proof of Theorem 3.8. By Lemmas 3.15 and 3.19, we can assume we are in the situation of Assumptions 3.20 with ${ \mathfrak { D } } = { \mathfrak { D } } ^ { \operatorname { c a n } }$ .
In checking (1) of Definition 2.26 for $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ in this situation, we want to check equalities

$$
\mathrm { L i f t } _ { Q ^ { \prime } } ( q ) = \theta _ { \gamma , \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } } ( \mathrm { L i f t } _ { Q } ( q ) )
$$

for $Q , Q ^ { \prime } \in \sigma _ { i - 1 , i }$ .
By Lemmas 3.27 and 3.28, it is sufficient to show that

$$
\mathrm { L i f t } _ { \nu ( Q ^ { \prime } ) } ( \nu ( q ) ) = \theta _ { \bar { \gamma } , \nu ( \mathfrak { D } ) } ( \mathrm { L i f t } _ { \nu ( Q ) } ( \nu ( q ) ) ) .
$$

To check this equality we can compare coefficients of monomials, and given any monomial $z ^ { p }$ appearing on the left- or right-hand sides, we can apply Theorems 3.25 and 3.30, where we take $P = P _ { \bar { \varphi } }$ , $I = { \mathfrak { m } } _ { P _ { \bar { \varphi } } } ^ { k }$ for sufficiently large $k$ so that $p \notin I$ .
The hypothesis $\theta _ { \gamma , \nu ( \mathfrak { D } ) } \equiv 1$ of Theorem 3.30 holds by Theorem 3.25.

To show (2) of Definition 2.26, we can take $Q = Q _ { - }$ and $Q ^ { \prime } = Q _ { + }$ on opposite sides of a ray $\rho _ { i }$ .
If $\gamma$ is a short path joining $Q$ and $Q ^ { \prime }$ , we still have (3.12) after inverting $f _ { i }$ .
We have a map

$$
\psi : = ( \psi _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } , - } , \psi _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } , + } ) : \overline { { { R } } } _ { \overline { { { \rho } } } _ { i } , I }  \overline { { { R } } } _ { \overline { { { \sigma } } } _ { i - 1 , i } , I } \times \overline { { { R } } } _ { \overline { { { \sigma } } } _ { i , i + 1 } , I } .
$$

If $\begin{array} { r } { f _ { i } = \bar { g } _ { \rho _ { i } } \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } ^ { - 1 } \bar { X } _ { i } ) } \end{array}$ then $\psi$ is given by

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \bar { X } _ { i - 1 } \mapsto \big ( z ^ { \overline { { \varphi } } _ { \overline { { \rho } } _ { i } } ( v _ { i - 1 } ) } , f _ { i } z ^ { \overline { { \varphi } } _ { \overline { { \rho } } _ { i } } ( v _ { i - 1 } ) } \big ) , } \\ & { \quad \bar { X } _ { i } \mapsto \big ( z ^ { \overline { { \varphi } } _ { \overline { { \rho } } _ { i } } ( v _ { i } ) } , z ^ { \overline { { \varphi } } _ { \overline { { \rho } } _ { i } } ( v _ { i } ) } \big ) , } \\ & { \quad \bar { X } _ { i + 1 } \mapsto \big ( f _ { i } z ^ { \overline { { \varphi } } _ { \overline { { \rho } } _ { i } } ( v _ { i + 1 } ) } , z ^ { \overline { { \varphi } } _ { \overline { { \rho } } _ { i } } ( v _ { i + 1 } ) } \big ) . } \end{array}
$$

One checks easily that this map is injective.
Furthermore, the image is described as follows.
Let $I _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } } \subset P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } } }$ be the monoid ideal

$$
I _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } } = \{ q \in P _ { \overline { { \rho } } _ { i } } | q - \varphi _ { \overline { { \sigma } } _ { i - 1 , i } } ( r ( q ) ) \in I \mathrm { ~ o r ~ } q - \varphi _ { \overline { { \sigma } } _ { i , i + 1 } } ( r ( q ) ) \in I \} .
$$

Then the image consists of those elements $( g _ { - } , g _ { + } )$ such that every monomial of $g _ { - }$ and $g _ { + }$ has exponent in $P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } } } \subset P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \overline { { \sigma } } _ { i - 1 , i } } , } P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \overline { { \sigma } } _ { i , i + 1 } } }$ , and the images $g _ { \pm }$ of $g \pm$ in $( \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \overline { { \rho } } _ { i } } ] / I _ { \bar { \rho } _ { i } } ) _ { f _ { i } }$ satisfy $\theta _ { \gamma , \nu ( \mathfrak { D } ) } ( \bar { g } _ { - } ) = \bar { g } _ { + }$ , where this makes sense as we have localized at $f _ { i }$ .
(See e.g., the proof of Lemma 2.34 in [GS07] for a similar statement.) Thus by (3.12) and Lemma 2.25, (2), there is an $\alpha \in \overline { { R } } _ { \overline { { \rho } } _ { i } , I }$ such that

$$
\psi _ { \overline { { { \rho } } } _ { i } , - } ( \alpha ) = \mathrm { L i f t } _ { \nu ( Q ) } ( \nu ( q ) ) , \quad \psi _ { \overline { { { \rho } } } _ { i } , + } ( \alpha ) = \mathrm { L i f t } _ { \nu ( Q ^ { \prime } ) } ( \nu ( q ) ) .
$$

Thus we may take $\mathrm { L i f t } _ { \rho _ { i } } ( q ) = p _ { i } ^ { - 1 } ( \alpha )$ , and by Lemma 3.28, $\psi _ { \rho _ { i } , \pm } ( \mathrm { L i f t } _ { \rho _ { i } } ( q ) ) = \mathrm { L i f t } _ { Q _ { \pm } } ( q )$ , giving consistency.


3.4. Step V: The proof of Theorem 3.25 and the connection with [GPS09].
Here we derive Theorem 3.25 from the main result of [GPS09].
We will need to review one form of this result, which gives an enumerative interpretation for the output of the Kontsevich-Soibelman lemma.

Fix $M \cong \mathbb { Z } ^ { 2 }$ as usual.
Suppose we are given positive integers $\ell _ { 1 } , \ldots , \ell _ { n }$ and primitive vectors $m _ { 1 } , \dots , m _ { n } \in M$ .
Let $\ell = \textstyle \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { n } \ell _ { i }$ and $Q = M \oplus \mathbb { N } ^ { \ell }$ , with $r : Q \to M$ the projection.
Denote the variables in $\mathbb { k } \lfloor Q \rfloor$ corresponding to the generators of $\mathbb { N } ^ { \ell }$ as $t _ { i j }$ , for $1 \leq i \leq n$ and $1 \le j \le \ell _ { i }$ .
Consider the scattering diagram for the data $r : Q \to M$ (in the sense of Definition 3.21)

$$
\mathfrak { D } = \{ ( \mathbb { R } _ { \ge 0 } m _ { i } , \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + t _ { i j } z ^ { m _ { i } } ) ) | 1 \le i \le n \} .
$$

We wish to interpret $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \in \mathrm { S c a t t e r } ( \mathfrak { D } ) \backslash \mathfrak { D }$ .
Choose a complete fan $\Sigma _ { 0 }$ in $M _ { \mathbb { R } }$ which contains the rays $\mathbb { R } _ { \ge 0 } m _ { 1 } , \ldots , \mathbb { R } _ { \ge 0 } m _ { n }$ as well as the ray $\mathfrak { o }$ (which may coincide with one of the other rays).
Let $X _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ be the corresponding toric surface, and let $D _ { 1 } , \ldots , D _ { n } , D _ { \mathrm { o u t } }$ be the divisors corresponding to the above rays.
Choose general points $x _ { i 1 } , \ldots , x _ { i \ell _ { i } } \in$ $D _ { i }$ , and let

$$
\nu : \tilde { X } _ { \mathfrak { d } } \to X _ { \mathfrak { d } }
$$

be the blow-up of all the points $\{ x _ { i j } \}$ .
Let $\dot { D } _ { 1 } , \dots , \dot { D } _ { n } , \dot { D } _ { \mathrm { o u t } }$ be the proper transforms of the divisors $D _ { 1 } , \ldots , D _ { n } , D _ { \mathrm { o u t } }$ and $E _ { i j }$ the exceptional curve over $x _ { i j }$ .

Now introduce the additional data of $\mathbf { P } = ( \mathbf { P } _ { 1 } , \ldots , \mathbf { P } _ { n } )$ , where $\mathbf { P } _ { i }$ denotes a sequence $p _ { i 1 } , \ldots , p _ { i \ell _ { i } }$ o f $\ell _ { i }$ non-negative numbers.
We will use the notation ${ \bf P } _ { i } = p _ { i 1 } + \cdots + p _ { i \ell _ { i } }$ and call ${ \bf P } _ { i }$ an ordered partition.
Define

$$
| \mathbf { P } _ { i } | = \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } p _ { i j } .
$$

We shall restrict attention to those $\mathbf { P }$ such that

$$
- \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { n } | { \bf P } _ { i } | m _ { i } = k _ { \bf P } m _ { \bar { \imath } }
$$

where $m _ { \mathfrak { d } } \in M$ is a primitive generator of $\mathfrak { o }$ and $k _ { \mathbf { P } }$ is a positive integer.

Given this data, consider the class $\beta \in A _ { 1 } ( X _ { 0 } , \mathbb { Z } )$ specified by the requirement that, if $\boldsymbol { D }$ is a toric divisor of $X _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ with $D \notin \{ D _ { 1 } , \ldots , D _ { n } , D _ { \mathrm { o u t } } \}$ , then $D \cdot \beta = 0$ ; if $D _ { \mathrm { o u t } } \ \notin$ $\{ D _ { 1 } , \ldots , D _ { n } \}$ ,

$$
D _ { i } \cdot \beta = | \mathbf { P } _ { i } | , \qquad D _ { \mathrm { o u t } } \cdot \beta = k _ { \mathbf { P } } ;
$$

while if $\boldsymbol { D _ { \mathrm { o u t } } } = \boldsymbol { D _ { j } }$ for some $j$ , then

$$
D _ { i } \cdot \beta = { \left\{ \begin{array} { l l } { | \mathbf { P } _ { i } | } & { i \neq j , } \\ { | \mathbf { P } _ { i } | + k _ { \mathbf { P } } } & { i = j . } \end{array} \right. }
$$

That such a class exists follows easily from (3.13) and the first part of Lemma 3.32 below.
It is also unique.
We can then define

$$
\beta _ { \mathbf { P } } = \nu ^ { * } ( \beta ) - \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } \sum _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } p _ { i j } [ E _ { i j } ] \in A _ { 1 } ( \tilde { X } _ { \mathfrak { d } } , \mathbb { Z } ) .
$$

We define $N _ { \mathbf { P } } : = N _ { \beta _ { \mathbf { P } } }$ as in Definition 3.1, using $( \tilde { Y } , \tilde { D } ) = ( \tilde { X } _ { 0 } , \tilde { D } )$ , where $\ddot { D }$ is the proper transform of the toric boundary of $X _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ , and using $C = \tilde { D } _ { \mathrm { o u t } }$ .
Then one of the main theorems of [GPS09] (see §5.7 of that paper) states

# Theorem 3.31.

$$
\log f _ { 0 } = \sum _ { \mathbf { P } } k _ { \mathbf { P } } N _ { \mathbf { P } } t ^ { \mathbf { P } } z ^ { - k _ { \mathbf { P } } m _ { 0 } } ,
$$

where the sum is over all $\mathbf { P }$ satisfying (3.13) and $t ^ { \mathbf { P } }$ denotes the monomial $\Pi _ { i j } t _ { i j } ^ { p _ { i j } }$

We can adapt this theorem for our purposes as follows.
Fix a fan $\Sigma$ in $M _ { \mathbb { R } }$ defining a complete non-singular toric surface $Y$ , with $D = D _ { 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + D _ { n }$ the toric boundary.
Choose points $x _ { i 1 } , \ldots , x _ { i \ell _ { i } } \in D _ { i }$ , and define a new surface $Y$ as the blow-up $\nu : Y \to Y$ at the points $\{ x _ { i j } \}$ .
Let $E _ { i j }$ be the exceptional curve over $x _ { i j }$ .

Let $P = \mathrm { { N E } } ( Y )$ ; because $Y$ is toric, this is a finitely generated monoid with $P ^ { \times } =$ $\{ 0 \}$ .
Let $\bar { \varphi } : M _ { \mathbb { R } } \to P _ { \mathbb { R } } ^ { \mathrm { g p } }$ be the $\Sigma$ -piecewise linear strictly $P$ -convex function given by Lemma 1.13.

We will need the following, an immediate corollary of Lemma 1.13, using the notation of that lemma applied to the fan $\Sigma$ for $Y$ :

Lemma 3.32. If $\textstyle \sum _ { \rho } a _ { \rho } t _ { \rho } \in \ker s$ , then the corresponding element of ker $s = A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { Z } )$ is

$$
\sum _ { \rho } a _ { \rho } \bar { \varphi } ( m _ { \rho } ) \in { \cal P } ^ { \mathrm { g p } } .
$$

Let $E \subset A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { Z } )$ be the lattice spanned by the classes of the exceptional curves of $\nu$ , so that $A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { Z } ) = \nu ^ { * } A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { Z } ) \oplus E$ .
We then obtain a map

$$
\varphi = \nu ^ { * } \circ \bar { \varphi } : M _ { \mathbb { R } } \to \nu ^ { * } P ^ { \mathrm { g p } } \oplus E .
$$

Let

There is an obvious projection $r : Q \to M$ , and by strict convexity of $\varphi$ , $Q ^ { \times } = E$ .
We consider the scattering diagram, $\mathfrak { D } _ { 0 }$ , over $\mathbb { k } [ Q ]$ given by

$$
\bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { 0 } = \{ ( \mathbb { R } _ { \ge 0 } m _ { i } , \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + z ^ { ( m _ { i } , \varphi ( m _ { i } ) - E _ { i j } ) } ) ) | 1 \le i \le n \} .
$$

Then we have

Theorem 3.33. Let $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { 0 } ) \in \mathrm { S c a t t e r } ( \mathfrak { D } _ { 0 } ) \ \backslash \ \mathfrak { D } _ { 0 }$ , assuming that there is at most one ray of $\mathrm { S c a t t e r } ( \bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { 0 } ) \ \backslash \ \bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { 0 }$ in each possible outgoing direction.
(Note by definition of Scatter $\textcircled{3} _ { 0 } )$ , $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } )$ cannot be incoming.) Then, following the notation of Definition 3.1 and 3.3,

$$
\log f _ { \mathfrak { d } } = \sum _ { \beta } k _ { \beta } N _ { \beta } z ^ { ( - k _ { \beta } m _ { 0 } , \pi _ { * } ( \beta ) - \varphi ( k _ { \beta } m _ { 0 } ) ) } .
$$

Here $\pi : \tilde { Y }  Y$ is the toric blow-up of $Y$ determined by $\mathfrak { d }$ and $C \subset { \tilde { Y } }$ is the component of the boundary determined by $\mathfrak { o }$ .
If $\mathfrak { d }$ is not one of the rays $\mathbb { R } _ { \geq 0 } m _ { i }$ , then we sum over all $\mathbb { A } ^ { 1 }$ -classes $\beta \in A _ { 1 } ( \tilde { Y } , \mathbb { Z } )$ satisfying (3.1), and if ${ \mathfrak { d } } = \mathbb { R } _ { \geq 0 } m _ { i }$ we sum over all such classes except for classes given by multiple covers of one of the exceptional divisors $E _ { i j }$ .

Proof.
Let $Q ^ { \prime }$ be the submonoid of $M \oplus \mathbb { N } ^ { \ell }$ generated by elements of the form $( m _ { i } , d _ { i j } )$ , where $d _ { i j }$ is the $( i , j )$ -th generator of $\mathbb { N } ^ { \ell }$ .
Note that $Q ^ { \prime }$ itself is freely generated by these elements.
Thus we can define a map

$$
\alpha : Q ^ { \prime }  Q
$$

by $( m _ { i } , d _ { i j } ) \mapsto ( m _ { i } , \varphi ( m _ { i } ) - E _ { i j } )$ .
The scattering diagram

$$
\mathfrak { D } ^ { \prime } : = \{ ( \mathbb { R } _ { \ge 0 } m _ { i } , \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + z ^ { ( m _ { i } , d _ { i j } ) } ) ) | 1 \le i \le n \}
$$

then has image under the map $\alpha$ (applying $\alpha$ to each $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ ) the scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D } _ { 0 }$ .
Thus if we apply $\alpha$ to each element of $\mathrm { S c a t t e r } ( { \mathfrak { D } } ^ { \prime } )$ , we must get $\mathrm { S c a t t e r } ( \mathfrak { D } _ { 0 } )$ , as $\theta _ { \gamma , \mathrm { S c a t t e r } ( \mathfrak { D } ^ { \prime } ) }$ being the identity on $\widehat { \mathbb { k } [ Q ^ { \prime } ] }$ implies that $\theta _ { \gamma , \alpha ( \mathrm { S c a t t e r } ( \mathfrak { D } ^ { \prime } ) ) }$ is the identity on $\widehat { \mathbb { k } [ Q ] }$ .

To obtain the result, we now note that the set of possible $\mathbb { A } ^ { 1 }$ -classes in $\dot { Y }$ occurring in the expression (3.15) are precisely the classes $\{ \beta _ { \mathbf { P } } \}$ where $\mathbf { P }$ runs over all partitions satisfying (3.13).
Now applying $\alpha$ to a term appearing in (3.14) of the form

$$
k _ { \mathbf { P } } N _ { \mathbf { P } } t ^ { \mathbf { P } } z ^ { - k _ { \mathbf { P } } m _ { 0 } } = k _ { \beta _ { \mathbf { P } } } N _ { \beta _ { \mathbf { P } } } \left( \prod t _ { i j } ^ { p _ { i j } } \right) z ^ { \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } | \mathbf { P } _ { i } | m _ { i } } ,
$$

we get

$$
k _ { \beta _ { \mathbf { P } } } N _ { \beta _ { \mathbf { P } } } z ^ { ( - k _ { \mathbf { P } } m _ { 0 } , \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } | \mathbf { P } _ { i } | \varphi ( m _ { i } ) - \sum _ { i , j } p _ { i j } E _ { i j } ) } .
$$

But by Lemma 3.32 and (3.13),

$$
\sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { p } | \mathbf { P } _ { i } | \varphi ( m _ { i } ) - \sum _ { i , j } p _ { i j } E _ { i j } = \pi _ { * } ( \beta _ { \mathbf { P } } ) - \varphi ( k _ { \mathbf { P } } m _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) ,
$$

hence the result.

A direct comparison of the formula of the above theorem and the formula in the definition of the canonical scattering diagram then yields Theorem 3.25.

4. Smoothness: Around the Gross–Siebert locus

Next we prove that our deformation of $\mathbb { V } _ { n }$ is indeed a smoothing.
The main theorem of this section (Theorem 4.6) will show this in the situation of Theorem 0.1 when $( Y , D )$ has a toric model.
The full smoothness statement of Theorem 0.1 will require some more work, which will be carried out in 6.

We prove smoothness by working over the Gross–Siebert locus (Definition 3.14). Here our deformation (when restricted to one-parameter subgroups associated to $p ^ { * } A$ , $A$ an ample divisor on $Y$ ) agrees with the construction of [GS07].
This is important here because the deformations of [GS07] come with explicit charts that cover all of $\mathbb { V } _ { n }$ , from which it is clear that they give a smoothing.
So conceptually, the smoothing claim is clear.
Because we work with formal families the actual argument is a bit more delicate.
First we make rigorous the notion of a smooth generic fibre for a formal family:

Definition-Lemma 4.1. Let $f : Z \to W$ be a flat finite type morphism of schemes of relative dimension $d$ .
Then $\operatorname { S i n g } ( f ) \subset Z$ is the closed embedding defined by the $d ^ { t h }$ Fitting ideal of $\Omega _ { Z / W } ^ { 1 }$ .
$\operatorname { S i n g } ( f )$ is empty if and only if $f$ is smooth.
Formation of $\operatorname { S i n g } ( f )$ commutes with all base extensions of $W$ .

Proof.
For the definition of the Fitting ideal, see e.g., [E95], 20.4. The fact that it commutes with base-change follows from the fact that $\Omega _ { Z / W } ^ { 1 }$ commutes with basechange and [E95], Cor.
20.5. That $\operatorname { S i n g } ( f )$ is empty if and only if $f$ is smooth follows from [E95], Prop.
20.6 and the definition of smoothness.


Now for a formal family, smoothness of the generic fibre is measured by the fact that $\operatorname { S i n g } ( f )$ does not surject scheme-theoretically onto the base.
More precisely:

Definition 4.2. Let $S$ be a normal variety, $V \subset S$ a connected closed subset, and $\mathfrak { S }$ the formal completion of $S$ along $V$ .
Let $\mathfrak { f } \colon { \mathfrak { X } }  { \mathfrak { S } }$ be an adic flat morphism of formal schemes of pure relative dimension and $3 \subset { \mathfrak { X } }$ the scheme theoretic singular locus of $\mathfrak { f }$ .
Then we say the generic fiber of $\mathfrak { f }$ is smooth if the map $\mathcal { O } _ { \mathfrak { S } } \to \mathfrak { f } _ { * } \mathcal { O } _ { 3 }$ is not injective.

For the statement of Proposition 4.3, we fix our usual setting of a surface $( Y , D )$ , and assume given Assumptions 3.20 and that $\nu ( \mathfrak { D } )$ is a scattering diagram in the sense of Definition 3.21. Suppose furthermore that $\theta _ { \gamma , \nu ( \mathfrak { D } ) } \equiv 1$ for a loop $\gamma$ around the origin.
Thus by Theorem 3.30, $\mathfrak { D }$ is consistent.
These hypotheses on $\mathfrak { D }$ apply in particular when ${ \mathfrak { D } } = { \mathfrak { D } } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ .

Let $T ^ { \mathrm { { \ u s } } }$ be the Gross–Siebert locus; we have $T ^ { \mathrm { g s } } = \operatorname { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P ] / G$ .
Consistency of $\mathfrak { D }$ gives a flat family

$$
f _ { I } : X _ { I } \to \operatorname { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P ] / I
$$

over a thickening of $\chi \mathrm { { s s } }$ whenever ${ \sqrt { I } } = G$ .

On the other hand, letting $\Sigma$ be the fan for $Y$ in $B = M _ { \mathbb { R } }$ , we have the piecewise linear function $\bar { \varphi } : B \to P _ { \mathbb { R } } ^ { \mathrm { g p } }$ with $\kappa _ { \bar { \rho } , \bar { \varphi } } = p ^ { * } [ D _ { \bar { \rho } } ]$ , as in (3.6).
This now determines the Mumford family

$$
{ \bar { f } } _ { I } : { \bar { X } } _ { I } \to \operatorname { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P ] / I .
$$

Our goal is to compare these two families.
Note that both $X _ { G }  T ^ { \mathrm { g s } }$ and $X _ { G }  T ^ { \mathrm { g s } }$ are the trivial family $\mathbb { V } _ { n } \times I ^ { \mathrm { { r g s } } } \to I ^ { \mathrm { { r g s } } }$ .
Thus either family contains a canonical copy of $T ^ { \mathrm { { \ u s } } }$ , i.e., $\{ 0 \} \times T ^ { \mathrm { g s } }$ , where $0$ is the vertex of $\mathbb { V } _ { n }$ .

Proposition 4.3. In the above situation, fix an ideal $I$ with ${ \sqrt { I } } = G$ .
There are open affine sets $U _ { I } \subset X _ { I }$ , $U _ { I } \subset X _ { I }$ , both sets containing the canonical copy of $T ^ { \mathrm { { \ u s } } }$ , and an isomorphism

$$
\mu _ { I } : U _ { I } \to \bar { U } _ { I }
$$

of families over Spec $\mathbb { k } [ P ] / I$ .

Moreover, there is a non-zero monomial $y \in \mathbb { k } | P |$ whose pullback to $X _ { I }$ is in the stalk at any point $x \in \{ 0 \} \times T ^ { \mathrm { g s } } \subset \mathbb { V } _ { n } \times T ^ { \mathrm { g s } }$ of the ideal of ${ \mathrm { S i n g } } _ { f _ { I } }$ for all $I$ .

Proof.
The generic fibre of the Mumford family over $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk | P |$ is smooth: indeed the family is trivial over the open torus orbit of $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P ]$ , with fibre an algebraic torus.
It follows that there is a non-zero monomial $y \in \Bbbk [ P ]$ in the ideal of $\operatorname { S i n g } _ { \bar { f } }$ for the global Mumford family

$$
\bar { f } : \mathrm { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ] \to \mathrm { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P ] .
$$

Of course its restriction then lies in the stalk at any point $x$ of the ideal sheaf of $\mathrm { S i n g } _ { \bar { f } _ { I } }$ for all $I$ .
Thus once we establish the claimed isomorphisms, the final statement follows.

Recall from §3.3 the construction of ${ \mathfrak { D } } : = \nu ( { \mathfrak { D } } )$ and the scheme $X _ { I , \hat { \mathfrak { D } } } ^ { o }$ from $\mathfrak { D }$ .
By Lemma 3.27, $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o } \cong X _ { I , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } } ^ { o }$ , so we in fact have an isomorphism

$$
X _ { I } \cong \operatorname { S p e c } \Gamma ( \bar { X } _ { I , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } } ^ { o } , \mathcal { O } _ { \bar { X } _ { I , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } } ^ { o } } ) = : \bar { X } _ { I , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } } .
$$

So we can work with $X _ { I , \hat { \mathfrak { D } } }$ instead of $X _ { I }$ .
On the other hand, the Mumford family $\operatorname { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ] / I \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ]$ over $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P ] / I$ can be described similarly.
Using the empty scattering diagram instead of the scattering diagram $\mathfrak { D }$ , one has by Lemma 2.9

$$
\bar { X } _ { I , \varnothing } = \operatorname { S p e c } \Gamma ( \bar { X } _ { I , \varnothing } ^ { o } , \mathcal { O } _ { \bar { X } _ { I , \varnothing } ^ { o } } ) .
$$

Now define an ideal $I _ { 0 } \subset P _ { \bar { \varphi } }$ as follows.
