For $\sigma \in \Sigma _ { \mathrm { m a x } }$ , let $\varphi _ { \sigma }$ denote the linear extension of $\bar { \varphi } | _ { \sigma }$ .
We set

$$
I _ { 0 } : = \{ ( m , p ) \in P _ { \bar { \varphi } } | p - \bar { \varphi } _ { \sigma } ( m ) \in I \mathrm { ~ f o r ~ s o m e ~ } \sigma \in \bar { \Sigma } _ { \operatorname* { m a x } } \} .
$$

Note that $\sqrt { I _ { 0 } } = \mathfrak { m } _ { P \bar { \varphi } }$ .
By assumption, $\mathfrak { D }$ is a scattering diagram for $P _ { \bar { \varphi } }$ , and hence there are only a finite number of $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \in \mathfrak { D }$ for which $f _ { \mathrm { { 0 } } } \not \equiv 1 \ \mathrm { m o d } \ I _ { 0 }$ .
Furthermore, modulo $I _ { 0 }$ , each $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ is a polynomial.

Let ${ \mathfrak { D } } _ { I }$ be the scattering diagram obtained from $\mathfrak { D }$ by, for each outgoing ray $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } )$ , truncating each $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ by throwing out all terms which lie in $I _ { 0 }$ .
The incoming rays remain unchanged.
Thus ${ \mathfrak { D } } _ { I }$ can be viewed as a finite scattering diagram.
Let

$$
h : = \prod _ { \mathfrak { d } \in \bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { I } } f _ { \mathfrak { d } } .
$$

This is an element of $\mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ]$ .
Note that necessarily $h \equiv 1$ mod ${ \mathfrak { m } } _ { P _ { \overline { { \varphi } } } }$ .
Thus $h \neq 0$ defines an open subset $U \subset \mathrm { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ] / G \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ] = X _ { G , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } } = X _ { G , \emptyset } = \mathbb { V } _ { n } \times T ^ { \mathrm { g s } }$ .
Furthermore,

$U$ contains the canonical copy of $T ^ { \mathrm { g s } }$ .
Since $X _ { I , \hat { \mathfrak { D } } }$ and $X _ { I , \emptyset }$ both have underlying topological space $X _ { G }$ , this defines open sets $U _ { I , \hat { \mathfrak { D } } }$ of $X _ { I , \hat { \mathfrak { D } } }$ and $U _ { I , \emptyset }$ of $X _ { I , \emptyset }$ .
We shall show these two open subschemes are isomorphic.

First the following claim shows that $X _ { I , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } } \cong X _ { I , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { I } }$ , as for any $\tau \in \Sigma \setminus \{ 0 \}$ , the automorphisms involved will have the same effect modulo $I _ { \tau }$ .
As a consequence, we can work with the scattering diagram ${ \mathfrak { D } } _ { I }$ .

Claim 4.4. Let $\tau \in \Sigma$ , and suppose $( m , p ) \in P _ { \bar { \varphi } }$ satisfies $- m \in \tau$ .
Then $( m , p ) \in I _ { 0 }$ if and only if $( m , p ) \in I _ { \tau }$ , where

$$
I _ { \tau } : = \{ ( m , p ) \in P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \tau } } | p - \bar { \varphi } _ { \sigma } ( m ) \in I \ f o r \ s o m e \ \sigma \in \bar { \Sigma } _ { \mathrm { m a x } } \ w i t h \ \tau \subset \sigma \}
$$

Proof of claim.
Clearly $I _ { \tau } \cap P _ { \overline { { \varphi } } } \subset I _ { 0 }$ , so one implication is clear.
Conversely, suppose that $( m , p ) \in I _ { 0 }$ , so that $p - \overline { { \varphi } } _ { \sigma } ( m ) \in I$ for some $\sigma \in \Sigma _ { \mathrm { m a x } }$ .
If $\tau \subset \sigma ^ { \prime } \in \Sigma _ { \mathrm { m a x } }$ , let $\rho _ { 1 } , \ldots , \rho _ { n }$ be the sequence of rays traversed in passing from $\sigma$ to $\sigma ^ { \prime }$ , chosen so that all $\rho _ { 1 } , \ldots , \rho _ { n }$ lie in a half-plane bounded by the line $\mathbb { R } m$ .
Then

$$
\overline { { { \varphi } } } _ { \sigma ^ { \prime } } ( m ) = \overline { { { \varphi } } } _ { \sigma } ( m ) + \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { n } \langle n _ { \rho _ { i } } , m \rangle \kappa _ { \rho _ { i } , \overline { { { \varphi } } } } ,
$$

with $n _ { \rho _ { i } }$ primitive, vanishing on $\rho _ { i }$ , and positive on $\rho _ { i + 1 }$ .
Note that since $- m \in \tau$ , we must have $\langle n _ { \rho _ { i } } , m \rangle \leq 0$ for each $i$ , and hence $p - \overline { { \varphi } } _ { \sigma ^ { \prime } } ( m ) = p - \overline { { \varphi } } _ { \sigma } ( m ) + p ^ { \prime }$ for some $p ^ { \prime } \in P$ .
Hence $( m , p ) \in I _ { \tau }$ .


To show that $U _ { I , \hat { \mathfrak { D } } }$ and $U _ { I , \emptyset }$ are isomorphic, let us describe these open subschemes explicitly away from the origin.
Recall that $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ is obtained by gluing together schemes which are spectra of rings $\overline { { R } } _ { \tau , I }$ for $\tau \in \Sigma$ .
However in the case that $\dim \tau = 1$ , this ring depends on the scattering diagram, so we write $\overline { { R } } _ { \tau , I , \mathfrak { D } }$ for $\mathfrak { D } = \mathfrak { D }$ or $\emptyset$ .

If $\dim \tau = 2$ , then $\overline { { R } } _ { \tau , I , \mathfrak { D } } = \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \tau } } ] / I \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \tau } } ]$ .
Since $h \in \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ] \subset \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \tau } } ]$ , $h$ defines an element of $\overline { { R } } _ { \tau , I , \mathfrak { D } }$ in this case.

If $\dim \tau = 1$ , then $\tau = \rho _ { i }$ for some $i$ , and we have a surjection

$$
\overline { { R } } _ { \rho _ { i } , I , \mathfrak { D } } \to \overline { { R } } _ { \rho _ { i } , G , \mathfrak { D } } = R _ { G } [ \bar { X } _ { i - 1 } , \bar { X } _ { i } ^ { \pm 1 } , \bar { X } _ { i + 1 } ] / ( \bar { X } _ { i - 1 } \bar { X } _ { i + 1 } ) \cong \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \rho _ { i } } } ] / C \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \rho _ { i } } } ] ,
$$

so that $h \in \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ] \subset \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \rho _ { i } } } ]$ defines an element of $\overline { { R } } _ { \rho _ { i } , G , \mathfrak { D } }$ .
Choosing any lift of $h$ t o $\overline { { R } } _ { \rho _ { i } , I , \mathfrak { D } }$ , we note the localization $( \overline { { R } } _ { \rho _ { i } , I , \mathfrak { D } } ) _ { h }$ is independent of the lift since the kernel of the above surjection is nilpotent.

We can then define regardless of $\mathrm { d i m } \tau$ ,

$$
S _ { \tau , I , \mathfrak { D } } : = ( \overline { { R } } _ { \tau , I , \mathfrak { D } } ) _ { h } .
$$

Note there is an isomorphism

$$
\psi _ { i } : S _ { \rho _ { i } , I , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } } \to S _ { \rho _ { i } , I , \emptyset }
$$

given by

$$
\bar { X } _ { i - 1 } \mapsto \bar { X } _ { i - 1 } \left( \bar { g } _ { \rho _ { i } } \prod _ { j = 1 } ^ { \ell _ { i } } ( 1 + b _ { i j } ^ { - 1 } \bar { X } _ { i } ) \right) , \quad \bar { X } _ { i } \mapsto \bar { X } _ { i } , \quad \bar { X } _ { i + 1 } \mapsto \bar { X } _ { i + 1 } .
$$

This has an inverse because of the localization at $h$ .

Given a path $\gamma$ in $M _ { \mathbb { R } } \ \backslash \ \{ 0 \}$ , note that by construction of $h$ , $\theta _ { \gamma , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { I } }$ makes sense as an automorphism of the localization $\mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } } ] _ { h }$ , since to define the automorphism associated with crossing a ray $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } )$ , we only need $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ to be invertible.
However since by construction $h$ is divisible by $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ , $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ is invertible.
In particular, $\theta _ { \gamma , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { I } }$ also makes sense as an automorphism of $( \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \tau } } ] / I \mathbb { k } [ P _ { \bar { \varphi } _ { \tau } } ] ) _ { h } = S _ { \tau , I , \emptyset }$ for any $\tau \in \Sigma \setminus \{ 0 \}$ .
Thus using the equality $S _ { \sigma , I , \emptyset } = S _ { \sigma , I , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } }$ for $\dim \sigma \ = \ 2$ we see that $\theta _ { \gamma , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { I } }$ also makes sense as an automorphism of $S _ { \sigma , I , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } }$ .

Choose an orientation on $M _ { \mathbb { R } }$ , labelling the rays $\rho _ { 1 } , \ldots , \rho _ { n }$ of $\Sigma$ in a counterclockwise order, with $\sigma _ { i - 1 , i }$ as usual the maximal cone containing $\rho _ { i - 1 }$ and $\rho _ { i }$ .
For two distinct points $p , q$ on the unit circle in $M _ { \mathbb { R } }$ not contained in $\mathrm { S u p p } ( \bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { I } )$ , let $\gamma _ { p , q }$ be a counterclockwise path from $p$ to $q$ , and write $\theta _ { p , q }$ for $\theta _ { \gamma _ { p , q } , \hat { \mathfrak { D } } _ { I } }$ acting on any of the rings Sτ,I,∅.

For each $\rho _ { i }$ , let $p _ { i , + }$ be a point on this unit circle contained in the connected component of $\sigma _ { i , i + 1 } \setminus \mathrm { S u p p } ( \bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { I } )$ adjacent to $\rho _ { i }$ , and $p _ { i , - }$ a point in this unit circle contained in the connected component of $\sigma _ { i - 1 , i } \setminus \mathrm { S u p p } ( \bar { \mathfrak { D } } _ { I } )$ adjacent to $\rho _ { i }$ .

Choose a base-point $q$ on the unit circle not in $\operatorname { S u p p } ( { \mathfrak { D } } _ { I } )$ .

Recall in the construction of $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } ^ { o }$ , the open sets Spec $\overline { { R } } _ { \rho _ { i + 1 } , I , \mathfrak { D } }$ and Spec $\overline { { R } } _ { \rho _ { i } , I , \mathfrak { D } }$ are glued together along the common open set Spec $\overline { { R } } _ { \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I , \mathfrak { D } }$ using the trivial automorphism or the automorphism $\theta _ { p _ { i , + } , p _ { i + 1 , - } }$ in the cases ${ \mathfrak { D } } = \emptyset$ or $\mathfrak { D } = \mathfrak { D }$ respectively.
After localizing at $h$ , we have a commutative diagram

![](images/31bd587b65a4329234aba4433efca03794685099f9ff0ca7f1d8d5128cd899a8.jpg)

Here the maps $\psi _ { \rho , \pm }$ are the ones defined in Proposition 2.5 and (2.8).
This shows that the isomorphisms $\theta _ { p _ { i , + } } \circ \psi _ { i }$ between $\mathrm { S p e c } S _ { \rho _ { i } , I , \bar { \mathfrak { D } } }$ and $\mathrm { S p e c } S _ { \rho _ { i } , I , \emptyset }$ are compatible with the gluings, and hence give an isomorphism between $U _ { I , \mathfrak { D } } \backslash ( \{ 0 \} \times T ^ { \mathrm { g s } } )$ and $U _ { I , \emptyset } \setminus \left( \{ 0 \} \times T ^ { \mathrm { g s } } \right)$ .

Now $\mathbb { V } _ { n }$ satisfies Serre’s condition $S _ { 2 }$ .
Since $X _ { I }$ and $X _ { I }$ are flat deformations of $\mathbb { V } _ { n } \times T ^ { \mathrm { g s } }$ , by Lemma 2.10 the above isomorphism extends across the codimension two set $\{ 0 \} \times T ^ { \mathrm { g s } }$ , giving the desired isomorphism between $U _ { I }$ and $U _ { I }$ .


We now need to use the above observations along the Gross-Siebert locus to obtain results about deformations away from the Gross-Siebert locus.
For the remainder of the section, we work with data $( Y , D )$ , $\eta$ , $P$ , but now as in Assumptions 3.13. Furthermore, we take ${ \mathfrak { D } } = { \mathfrak { D } } ^ { \operatorname { c a n } }$ .
Thus if we take $I$ an ideal with either $\sqrt { I } = G$ or $\sqrt { I } = { \mathfrak { m } }$ , we obtain a flat family $X _ { I }  \mathrm { S p e c } R _ { I } = : S _ { I }$ , and in the former case, $X _ { I }  S _ { I }$ restricts to the open subscheme of ${ \mathrm { S p e c } } R _ { I }$ whose underlying open subset is $T ^ { \mathrm { { \ u s } } }$ , giving the family over the thickening of the Gross-Siebert locus.

With $J = { \mathfrak { m } }$ or $G$ , let $\mathfrak { f } _ { J } \colon \mathfrak { X } _ { J }  \mathfrak { S } _ { J }$ denote the formal deformation determined by the deformations $X _ { J ^ { N + 1 } }  \mathrm { S p e c } R _ { J ^ { N + 1 } }$ for $N \geq 0$ .
Thus $\mathfrak { S } _ { J } = \mathrm { S p f } ( \varprojlim \mathbb { k } [ P ] / J ^ { N + 1 } )$ is the formal spectrum of the $J .$ -adic completion of $\mathbb { k } [ P ]$ , $\mathfrak { X } _ { J }$ is a formal scheme, and $\mathfrak { X } _ { J } \to \mathfrak { S } _ { J }$ is an adic flat morphism of formal schemes.
We refer to [G60] for background on formal schemes.

Let $Z _ { I } : = \operatorname { S i n g } ( f _ { I } ) \subset X _ { I }$ denote the singular locus of $f _ { I } \colon X _ { I } \to S _ { I }$ .
Thus $Z _ { I } \subset X _ { I }$ is a closed embedding of schemes.
Since the singular locus is compatible with basechange, the singular loci $Z _ { J ^ { n } } \subset X _ { J ^ { n } }$ determine a closed embedding $3 J \subset \mathfrak { X } _ { J }$ which we refer to as the singular locus of $\mathfrak { f } _ { J } \colon \mathfrak { X } _ { J }  \mathfrak { S } _ { J }$ .

Again, with $J ~ = ~ { \mathfrak { m } }$ or $G$ , we have a section $s \colon S _ { J }  X _ { J } = S _ { J } \times \mathbb { V } _ { n }$ given by $s ( t ) = t \times \{ 0 \}$ for $t \in S _ { J }$ .
We write $X _ { J } ^ { o } : = X _ { J } \setminus s ( S _ { J } ) \subset X _ { J }$ and $X _ { I } ^ { o } \subset X _ { I }$ , $\mathfrak { X } _ { J } ^ { o } \subset \mathfrak { X } _ { J }$ for the induced open embeddings.

Lemma 4.5. In the above situation, there exists $0 \not = g \in \mathbb { k } \vert P \vert$ such that $\mathrm { S u p p } ( g \cdot \mathcal { O } _ { 3 J } )$ is contained in $s ( S _ { J } )$ .
In particular, $\mathfrak { f } _ { J * } ( g \cdot \mathcal { O } _ { 3 _ { J } } )$ is a coherent sheaf on $\mathfrak { S } _ { J }$ .

Proof.
We can write an explicit open covering $\{ \mathfrak { U } _ { i , J } \}$ of $\mathfrak { X } _ { J } ^ { o }$ in the two cases $J = { \mathfrak { m } }$ o r $J = G$ , as follows.
Write $a _ { i } = z ^ { \left\lfloor D _ { i } \right\rfloor }$ and $m _ { i } = - D _ { i } ^ { 2 }$ .
In the case $J = { \mathfrak { m } }$ ,

$$
\mathfrak { U } _ { i , J } = V ( X _ { i - 1 } X _ { i + 1 } - a _ { i } X _ { i } ^ { m _ { i } } ) \subset \mathbb { A } _ { X _ { i - 1 } , X _ { i + 1 } } ^ { 2 } \times ( \mathbb { G } _ { m } ) _ { X _ { i } } \times \mathfrak { S } _ { J } .
$$

In the case $J = G$ ,

$$
\mathfrak { U } _ { i , J } = V \big ( X _ { i - 1 } X _ { i + 1 } - a _ { i } X _ { i } ^ { m _ { i } } \big ] \prod ( 1 + b _ { i j } X _ { i } ^ { - 1 } ) \big ) \subset \mathbb { A } _ { X _ { i - 1 } , X _ { i + 1 } } ^ { 2 } \times ( \mathbb { G } _ { m } ) _ { X _ { i } } \times \mathfrak { S } _ { J } ,
$$

with $b _ { i j } = z ^ { \lfloor E _ { i j } \rfloor }$ as usual.

We now use the charts $\mathfrak { U } _ { i , J }$ to compute the singular locus explicitly.
In the case $J = { \mathfrak { m } }$ , the singular locus $3 _ { i , J }$ of $\mathfrak { U } _ { i , J } / \mathfrak { S } _ { J }$ is given by

$$
\mathfrak { Z } _ { i , J } = V ( X _ { i - 1 } , X _ { i + 1 } , a _ { i } ) \subset \mathfrak { U } _ { i , J } .
$$

Hence if we define $g = u _ { 1 } \cdot \cdot \cdot u _ { n }$ then $\mathrm { S u p p } ( g \cdot \mathcal { O } _ { 3 J } )$ is contained in $s ( S _ { J } )$ .

Similarly, if $J = G$ , the structure sheaf of the singular locus of $\mathfrak { U } _ { i , J }$ is annihilated by $\begin{array} { r } { g _ { i } : = a _ { i } \prod _ { j \neq k } ( b _ { i j } - b _ { i k } ) } \end{array}$ .
(Here $\begin{array} { r } { \prod _ { j \neq k } ( b _ { i j } - b _ { i k } ) } \end{array}$ is the discriminant of the polynomial $f ( X _ { i } ) : = \prod ( X _ { i } + b _ { i j } )$ .
It is a linear combination of $f$ and $f ^ { \prime }$ with coefficients in $\mathbb { k } [ \{ b _ { i j } \} ] [ X _ { i } ]$ .
See [L02], p.
200–204.) So we can take $g = g _ { 1 } \cdots g _ { n }$ .

The support of $g \cdot { \mathcal { O } } _ { 3 J }$ is a closed subset of $s ( S _ { J } )$ , hence proper over $S _ { J }$ .
It follows that $\mathfrak { f } _ { J * } ( g \cdot \mathcal { O } _ { 3 _ { J } } )$ is coherent by [G61], 3.4.2. 

Theorem 4.6. Let $( Y , D ) , \eta , P$ satisfy Assumptions 3.13, and take $\mathfrak { D } = \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ .
Then the maps $\mathbb { k } [ P ]  \mathfrak { f } _ { \mathfrak { m } _ { * } } \mathcal { O } _ { 3 \mathfrak { m } }$ and $\mathcal { O } _ { { \widetilde { \Theta } _ { \mathfrak { m } } } } \to \mathfrak { f } _ { \mathfrak { m } _ { * } } \mathcal { O } _ { 3 \mathfrak { m } }$ are not injective, so the generic fibre of $\mathrm { f } _ { \mathfrak { m } }$ is smooth in the sense of Definition 4.2. This also implies that for $I = { \mathfrak { m } } ^ { N + 1 }$ and $N \gg 0$ , the map $\mathbb { k } [ P ] / I \to f _ { I * } { \mathcal { O } } _ { \operatorname { S i n g } ( f _ { I } ) }$ is not injective.

Proof.
By Lemma 4.5 there exists $0 \not = g \in \mathbb { k } | P |$ such that $\mathrm { S u p p } ( g \cdot { \mathcal { O } } _ { 3 _ { G } } ) \subset s ( S _ { G } )$ .
Let $E$ be the subgroup of $P ^ { \mathrm { g p } }$ generated by $P \setminus G$ , so that $U = { \mathrm { S p e c } } \Bbbk [ P + E ]$ is an open subset of $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk | P |$ .
Denote by ${ \mathfrak { S } } _ { G } ^ { o }$ the open subset of ${ \mathfrak { S } } _ { G }$ isomorphic to the completion of $U$ along the subscheme defined by $G + E$ .
This is the formal thickening of the Gross-Siebert locus $T ^ { \mathrm { \Delta \mathfrak { g s } } }$ .
By Proposition 4.3 there then exists $0 \neq h \in \mathbb { k } \lfloor P + E \rfloor$ such that $\mathrm { S u p p } ( h \cdot { \mathcal { O } } _ { 3 _ { G } \cap \mathfrak { f } _ { G } ^ { - 1 } ( \mathfrak { G } _ { G } ^ { o } ) } )$ is disjoint from $s ( S _ { G } \cap U )$ .
By multiplying $h$ by a monomial whose exponent lies in $P \setminus G$ , we can assume that $h \in \mathbb { k } \lfloor P \rfloor$ .
Thus $g h \cdot { \mathcal { O } } _ { 3 G }$ has support in the closed subset $s ( S _ { G } \setminus ( S _ { G } \cap U ) )$ .
Since this sheaf is coherent, there exists a non-zero element $k \in \mathfrak { m } \subset \mathbb { k } [ P ]$ such that $g h k \cdot \mathcal { O } _ { 3 _ { G } } = 0$ .
Noting by construction that $g h k \in \mathbb { k } [ P ]$ , we have $\mathbb { k } [ P ]  \Gamma ( 3 _ { G } , \mathcal { O } _ { 3 _ { G } } )$ is not injective, hence the composition $\begin{array} { r } { \mathbb { k } [ P ]  \Gamma ( \mathfrak { Z } _ { G } , \mathcal { O } _ { 3 _ { G } } )  \Gamma ( \mathfrak { Z } _ { \mathfrak { m } } , \mathcal { O } _ { 3 _ { \mathfrak { m } } } ) } \end{array}$ is not injective.
Since $\mathbb { k } [ P ] \subset \Gamma ( \mathfrak { S } _ { \mathfrak { m } } , \mathcal { O } _ { \mathfrak { S } _ { \mathfrak { m } } } )$ , $\mathcal { O } _ { \mathfrak { S } _ { \mathfrak { m } } } \to$ $\mathfrak { f } _ { \mathfrak { m } * } \mathcal { O } _ { 3 \mathfrak { m } }$ is not injective.


# 5. The relative torus

The flat deformations $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } } \to \operatorname { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P ] / I$ produced by the canonical scattering diagram have a useful special property: there is a natural torus action on the total space $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } }$ compatible with a torus action on the base.
The meaning of this action will be clarified in Part II, where we will prove that our family extends naturally, in the positive case, to a universal family of Looijenga pairs $( Z , D )$ together with a choice of isomorphism $D \to D _ { * }$ , where $D _ { * }$ is a fixed $n$ -cycle.
The torus action then corresponds to changing the choice of isomorphism.

Fixing the pair $( Y , D )$ as usual, $D = D _ { 1 } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + D _ { n }$ , let $\mathbb { A } ^ { D } = \mathbb { A } ^ { n }$ be the affine space with one coordinate for each component $D _ { i }$ .
Let $T ^ { D }$ be the diagonal torus acting on $\mathbb { A } ^ { D }$ , i.e., the torus $T ^ { D }$ whose character group

$$
\chi ( T ^ { D } ) = \mathbb { Z } ^ { D }
$$

is the free module with basis $e _ { D _ { 1 } } , \ldots , e _ { D _ { n } }$ .

Definition 5.1. We define a canonical map $w : A _ { 1 } ( Y )  \chi ( T ^ { D } )$ given by

$$
C \mapsto \sum ( C \cdot D _ { i } ) e _ { D _ { i } } .
$$

Suppose $P \subset A _ { 1 } ( Y )$ is a toric submonoid containing $\mathrm { N E } ( Y )$ .
We then get an action of $T ^ { D }$ on $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P ]$ , as well as on $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P ] / I$ for any monomial ideal $I$ , and hence also on $\widehat { \operatorname { S p f } ( \mathbb { k } [ P ] ) }$ for any completion of $\mathbb { k } \lfloor P \rfloor$ with respect to a monomial ideal.

We can also define a unique piecewise linear map

$$
w : B \to \chi ( T ^ { D } ) \otimes _ { \mathbb { Z } } \mathbb { R }
$$

with $w ( 0 ) = 0$ and $w ( v _ { i } ) = e _ { D _ { i } }$ , for $v _ { i }$ the primitive generator of the ray $\rho _ { i }$ .

Theorem 5.2. Let $I$ be an ideal for which $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } } \to \operatorname { S p e c } \mathbb { k } | P | / I$ is defined.
Then $T ^ { D }$ acts equivariantly on $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } } \to \operatorname { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P ] / I$ .
Furthermore, each theta function $\vartheta _ { q }$ , $q \in B ( \mathbb { Z } )$ , is an eigenfunction of this action, with character $w ( q )$ .

Proof.
It’s enough to check this on the open subset $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } } ^ { o } \subset X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } }$ .
We have a cover of $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } } ^ { o }$ by open sets the hypersurfaces

$$
U _ { \rho _ { i } , I } \subset \mathbb { A } _ { X _ { i - 1 } , X _ { i + 1 } } ^ { 2 } \times ( \mathbb { G } _ { m } ) _ { X _ { i } } \times \operatorname { S p e c } R _ { I }
$$

given by the equation

$$
X _ { i - 1 } X _ { i + 1 } = z ^ { [ D _ { i } ] } X _ { i } ^ { - D _ { i } ^ { 2 } } f _ { \rho _ { i } } ,
$$

where $f _ { \rho _ { i } }$ is the function attached to the ray $\rho _ { i }$ in $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ .
If we act on $X _ { j }$ with weight $w ( v _ { j } )$ and on $z ^ { p }$ with weight $w ( p )$ (for $p \in P$ ), then we note that for every $( \mathfrak { d } , f _ { \mathfrak { d } } ) \in \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ , every monomial in $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ has weight zero by the explicit description of $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ in Definition 3.3. In particular, the equation defining $U _ { \rho _ { i } , I }$ is clearly $T ^ { D }$ -equivariant, and each of the monomials is an eigenfunction.

Now $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } } ^ { o }$ is obtained by gluing $U _ { \rho _ { i } , \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } \subset U _ { \rho _ { i } , I }$ with $U _ { \rho _ { i + 1 } , \sigma _ { i , i + 1 } , I } \subset U _ { \rho _ { i + 1 } , I }$ , using scattering automorphisms of $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ , and these open sets are naturally identified with $( \mathbb { G } _ { m } ) _ { X _ { i } , X _ { i + 1 } } ^ { 2 } \times \operatorname { S p e c } R _ { I }$ .
The scattering automorphisms commute with the $T ^ { D }$ action, by the fact that the scattering functions have weight zero.
Thus $T ^ { D }$ acts equivariantly on $X _ { I , \mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } } \to \operatorname { S p e c } \mathbb { k } | P | / I$ .

Now we check our canonical global function $\vartheta _ { q }$ is an eigenfunction, with character $w ( q )$ .
By construction, given a broken line $\gamma$ , the weights of monomials attached to adjacent domains of linearity are the same, since the functions in the scattering diagram are of weight zero.
Thus the weight of Mono( $\gamma$ ) only depends on $q$ .
This weight can be determined by fixing the base point $Q$ in a cone $\sigma$ which contains $q$ , in which case the broken line for $q$ which doesn’t bend and is wholly contained in $\sigma$ yields the monomial $z ^ { \varphi _ { \sigma } ( q ) }$ , which has weight $w ( q )$ .
Thus $\vartheta _ { q }$ is an eigenfunction with weight $w ( q )$ .
$\boxed { \begin{array} { r l } \end{array} }$

Here we prove Theorem 0.1 and Theorem 0.2. Let us review what we know so far.
For any pair $( Y , D )$ , we know that $\mathfrak { D } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ is consistent by Theorem 3.8. Thus, if the number $n$ of irreducible components of $\boldsymbol { D }$ satisfies $n \geq 3$ , Theorem 2.28 gives the construction of $f : X _ { I } \to \operatorname { S p e c } R _ { I }$ of Theorem 0.1. The algebra structure on $A _ { I }$ has structure constants given by counts of broken lines as in Theorem 2.34. The $T ^ { D }$ equivariance is given by Theorem 5.2. If furthermore $( Y , D )$ has a toric model, then the smoothness statement follows from Theorem 4.6.

We will give a proof of Theorem 0.2 and the remaining cases of 0.1 by first proving Theorem 0.2 in the case that we know that we have the desired algebra structure on $A _ { I }$ , and then bootstrap to the general case for both theorems.

6.1. Theorem 0.2 in the case that $( Y , D )$ has a toric model.
As usual, let $P$ be the toric monoid associated to a strictly convex rational polyhedral cone $\sigma _ { P } \subset A _ { 1 } ( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } }$ which contains the Mori cone $\mathrm { N E } ( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } }$ .
We have ${ \mathfrak { m } } = P \setminus \{ 0 \}$ .
For a monomial ideal $I \subset P$ we define

$$
A _ { I } : = \bigoplus _ { q \in B ( \mathbb { Z } ) } R _ { I } \cdot \vartheta _ { q }
$$

where $R _ { I } = \Bbbk \lfloor P \rfloor / I$ .
We take throughout ${ \mathfrak { D } } = { \mathfrak { D } } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ .

Assumptions 6.1. For any monomial ideal $I$ with ${ \sqrt { I } } = { \mathfrak { m } }$ , the multiplication rule of Theorem 2.34 defines an $R _ { I }$ -algebra structure on $A _ { I }$ , so that $A _ { I } \otimes _ { R _ { I } } R _ { \mathfrak { m } } = H ^ { \cup } ( \mathbb { V } _ { n } , { \mathcal { O } } _ { \mathbb { V } _ { n } } )$ .

Note we have already shown that Assumptions 6.1 hold if $n \geq 3$ by Theorems 2.28, 2.34 and 3.8.

Let $\Gamma \subset B ( \mathbb { Z } )$ be a finite collection of integral points such that the corresponding functions $\vartheta _ { q }$ generate the k-algebra $H ^ { 0 } ( \mathbb { V } _ { n } , { \mathcal { O } } _ { \mathbb { V } _ { n } } )$ .
(Then the $\vartheta _ { q }$ , $q \in \Gamma$ generate $A _ { I }$ as an $R _ { I }$ -algebra if $\sqrt { I } = { \mathfrak { m } }$ and Assumptions 6.1 hold.) Note for $n \geq 3$ we can take for $\Gamma$ the points $\{ v _ { i } \}$ , and for $n = 1 , 2$ , one can make a simple choice for $\Gamma$ , see §6.2.

Lemma 6.2. For any monomial ideal $J \subset P$ , $\textstyle \bigcap _ { k > 0 } ( J + \mathfrak { m } ^ { k } ) = J$ .

Proof.
The inclusion $\supset$ is obvious.
For the other direction, as the intersection is a monomial ideal, it’s enough to consider a monomial in the intersection.
But notice that a monomial is in $J + \mathfrak { m } ^ { k }$ iff it is either in $J$ or in $\mathfrak { m } ^ { k }$ .
The result follows since $\cap { \mathfrak { m } } ^ { k } = 0$ .


Assuming 6.1, let $\mathcal { A }$ be the collection of monomial ideals $J \subset P$ with the following properties:

(1) There is an $R _ { J }$ -algebra structure on $A _ { J }$ such that the canonical isomorphism of $R$ -modules $A _ { J } \otimes _ { R _ { J } } R _ { I + J } = A _ { I + J }$ is an algebra isomorphism, for all $\sqrt { I } = \mathfrak { m }$ .

(2) $\vartheta _ { q } , q \in \Gamma$ generate $A _ { J }$ as an $R _ { J }$ -algebra.

By the lemma, the algebra structure in (1) is unique if it exists.
The algebra structure on all $A _ { I }$ determines such a structure on ${ \widehat { A } } : = \varprojlim _ { \sqrt { I } = \mathfrak { m } } A _ { I }$ , $\widehat { A } _ { J } : = \varprojlim _ { \sqrt { I } = \mathfrak { m } } A _ { I + J }$ .
Also, there are canonical inclusions

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { \displaystyle \widehat { A } \subset \displaystyle \prod _ { q \in B ( \mathbb { Z } ) } \hat { R } \cdot \vartheta _ { q } } } \\ { { \displaystyle \widehat { A } _ { J } \subset \displaystyle \prod _ { q \in B ( \mathbb { Z } ) } \hat { R } _ { J } \cdot \vartheta _ { q } } } \end{array}
$$

where $\hat { R }$ is the completion of $R$ at $\mathfrak { m }$ and $\hat { R } _ { J } = \varprojlim R / ( I + J )$ the inverse limit over all ideals $I$ with $\sqrt { I } = { \mathfrak { m } }$ ←−.
Here the direct products are viewed purely as $\hat { R } , \hat { R } _ { J }$ modules.
We can also view

$$
A _ { J } : = \bigoplus _ { q \in { \cal B } ( \mathbb { Z } ) } R _ { J } \cdot \vartheta _ { q } \subset \prod _ { q \in { \cal B } ( \mathbb { Z } ) } \hat { R } _ { J } \cdot \vartheta _ { q } .
$$

It is clear that $A _ { J } \subset { \widehat { A } } _ { J }$ (as submodules of the direct product).
Thus (1) holds if and only if the following holds:

(1 $\cdot ^ { \prime }$ ) For each $p , q \in B ( \mathbb { Z } )$ , at most finitely many $z ^ { C } \vartheta _ { s }$ with $C \notin J$ appear in the product expansion of Theorem 2.34 for $\vartheta _ { p } \cdot \vartheta _ { q } \in \widehat { A } _ { J }$ .

Lemma 6.3. If $J \in { \mathcal { A } }$ and $J \subset J ^ { \prime }$ , then $J ^ { \prime } \in { \mathcal { A } }$ .
In addition, $\mathcal { A }$ is closed under finite intersections.

Proof.
The first statement is clear.
Now assume $J _ { 1 } , J _ { 2 } \in { \mathcal { A } }$ .
It’s clear that ( $1 ^ { \prime }$ ) holds for $J _ { 1 } \cap J _ { 2 }$ , so $A _ { J _ { 1 } \cap J _ { 2 } }$ is an algebra.
Moreover we have an exact sequence of $\Bbbk$ -modules

$$
0  A _ { J _ { 1 } \cap J _ { 2 } }  A _ { J _ { 1 } } \times A _ { J _ { 2 } }  A _ { J _ { 1 } + J _ { 2 } }  0
$$

exhibiting $A _ { J _ { 1 } \cap J _ { 2 } }$ as the fibre product $A _ { J _ { 1 } } \times _ { A _ { J _ { 1 } + J _ { 2 } } } A _ { J _ { 2 } } = : A _ { 1 } \times _ { B } A _ { 2 } = : A$ .
We now show this fibre product is a finitely generated $\Bbbk$ -algebra.
Indeed, note that since the maps $A _ { 1 } , A _ { 2 }  B$ are surjective, so are the maps $A \to A _ { i }$ .
Let $\{ u _ { i } \}$ be a generating set for the ideal $\ker ( A _ { 2 }  B )$ .
Since $A _ { i }$ is Noetherian, one can find a finite such set.
Note that $\tilde { u } _ { i } : = ( 0 , u _ { i } ) \in A$ .
In addition, choose finite sets $\{ x _ { i } \}$ , $\{ y _ { j } \}$ generating $A _ { 1 }$ and $A _ { 2 }$ as k-algebras.
For each of these elements, choose a lift to $A$ , giving a finite set of lifts $\{ \tilde { u } _ { i } , \tilde { x } _ { i } , \tilde { y } _ { i } \}$ , which we claim generate $A$ .
Indeed, given $( x , y ) \in A$ , one can subtract a polynomial in the $\tilde { x } _ { i }$ ’s to obtain $( 0 , y ^ { \prime } )$ .
Necessarily $y ^ { \prime } \in \ker ( A _ { 2 } \to B )$ , and hence we can write $\boldsymbol { y } ^ { \prime } = \sum f _ { i } \boldsymbol { u } _ { i }$ with $f _ { i }$ a polynomial in the $y _ { i }$ ’s.
Let $\tilde { f } _ { i }$ be the same polynomial in the $\tilde { y } _ { i }$ ’s.
Then $\sum \tilde { f } _ { i } \tilde { u } _ { i } = ( 0 , y ^ { \prime } )$ , showing generation.

Thus $A _ { J _ { 1 } \cap J _ { 2 } }$ is also a finitely generated $R _ { J _ { 1 } \cap J _ { 2 } }$ -algebra.
Now the generation statement follows from Lemma 6.4, taking $R = R _ { J _ { 1 } \cap J _ { 2 } }$ , $S = A _ { J _ { 1 } \cap J _ { 2 } }$ , $I = J _ { 1 } / J _ { 1 } \cap J _ { 2 }$ , $J = J _ { 2 } / J _ { 1 } \cap J _ { 2 }$ , $\Gamma = \{ q _ { 1 } , \dots , q _ { m } \}$ , and the map $R [ T _ { 1 } , \dots , T _ { m } ]  S$ given by $T _ { i } \mapsto \vartheta _ { q _ { i } }$ .


Lemma 6.4. Let $I , J \subset R$ be ideals in a Noetherian ring, with $I \cdot J = 0$ , and let $S$ be a finitely generated $R$ -algebra, and $R [ T _ { 1 } , \dots , T _ { m } ] \to S$ an $R$ -algebra map which is surjective modulo $I$ and $J$ .
Then the map is surjective.

Proof.
The associated map Spec $S \to \mathbb { A } ^ { m } \times \operatorname { S p e c } R$ is proper, as can be easily checked using the valuative criterion for properness.
Indeed, any map $S  K$ for a field $K$ factors through either $S / I S$ or $S / J S$ .
Since this is a map of affine schemes, $S$ is a finite $R [ T _ { 1 } , \ldots , T _ { m } ]$ -module.
Now we can apply Nakayama’s lemma.


Proposition 6.5. There is a unique minimal radical monomial ideal $I _ { \operatorname* { m i n } } \subset P$ such that (1) and (2) hold for any monomial ideal $J$ with $I _ { \operatorname* { m i n } } \subset \sqrt { J }$ .

Proof.
Certainly any ideal $J$ with ${ \sqrt { J } } = { \mathfrak { m } }$ lies in $\mathcal { A }$ .
Note that a radical monomial ideal is the complement of a union of faces of $P$ , so there are only a finite number of such ideals.
Suppose $I _ { 1 } , I _ { 2 }$ are two radical ideals such that $J _ { i } \in { \mathcal { A } }$ for any $J _ { i }$ with $I _ { i } \subset \sqrt { J _ { i } }$ .
Note that any ideal $J$ with $I _ { 1 } \cap I _ { 2 } \subset { \sqrt { J } }$ can be written as $J _ { 1 } \cap J _ { 2 }$ , with $I _ { i } \subset \sqrt { J _ { i } }$ .
(Indeed, we can use the primary decomposition of $J$ .
If $\boldsymbol { J } = \bigcap _ { k } \mathfrak { p } _ { k }$ is an intersection of primary ideals, necessarily the prime ideal $\sqrt { { \mathfrak { p } } _ { k } }$ contains either $I _ { 1 }$ or $I _ { 2 }$ for each $k$ .
Then let $J _ { 1 }$ be the intersection of those ${ \mathfrak { p } } _ { k }$ whose radical contains $I _ { 1 }$ and $J _ { 2 }$ be the intersection of those ${ \mathfrak { p } } _ { k }$ whose radical contains $I _ { 2 }$ .) Thus by Lemma 6.3, $J \in { \mathcal { A } }$ .
This shows the existence of $I _ { \mathrm { m i n } }$ .


# Proposition 6.6. Assume 6.1.

(1) Suppose the intersection matrix $( D _ { i } \cdot D _ { j } )$ is not negative semi-definite.
Then $I _ { \operatorname* { m i n } } = ( 0 ) \subset \mathbb { k } [ P ]$ .  
(2) Suppose $F ~ \subset ~ \sigma _ { P }$ is a face such that $F ^ { \prime }$ does not contain the class of every component of $\boldsymbol { D }$ .
Then $I _ { \operatorname* { m i n } } \subset P \setminus F$ .

Proof.
We prove both cases simultaneously, writing $F : = P$ in case (1).
We claim there exists an effective divisor $W = \sum a _ { i } D _ { i }$ with support $\boldsymbol { D }$ such that $W \cdot D _ { j } > 0$ for all $D _ { j }$ contained in $F ^ { \prime }$ and $a _ { i } > 0$ for all $i$ .
For case (1), see Lemma 6.9. In case (2), say $[ D _ { 1 } ] \notin F$ .
Then we can take $a _ { 1 } \gg a _ { 2 } \gg \cdots \gg a _ { n } > 0$ .

The algebra structure depends only on the deformation type of $( Y , D )$ .
By Proposition 4.1 of [GHK12], we may replace $( Y , D )$ by a deformation equivalent pair such that any irreducible curve $C \subset Y$ intersects $\boldsymbol { D }$ .

Let $\overline { { \mathrm { N E } } } ( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } } \subset A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { R } )$ denote the closure of $\mathrm { N E } ( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } }$ .
Let $F ^ { \prime } : = \overline { { \mathrm { N E } } } ( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } } \cap F$ , a face of $\overline { { \mathrm { N E } } } ( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } }$ .
Define $\Delta = D - \epsilon W$ , $0 < \epsilon \ll 1$ .
Then $( Y , \Delta )$ is KLT (Kawamata log terminal).

We claim $K _ { Y } + \Delta \sim - \epsilon W$ is negative on $F ^ { \prime } \backslash \{ 0 \}$ .
By construction $( K _ { Y } + \Delta ) \cdot D _ { j } < 0$ for $\vert D _ { j } \vert \in F ^ { \prime }$ and $( K _ { Y } + \Delta ) \cdot C < 0$ for $C \ \mathcal { L } \ D$ .
Let $N$ be a nef divisor such that ${ \cal F } ^ { \prime } = \overline { { { \mathrm { N E } } } } ( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } } \cap { \cal N } ^ { \bot }$ .
Then $a N - ( K _ { Y } + \Delta )$ is nef and big for $a \gg 0$ , and thus some multiple of $N$ defines a birational morphism $g$ by the basepoint-free theorem [KM98], Theorem 3.3. Thus $F ^ { \prime }$ is generated by exceptional curves of $g$ .
We deduce that $( K _ { Y } + \Delta ) ^ { \perp } \cap F ^ { \prime } = \{ 0 \}$ and $( K _ { Y } + \Delta )$ is negative on $F ^ { \prime } \backslash \{ 0 \}$ as claimed.

Now by the cone theorem [KM98], Theorem 3.7, $\overline { { \mathrm { N E } } } ( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } }$ is rational polyhedral near $F ^ { \prime }$ and there is a contraction $p \colon Y  Y$ such that $F ^ { \prime }$ is generated by the classes of curves contracted by $p$ .
It follows that we can find $\mathrm { N E } ( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } } \subset \sigma _ { P ^ { \prime } } \subset \sigma _ { P }$ such that $F ^ { \prime }$ is a face of $\sigma _ { P ^ { \prime } }$ .
Now the algebra structure for $P$ comes from $P ^ { \prime }$ by base extension, so (replacing $P$ by $P ^ { \prime }$ ) we can assume $F = F ^ { \prime }$ , and thus that $W$ is positive on $F \setminus \{ 0 \}$ .

Now let $J$ be a monomial ideal with ${ \sqrt { J } } = P \setminus F$ .
Consider condition ( $1 ^ { \prime }$ ).
By the $T ^ { D }$ -equivariance of Theorem 5.2, any $z ^ { C } \vartheta _ { s }$ that appears in $\vartheta _ { p } \cdot \vartheta _ { q }$ has the same weight for $T ^ { D }$ .
Thus it is enough to show that the map

$$
w \colon B ( \mathbb { Z } ) \times ( P \setminus J ) \to \chi ( T ^ { D } ) , \quad ( q , C ) \mapsto w ( q ) + w ( C )
$$

has finite fibres.
It is enough to consider fibres of $\sigma ( \mathbb { Z } ) \times ( P \setminus J )  \chi ( T ^ { D } )$ for each $\sigma \in \Sigma _ { \mathrm { m a x } }$ .
Note that $\sigma ( \mathbb { Z } ) \times P$ is the set of integral points of a rational polyhedral cone, and $w$ is linear on this set.
Thus it is enough to check that $\ker ( w ) \cap ( \sigma ( \mathbb { Z } ) \times F ) = 0$ .
So suppose we have $q \in \sigma ( \mathbb { Z } )$ , $C \in F$ with $w ( q ) + w ( C ) = 0$ .
Say $\sigma = \sigma _ { i , i + 1 }$ .
Then $q = a v _ { i } + b v _ { i + 1 }$ , for $a , b \in \mathbb { Z } _ { \geq 0 }$ .
We have

$$
w ( q ) + w ( C ) = a e _ { D _ { i } } + b e _ { D _ { i + 1 } } + \sum _ { j } ( C \cdot D _ { j } ) e _ { D _ { j } } ;
$$

thus if this is zero, we have $C \cdot D _ { j } \leq 0$ for all $j$ .
In particular, $W \cdot C \leq 0$ .
Since $W$ is positive on $F \setminus \{ 0 \}$ , $C = 0$ .
Now necessarily $a = b = q = 0$ .
This proves ( $1 ^ { \prime }$ ).

For (2), let $A _ { J } ^ { \prime } \subset A _ { J }$ be the subalgebra generated by the $\vartheta _ { q } , q \in \Gamma$ .
Fix a weight $w \in \chi ( T ^ { D } )$ .
To show $A _ { J } ^ { \prime } = A _ { J }$ it is enough to show that the finite set

$$
\{ z ^ { C } \vartheta _ { q } \in A _ { J } | ( q , C ) \in B ( \mathbb { Z } ) \times ( P \setminus J ) \mathrm { ~ o f ~ w e i g h t ~ } w \}
$$

is contained in $A _ { J } ^ { \prime }$ (since the $z ^ { C } \vartheta _ { q }$ give a $\Bbbk$ -basis of $A _ { J }$ ).
We argue by decreasing induction on $\mathrm { o r d } _ { \mathfrak { m } } ( C )$ (see Definition 2.21). Since the set of possible $( q , C )$ is finite, there is an upper bound on the possible ord $\mathfrak { m }$ ’s.
So the claim is vacuously true for large $\mathrm { o r d } _ { \mathfrak { m } }$ .
Consider $z ^ { C } \cdot \vartheta _ { p }$ , with $\mathrm { o r d } _ { \mathfrak { m } } ( C ) = h$ .
Since the $\vartheta _ { q }$ generate $A _ { J }$ modulo $\mathfrak { m }$ , we can find $a \in A _ { J } ^ { \prime }$ such that

$$
\vartheta _ { p } = a + m
$$

with $m \in { \mathfrak { m } } \cdot A _ { J }$ .
Moreover, we can assume $a$ , and thus $m$ , is homogeneous for the $T ^ { D }$ action.
Now

$$
z ^ { C } \vartheta _ { p } = z ^ { C } a + z ^ { C } m .
$$

Clearly $z ^ { C } m$ is a sum of terms $z ^ { D } \vartheta _ { q }$ of weight $w$ and $\operatorname { o r d } _ { \mathfrak { m } } ( D ) > h$ , so $z ^ { C } m \in A _ { J } ^ { \prime }$ by induction.
$\boxed { \begin{array} { r l } \end{array} }$

Remark 6.7. Suppose $p \colon Y  Y ^ { \prime }$ is a contraction such that some component of $\boldsymbol { D }$ is not contracted by $p$ .
Let $F ^ { \prime }$ be the face of $\mathrm { N E } ( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } }$ generated by classes of curves contracted by $p$ .
Then $\mathrm { N E } ( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } }$ is rational polyhedral near $F ^ { \prime }$ .
(This follows from the cone theorem, cf.
the proof of Proposition 6.6.) In particular there exists a rational polyhedral cone $\sigma _ { P } \subset A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { R } )$ such that N $\operatorname { E } ( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } } \subset \sigma _ { P }$ and $\sigma _ { P }$ coincides with NE $( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } }$ near $F ^ { \prime }$ .

Corollary 6.8. Theorem 0.2 holds if $D$ has $n \geq 3$ irreducible components.

Proof.
Immediate from Proposition 6.6.

6.2. Proof of Theorems 0.1 and 0.2 in general.
We now consider an arbitrary Looijenga pair $( Y , D )$ , along with a toric monoid $P$ with $\mathrm { N E } ( Y ) \subset P \subset A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { Z } )$ .
Let $\tau : ( Y ^ { \prime } , D ^ { \prime } )  ( Y , D )$ be a toric blowup such that $( Y ^ { \prime } , D ^ { \prime } )$ has a toric model $p : ( Y ^ { \prime } , D ^ { \prime } )  ( \bar { Y } , \bar { D } )$ .
We have the map $\tau _ { * } : A _ { 1 } ( Y ^ { \prime } , \mathbb { Z } ) \to A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { Z } )$ .
We can find a strictly convex rational polyhedral cone $\sigma _ { P ^ { \prime } }$ with

$$
\mathrm { N E } ( Y ^ { \prime } ) _ { \mathbb { R } } \subset \sigma _ { P ^ { \prime } } \subset A _ { 1 } ( Y ^ { \prime } , \mathbb { R } )
$$

which has a face $F ^ { \prime }$ spanned by the $\tau$ -exceptional curves, and which surjects under $\tau _ { * }$ onto $\sigma _ { P } \subset A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { R } )$ .
For any monomial ideal $I \subset P$ with $\sqrt { I } = { \mathfrak { m } }$ , let ${ \cal I } ^ { \prime } \subset \mathop { P ^ { \prime } }$ be the inverse image of $I$ under $\tau _ { * }$ .
Then $\sqrt { I ^ { \prime } }$ is the prime monomial ideal associated to the face $F ^ { \prime }$ .
Since the exceptional curves are a proper subset of $D ^ { \prime }$ we have $\sqrt { I ^ { \prime } } \in$ $\mathcal { A } ( Y ^ { \prime } )$ by Proposition 6.6. Note that $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P ] / I$ is naturally a closed subscheme of $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P ^ { \prime } ] / I ^ { \prime }$ , via the map induced by the surjection $\tau _ { * } : P ^ { \prime } \to P$ .
Now restrict the family $\mathcal { X } _ { I ^ { \prime } }  \mathrm { S p e c } \| \mathbf { \boldsymbol { x } } [ P ^ { \prime } ] / I ^ { \prime }$ to $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P ] / I$ .
This gives an algebra structure on

$$
A _ { I } : = \bigoplus _ { q \in B ( \mathbb { Z } ) } ( \mathbb { k } [ P ] / I ) \vartheta _ { q } .
$$

We now verify Assumptions 6.1. First, we show that the multiplication rule of this algebra is the one described in Theorem 2.34. The argument is just as in the proof of Proposition 3.12: We have $B _ { ( Y ^ { \prime } , D ^ { \prime } ) } = B _ { ( Y , D ) }$ and take $\psi : = \tau _ { * } : P ^ { \prime }  P$ .
Note $\psi ( \mathfrak { D } _ { ( Y ^ { \prime } , D ^ { \prime } ) } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } } ) = \mathfrak { D } _ { ( Y , D ) } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ (i.e., the rays are the same, and we apply $\psi$ to the decoration function).
This does not literally give a bijection on broken lines (because different exponents in the decoration of a ray in ${ \mathfrak { D } } _ { ( Y ^ { \prime } , D ^ { \prime } ) } ^ { \operatorname { c a n } }$ could map to the same exponent under $\psi$ ).
However, by Equation (3.4), with $z$ a point close to $q$ ,

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { \displaystyle \sum _ { \mathrm { \normalfont ~ l i m i t s } ( \gamma _ { 1 } ) = ( q _ { i } , z ) } c ( \gamma _ { 1 } ) c ( \gamma _ { 2 } ) = } & { \sum _ { \mathrm { \normalfont ~ l i m i t s } ( \gamma _ { 1 } ) = ( q _ { i } , z ) } \psi \left( \sum _ { \gamma _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } \in \mathcal { E } _ { \gamma _ { 1 } } } c ( \gamma _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } ) \right) \psi \left( \sum _ { \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } \in \mathcal { E } _ { \gamma _ { 2 } } } c ( \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } ) \right) } \\ { \displaystyle \operatorname* { l i m i t s } ( \gamma _ { i } ) = ( q _ { i } , z ) } & { \quad \mathrm { \normalfont ~ l i m i t s } ( \gamma _ { 1 } ) = ( q _ { i } , z ) } \\ { \displaystyle s ( \gamma _ { 1 } ) + s ( \gamma _ { 2 } ) = q } & { } \\ & { = \psi \left( \begin{array} { c } { \displaystyle \sum _ { \mathrm { \normalfont ~ l i m i t s } ( \gamma _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } ) \geq ( q _ { i } , z ) } c ( \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } ) } \\ { \displaystyle \sum _ { \mathrm { \normalfont ~ l i m i t s } ( \gamma _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } ) = ( q _ { i } , z ) } c ( \gamma _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } ) c ( \gamma _ { 2 } ^ { \prime } ) } \\ { \displaystyle \sum _ { \mathrm { \normalfont ~ l i m i t s } ( \gamma _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } ) \geq ( q _ { i } , z ) } c ( \gamma _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } ) } \\ { \displaystyle s ( \gamma _ { 1 } ) + s ( \gamma _ { 2 } ) = q } \end{array} \right) , } \end{array}
$$

where $\xi _ { \gamma _ { i } }$ denotes the set of all broken lines $\gamma _ { i } ^ { \prime }$ for ${ \mathfrak { D } } _ { ( Y ^ { \prime } , D ^ { \prime } ) } ^ { \mathrm { c a n } }$ such that $\psi \circ \gamma _ { i } ^ { \prime } = \gamma _ { i }$ as paths and the monomials attached to $\psi ( \gamma _ { i } ^ { \prime } )$ differ from those attached to $\gamma _ { i }$ only in the $\Bbbk$ -valued coefficients (see the proof of Proposition 3.12). This implies the claim.

Next we need to check that the fibre over the zero stratum of $\mathrm { S p e c } \Bbbk [ P ]$ is $\mathbb { V } _ { n }$ .
In case $n \geq 3$ , this is straightforward from the multiplication rule.
Indeed, modulo $\mathfrak { m }$ , every broken line contributing to the multiplication rule is a straight line, and furthermore it cannot cross any ray of $\Sigma$ .
$\begin{array} { r } { \boldsymbol { \dot { \lambda } } } \end{array}$ From this one sees that $A _ { \mathfrak { m } } = R _ { \mathfrak { m } } [ \Sigma ]$ .

The cases $n = 1$ and 2 require special attention.
We will do the case of $n = 1$ , as $n \ = \ 2$ is similar (and simpler).
We cut $B \ = \ B _ { ( Y , D ) }$ along the unique ray $\rho =$ $\rho _ { 1 } \in \Sigma$ , and consider the image under a set of linear coordinates $\psi$ on $B \setminus \rho$ .
This identifies $B \setminus \rho$ with a strictly convex rational cone in $\mathbb { R } ^ { 2 }$ .
Let $w , w ^ { \prime }$ be the primitive generators of the two boundary rays.
Modulo $\mathfrak { m }$ the decoration on every scattering ray is trivial, so every broken line is straight.
Moreover, no line can cross $\rho$ (or the attached monomial becomes trivial modulo $\mathfrak { m }$ by the strict convexity of $\varphi$ ).
Now it follows for any $x \in B ( \mathbb { R } ) \setminus \rho$ and any $q \ \in \ ( B \setminus \rho ) ( \mathbb { Z } )$ there is a unique (straight) broken line with $\mathrm { L i m i t s } = ( q , x )$ , while there are exactly two (straight) broken lines with ${ \mathrm { L i m i t s } } = ( v , x )$ , $v = v _ { 1 }$ — under $\psi$ these become two distinct straight lines with directions $w , w ^ { \prime }$ .
Performing a toric blowup of $( Y , D )$ to get $n ^ { \prime } = 3$ can be accomplished by subdividing the cone generated by $w$ and $w ^ { \prime }$ along the rays generated by $w + w ^ { \prime }$ a nd $2 w + w ^ { \prime }$ .
Then by Theorem 0.2 in the case $n = 3$ , we see that $A _ { \mathfrak { m } }$ is generated over $\Bbbk$ by

$$
\vartheta _ { v } = \vartheta _ { w } = \vartheta _ { w ^ { \prime } } , \vartheta _ { w + w ^ { \prime } } , \vartheta _ { 2 w + w ^ { \prime } }
$$

where we abuse notation and use the same symbol for an integer point in the convex cone generated by $w$ and $w ^ { \prime }$ , and the corresponding point in $B ( \mathbb { Z } )$ .
Now applying the

multiplication rule of Theorem 2.34 one checks easily the equalities:

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \vartheta _ { v } \cdot \vartheta _ { w + w ^ { \prime } } = \vartheta _ { 2 w + w ^ { \prime } } + \vartheta _ { w + 2 w ^ { \prime } } } \\ { \qquad \vartheta _ { 2 w + w ^ { \prime } } \cdot \vartheta _ { w + 2 w ^ { \prime } } = \vartheta _ { 3 w + 3 w ^ { \prime } } = \vartheta _ { w + w ^ { \prime } } ^ { 3 } . } \end{array}
$$

It follows that

$$
\vartheta _ { 2 w + w ^ { \prime } } \cdot \vartheta _ { v } \cdot \vartheta _ { w + w ^ { \prime } } = \vartheta _ { 2 w + w ^ { \prime } } ^ { 2 } + \vartheta _ { w + w ^ { \prime } } ^ { 3 }
$$

and thus $A _ { \mathfrak { m } } = \mathbb { k } [ x , y , z ] / ( x y z - x ^ { 2 } - z ^ { 3 } )$ , which is isomorphic to the ring of sections

$$
\bigoplus _ { m \geq 0 } H ^ { 0 } ( C , { \mathcal { O } } ( m ) )
$$

for a line bundle $\mathcal { O } ( 1 )$ of degree one on an irreducible rational nodal curve $C$ of arithmetic genus 1. Thus Spec $A _ { \mathfrak { m } } = \mathbb { V } _ { 1 }$ .

Combining this with Propositions 6.5 and 6.6, this proves Theorems 0.1 and 0.2 hold for all $( Y , D )$ except for the smoothness statement of Theorem 0.1.

To show smoothness, note that if ${ \mathfrak { m } } ^ { \prime }$ denotes the maximal monomial ideal of $P ^ { \prime }$ , $\mathfrak { X } _ { \mathfrak { m ^ { \prime } } } ^ { \prime } \to \mathfrak { S } _ { \mathfrak { m ^ { \prime } } }$ the formal deformation provided by Theorem 0.1 for the pair $( Y ^ { \prime } , D ^ { \prime } )$ with the toric model, we know that $\mathbb { k } [ P ^ { \prime } ]  H ^ { \scriptscriptstyle 0 } ( 3 _ { \mathfrak { m } ^ { \prime } } , { \mathcal { O } } _ { 3 _ { \mathfrak { m } ^ { \prime } } } )$ is not injective by Theorem 4.6. Now choose (see the beginning of the proof of Proposition 6.6) a divisor $A =$ $\sum _ { i } a _ { i } D _ { i } ^ { \prime }$ with $a _ { i } \geq 0$ for all $i$ and $A$ relatively $\tau$ -ample, so that $A \cdot D _ { j } ^ { \prime } > 0$ for any $D _ { j } ^ { \prime }$ contracted by $\tau$ .
This determines a one-parameter subgroup $T ^ { A } \cong \mathbb { G } _ { m }$ of $T ^ { D ^ { \prime } }$ via the map $\chi ( T ^ { D ^ { \prime } } ) \to \mathbb { Z }$ given by $e _ { D _ { i } ^ { \prime } } \mapsto a _ { i }$ .

Let $J = P ^ { \prime } \setminus F$ , so that $[ C ] \in J$ if and only if $C$ is not contracted by $\tau$ .
Thus if $[ C ] \in F$ , $T ^ { A }$ acts on $z ^ { [ C ] }$ with weight $C . A > 0$ , and for $q \in B ( \mathbb { Z } )$ , $T ^ { A }$ acts with non-negative weight since $a _ { i } \geq 0$ for all $i$ .
It then follows that the map

$$
H ^ { 0 } ( 3 _ { J } , \mathcal { O } _ { 3 _ { J } } )  H ^ { 0 } ( 3 _ { \mathfrak { m } ^ { \prime } } , \mathcal { O } _ { 3 _ { \mathfrak { m } ^ { \prime } } } )
$$

is injective because every component of $3 J$ has a limit point in $3 \mathrm { m } ^ { \prime }$ under the $T ^ { A }$ action.  
So we conclude that $\mathbb { k } [ P ^ { \prime } ]  H ^ { \cup } ( 3 _ { J } , { \mathcal { O } } _ { 3 _ { J } } )$ is not injective.

Now $F ^ { \mathrm { g p } }$ is generated by the classes of the $D _ { i } ^ { \prime }$ contracted by $\tau$ .
Let $T ^ { F } : = \operatorname { H o m } ( F , \mathbb { G } _ { m } )$ The composition $F ^ { \mathrm { g p } } \subset A _ { 1 } ( Y ^ { \prime } , \mathbb { Z } ) \to \chi ( T ^ { D ^ { \prime } } )$ is a primitive embedding, because the intersection matrix of $F ~ \subset ~ \langle D _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } , \ldots , D _ { r } ^ { \prime } \rangle$ is unimodular, where $D _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } , \ldots , D _ { r } ^ { \prime }$ are the irreducible components of the boundary of $Y ^ { \prime }$ .
So the corresponding composition $T ^ { D ^ { \prime } } \to \operatorname { H o m } ( A _ { 1 } ( Y ^ { \prime } , \mathbb { Z } ) , \mathbb { G } _ { m } ) \to T ^ { F }$ admits a splitting $T ^ { F }  T ^ { D ^ { \prime } }$ .
By $T ^ { F }$ -equivariance, the restriction of the family $\mathfrak { X } _ { J } ^ { \prime } / \mathfrak { S } _ { J } ^ { \prime }$ to the open subscheme of $\mathfrak { S } _ { J } ^ { \prime }$ defined by $T ^ { F } \subset S _ { J } ^ { \prime }$ is isomorphic to a direct product of ${ \mathfrak { X } } _ { \mathfrak { m } } / { \mathfrak { S } } _ { \mathfrak { m } }$ (coming from $( Y , D ) , P )$ with $T ^ { F }$ .
In particular, ${ \mathfrak { X } } _ { \mathfrak { m } } / { \mathfrak { S } } _ { \mathfrak { m } }$ has smooth generic fibre.

6.3. The case that $( Y , D )$ is positive.

Lemma 6.9. The following are equivalent for a Looijenga pair $( Y , D )$ :

(1.1) There exist integers $a _ { 1 } , \ldots , a _ { n }$ such that $( \sum a _ { i } D _ { i } ) ^ { 2 } > 0$ .
(1.2) There exist positive integers $b _ { 1 } , \ldots , b _ { n }$ such that $\left( \sum b _ { i } D _ { i } \right) \cdot D _ { j } > 0$ for all $j$ .
(1.3) $Y \setminus D$ is the minimal resolution of an affine surface with (at worst) Du Val singularities.
(1.4) There exist $0 < c _ { i } < 1$ such that $- ( K _ { Y } + \sum c _ { i } D _ { i } )$ is nef and big.
If any of the above equivalent conditions hold, then so do the following: (2.1) The Mori cone NE $( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } }$ is rational polyhedral, generated by finitely many classes of rational curves.
Every nef line bundle on $Y$ is semi-ample.
(2.2) The subgroup $G$ of $\operatorname { A u t } ( \operatorname* { P i c } ( Y ) , \langle \cdot , \cdot \rangle )$ fixing the classes $[ D _ { i } ]$ is finite.
(2.3) The union $R \subset Y$ of all curves disjoint from $\boldsymbol { D }$ is contractible.

Definition 6.10. We say a Looijenga pair $( Y , D )$ is positive if it satisfies any of the equivalent conditions (1.1)-(1.4) of the above lemma.

Proof.
We have

$$
K _ { Y } + \sum c _ { i } D _ { i } = ( K _ { Y } + D ) - \sum ( 1 - c _ { i } ) D _ { i } = - \sum ( 1 - c _ { i } ) D _ { i }
$$

so (1.2) implies (1.4), and (1.2) obviously implies (1.1).

If (1.1) holds then $( D ^ { \perp } , \langle \cdot , \cdot \rangle )$ , where $D ^ { \perp } = \{ H \in \mathrm { P i c } Y | H \cdot D _ { i } = 0 \quad \forall i \}$ , is negative definite, by the Hodge Index Theorem, and this implies (2.2) and (2.3).

Suppose (1.4) holds.
By the basepoint-free theorem [KM98], 3.3, the linear system

$$
| m ( \sum b _ { i } D _ { i } ) | = | - m ( K _ { Y } + \sum c _ { i } D _ { i } ) |
$$

defines a birational morphism for $m \in \mathbb { N }$ sufficiently large, with exceptional locus the union $R$ of curves disjoint from $\boldsymbol { D }$ .
Adjunction shows $R$ is a contractible configuration of $\left( - 2 \right)$ -curves, which gives (1.3).
(2.1) follows from the cone theorem [KM98], 3.7.

We show (1.1) implies (1.2).
By the Riemann–Roch theorem, if $W$ is a Weil divisor (on any smooth surface) and $W ^ { 2 } > 0$ then either $W$ or $- W$ is big (i.e., the rational map given by $| n W |$ is birational for sufficiently large $n$ ).
So, possibly replacing the divisor by its negative, we may assume $\begin{array} { r } { W = \sum a _ { i } D _ { i } } \end{array}$ is big.
Write

$$
W ^ { \prime } = \sum _ { a _ { i } > 0 } a _ { i } D _ { i } = W + \sum _ { - a _ { i } > 0 } ( - a _ { i } ) D _ { i } .
$$

Thus $W ^ { \prime }$ is big, and replacing $W$ by $n W ^ { \prime }$ , we may assume all $a _ { i } \geq 0$ and $| W |$ defines a birational (rational) map.
Subtracting off the divisorial base-locus (which does not affect the rational map) we may further assume the base locus is at most zero dimensional.
Now $W = \sum b _ { i } D _ { i }$ is effective, nef and big, and supported on $\boldsymbol { D }$ .
We show we may assume that in addition $b _ { i } > 0$ and $W \cdot D _ { i } > 0$ for each $i$ .
If $W \cdot D _ { i } > 0$ , then we may assume $b _ { i } > 0$ (by adding $\epsilon D _ { i }$ to $W$ if necessary).
Now consider the set $S \subset \{ 1 , \ldots , n \}$ of components $D _ { i }$ of $\boldsymbol { D }$ such that $W \cdot D _ { i } = 0$ .
By connectedness of $\boldsymbol { D }$ we find $b _ { i } > 0$ for each $i \in S$ .
Thus $\operatorname { S u p p } ( W ) = D$ .
By the Hodge index theorem the intersection matrix $( D _ { i } \cdot D _ { j } ) _ { i , j \in S }$ is negative definite.
Hence there exists a linear combination $\begin{array} { r } { E = \sum _ { i \in S } \alpha _ { i } D _ { i } } \end{array}$ , with $\alpha _ { i } \in \mathbb { Z }$ for each $i \in S$ , such that $E \cdot D _ { i } < 0$ for each $i \in S$ .
Now replacing $W$ by $W - \epsilon E$ , we obtain $W \cdot D _ { i } > 0$ for each $i = 1 , \ldots , n$ .

Finally we show (1.3) implies (1.1).
Since $U = Y \setminus D$ is the resolution of an affine variety $U ^ { \prime }$ with du Val singularities, we have $U ^ { \prime } = Y ^ { \prime } \backslash D ^ { \prime }$ where $Y ^ { \prime }$ is a normal projective surface and $D ^ { \prime }$ is a Weil divisor such that $D ^ { \prime }$ is the support of an ample divisor $A$ .
Let $\pi \colon \tilde { Y }  Y ^ { \prime }$ be a resolution of singularities such that $\pi | _ { \pi ^ { - 1 } ( U ^ { \prime } ) } : \pi ^ { - 1 } ( U ^ { \prime } ) \to U ^ { \prime }$ is the resolution $U  U ^ { \prime }$ .
Furthermore, we can assume the inclusion $U \subset Y$ extends to a birational morphism $f \colon { \tilde { Y } }  Y$ .
Let $\tilde { D }$ be the inverse image of $D ^ { \prime }$ under $\pi$ , so $\tilde { Y } \backslash \tilde { D } =$ $U$ .
The divisor $\pi ^ { * } A$ has support $\tilde { D }$ .
So we can write $\pi ^ { * } A = f ^ { * } ( \sum a _ { i } D _ { i } ) + \sum \mu _ { j } E _ { j }$ where the $E _ { j } ^ { ' }$ are the $f$ -exceptional curves and $a _ { i } , \mu _ { j } \in \mathbb { Z }$ .
Then $( \sum a _ { i } D _ { i } ) ^ { 2 } \geq ( \pi ^ { * } A ) ^ { 2 } =$ $A ^ { 2 } > 0$ .


Corollary 6.11. Let $( Y , D )$ be a positive Looijenga pair.
Let $P = \mathrm { { N E } } ( Y )$ .
The multiplication rule Theorem 2.34 applied with ${ \mathfrak { D } } = { \mathfrak { D } } ^ { \operatorname { c a n } }$ determines a finitely generated $T ^ { D }$ -equivariant $R = \Bbbk \lfloor P \rfloor$ -algebra structure on the free $R$ -module

$$
A = \bigoplus _ { q \in B ( \mathbb { Z } ) } R \cdot \vartheta _ { q } .
$$

Furthermore, Spec $A $ Spec $R$ is a flat affine family of Gorenstein semi-log canonical (SLC) surfaces with central fibre $\mathbb { V } _ { n }$ , and smooth generic fibre.
Any collection of $\vartheta _ { q }$ whose restrictions generate $A / { \mathfrak { m } } = H ^ { \cup } ( \mathbb { V } _ { n } , { \mathcal { O } } _ { \mathbb { V } _ { n } } )$ generate $A$ as an R-algebra.
In particular the $\vartheta _ { v _ { i } }$ generate for $n \geq 3$ .

Proof.
Everything but the singularity statement follows from Theorem 0.2. The Gorenstein SLC locus in the base is open, and $T ^ { D }$ -equivariant.
Taking a big and nef divisor $H = \sum a _ { i } D _ { i }$ with $a _ { i } > 0$ and $H \cdot D _ { i } > 0$ for all $i$ , we obtain a one-parameter subgroup of $T ^ { D }$ given by the map $\chi ( T ^ { D } ) \to \mathbb { Z }$ , $e _ { D _ { i } } \ \mapsto \ a _ { i }$ .
By definition of the weights, the weights of $z ^ { \left[ C \right] }$ for $C \in \operatorname { N E } ( Y )$ and $\vartheta _ { p }$ for $p \in B ( \mathbb { Z } )$ are all non-negative.
Thus the corresponding torus $T _ { H } \cong \mathbb { G } _ { m }$ gives a contracting action.
In particular, since $\mathbb { V } _ { n }$ is Gorenstein and SLC, all fibres are Gorenstein and SLC.
Moreover, the map

$$
H ^ { 0 } ( Z , \mathcal { O } _ { Z } ) \to H ^ { 0 } ( 3 _ { \mathfrak { m } } , \mathcal { O } _ { 3 _ { \mathfrak { m } } } )
$$

is injective, where $Z$ is the singular locus of the family $X ~  ~ S$ .
But since $R $ $H ^ { 0 } ( \mathcal { O } _ { 3 _ { \mathrm { m } } } , \mathcal { O } _ { 3 _ { \mathrm { m } } } )$ is not injective, as shown in the proof of Theorem 0.1, we deduce that the map $R \to H ^ { 0 } ( Z , { \mathcal { O } } _ { Z } )$ is not injective.
Letting $f$ be in the kernel of the map, the fibres over $\operatorname { S p e c } R \setminus V ( f )$ are smooth.
$\boxed { \begin{array} { r l } \end{array} }$

In Part II we will prove that when $\boldsymbol { D }$ is positive, our mirror family admits a canonical fibrewise $T ^ { D }$ -equivariant compactification $\mathcal { X } \subset \mathsf { \Gamma } ( \mathcal { Z } , \mathcal { D } )$ .
The restriction $( \mathcal { Z } , \mathcal { D } ) $ $T _ { Y } : = \operatorname { P i c } ( Y ) \otimes \mathbb { G } _ { m }$ comes with a trivialization $\mathcal { D } \stackrel { \sim } { \to } D _ { * } \times T _ { Y }$ .
We will show that $( \mathcal { Z } , \mathcal { D } )$ is the universal family of Looijenga pairs $( Z , D _ { Z } )$ deformation equivalent to $( Y , D )$ together with a choice of isomorphism $D _ { Z } \ { \overset { \sim } { \to } } \ D _ { * }$ .
Now for any positive pair $( Z , D _ { Z } )$ together with a choice of isomorphism $\phi \colon D _ { Z } \ { \overset { \sim } { \to } } \ D _ { * }$ , our construction equips the complement $U = Z \backslash D _ { Z }$ with canonical theta functions $\vartheta _ { q }$ , $q \in B _ { ( Z , D ) } ( \mathbb { Z } )$ .
We will give a characterisation in terms of the intrinsic geometry of $( Z , D _ { Z } )$ .
Changing the choice of isomorphism $\phi$ changes $\vartheta _ { q }$ by a character of $T ^ { D } = \mathrm { A u t } ^ { 0 } ( D _ { * } )$ , the identity component of $\mathrm { A u t } ( D _ { * } )$ .
Here we illustrate with two examples:

Example 6.12. Consider first the case $( Y , D )$ a 5-cycle of $( - 1 )$ -curves on the (unique) degree 5 del Pezzo surface, Example 3.7. In this case $T ^ { D } = T _ { Y } = \operatorname { P i c } ( Y ) \otimes _ { \mathbb { Z } } \mathbb { G } _ { m }$ , and thus by the $T ^ { D }$ -equivariance, all fibres of the restriction $\mathcal { X }  T _ { Y }$ are isomorphic.
We consider the fibre over the identity $e \in T _ { Y }$ , thus specializing the equations of Example 3.7 by setting all $z ^ { D _ { i } } = 1$ .
It’s well known that these equations define an embedding of the original $U = Y \setminus D$ into A5 — if we take the closure in $\mathbb { P } ^ { 5 }$ (for the standard compactification $\mathbb { A } ^ { 5 } \subset \mathbb { P } ^ { 5 }$ ) one checks easily we obtain $Y$ with $D$ the hyperplane section at infinity.

Now it is easy to compute the zeroes and poles:

$$
( \vartheta _ { v _ { i } } ) = E _ { i } + D _ { i } - D _ { i + 2 } - D _ { i - 2 }
$$

(indices mod 5).
In particular $\{ \vartheta _ { v _ { i } } = 0 \} = E _ { i } \cap U \subset U$ , which characterizes $\vartheta _ { v _ { i } }$ up to scaling.

Example 6.13. Now let $Y , D = D _ { 1 } + D _ { 2 } + D _ { 3 } )$ be (the deformation type of) a cubic surface together with a triangle of lines.
Let $\mathcal { X } \subset \mathrm { S p e c } ( \mathbb { k } [ \mathrm { N E } ( Y ) ] ) \times \mathbb { A } ^ { 3 }$ be the canonical embedding given by $\vartheta _ { i } : = \vartheta _ { v _ { i } }$ , $i = 1 , 2 , 3$ .
In this case, as we shall see in Part II, the scattering diagram is particularly beautiful, with every ray $\mathfrak { o }$ of rational slope occuring, with precisely six curves on the cubic surface contributing to $f _ { \mathfrak { d } }$ .
We will also show in Part II that the mirror is given by the equation

$$
\vartheta _ { 1 } \vartheta _ { 2 } \vartheta _ { 3 } = \sum _ { i } z ^ { D _ { i } } \vartheta _ { i } ^ { 2 } + \sum _ { i } \left( \sum _ { j } z ^ { E _ { i j } } \right) z ^ { D _ { i } } \vartheta _ { i } + \sum _ { \pi } z ^ { \pi ^ { * } H } - 4 z ^ { D _ { 1 } + D _ { 2 } + D _ { 3 } } .
$$

Here the $E _ { i j }$ are the interior $( - 1 )$ -curves meeting $D _ { i }$ , and the sum over $\pi$ is the sum over all possible toric models $\pi \colon Y  Y$ of $( Y , D )$ to a pair $( Y , D )$ isomorphic to $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ with its toric boundary.
(Such $\pi$ are permuted simply transitively by the Weyl group $W ( D _ { 4 } )$ by [L81], Prop.
4.5, p.
283.) The same family, in the same canonical coordinates, was discovered by Oblomkov [Ob04].
As we learned from Dolgachev, after a change of variables (in A3), and restricting to $T _ { Y }$ (the locus over which the fibers have at worst Du Val singularities) this is identified with the universal family of affine cubic surfaces (the complement to a triangle of lines on projective cubic surface) constructed by Cayley in [C1869].
The universal family of cubic surfaces with triangle is obtained as the closure in $\mathbb { A } ^ { 3 } \subset \mathbb { P } ^ { 3 }$ .
In particular, as in the first example, our mirror family compactifies naturally to the universal family of Looijenga pairs deformation equivalent to the original $( Y , D )$ .
There is again a geometric characterisation of $\vartheta _ { i }$ (up to scaling): The linear system $| - K _ { Y } - D _ { i } | = | D _ { j } + D _ { k } |$ (here $\{ i , j , k \} = \{ 1 , 2 , 3 \}$ ) is a basepoint free pencil.
It defines a ruling $\pi : Y \to { \mathbb { P } } ^ { 1 }$ which restricts to a double cover $D _ { i } \to { \mathbb { P } } ^ { 1 }$ .
Let $\{ a , b \} \subset \mathbb { P } ^ { 1 }$ be the branch points of $\pi | _ { D _ { i } }$ .
Let $p = \pi ( D _ { j } + D _ { k } ) \in \mathbb { P } ^ { 1 }$ .
There is a unique point $q \in \mathbb { P } ^ { 1 } \setminus \{ a , b , p \}$ fixed by the unique involution of $\mathbb { P } ^ { 1 }$ interchanging $a$ and $b$ and fixing $p$ .
Let $Q = \pi ^ { * } ( q ) \in | D _ { j } + D _ { k } |$ be the corresponding divisor.
The curve $Q \subset \mathbb { P } ^ { 3 }$ is a smooth conic.
In Part II we will show

Proposition 6.14. $( \vartheta _ { i } ) = Q - D _ { j } - D _ { k }$ .

# 7. Looijenga’s conjecture

In this section we apply the main construction of this paper to give a proof of Looijenga’s conjecture on smoothability of cusp singularities, Theorem 0.5. The simple conceptual idea is explained in the introduction.
Here we give the rather involved details.

7.1. Duality of cusp singularities.
We review the notion of dual cusp singularities.
By definition, a cusp is a normal surface singularity for which the exceptional locus of the minimal resolution is a cycle of rational curves.
The self-intersections of these exceptional curves determine the analytic type of the singularity, see [L73].
Cusps have a quotient construction due to Hirzebruch [Hi73] which we explain here.
See also [L81], III, §2 for this point of view.

Let $M = \mathbb { Z } ^ { 2 }$ , and let $T \in { \mathrm { S L } } ( M )$ be a hyperbolic matrix, i.e., $T$ has a real eigenvalue $\lambda > 1$ .
Then $T$ determines a pair of dual cusps as follows: Let $w _ { 1 } , w _ { 2 } \in M _ { \mathbb { R } }$ be eigenvectors with eigenvalues $\lambda _ { 1 } = 1 / \lambda$ , $\lambda _ { 2 } = \lambda$ , chosen so that $w _ { 1 } \wedge w _ { 2 } > 0$ (in the standard counter-clockwise orientation of $\mathbb { R } ^ { 2 }$ ).
Let ${ \overline { { C } } } , { \overline { { C } } } ^ { \prime }$ be the strictly convex cones spanned by $w _ { 1 } , w _ { 2 }$ and $w _ { 2 } , - w _ { 1 }$ , and let $C , C ^ { \prime }$ be their interiors, either of which is preserved by $T$ .
Let $U _ { C } , U _ { C ^ { \prime } }$ be the corresponding tube domains, i.e.,

$$
U _ { C } : = \{ z \in M _ { \mathbb { C } } \mid \operatorname { I m } ( z ) \in C \} / M \subset M _ { \mathbb { C } } / M = M \otimes \mathbb { G } _ { m } .
$$

$T$ acts freely and properly discontinuously on $U _ { C } , U _ { C ^ { \prime } }$ .
Write $Y _ { C } , Y _ { C ^ { \prime } }$ for the holomorphic hulls of $U _ { C } / \Gamma , U _ { C ^ { \prime } } / \Gamma$ , where $\Gamma$ is the group generated by $T$ .
These each have one additional point, $p \in Y _ { C }$ , $p ^ { \prime } \in Y _ { C ^ { \prime } }$ , and $( Y _ { C } , p )$ , $( Y _ { C ^ { \prime } } , p ^ { \prime } )$ are normal surface germs of cusps.

Definition 7.1. $( Y _ { C } , p )$ and $( Y _ { C ^ { \prime } } , p ^ { \prime } )$ are dual cusp singularities.

All cusps (and their duals) arise this way.

Remark 7.2. If $M$ is identified with its dual by choosing an isomorphism $\textstyle \bigwedge ^ { 2 } M \cong \mathbb { Z }$ , the cone $\overline { { C } } ^ { \prime }$ is identified with the dual cone of $\overline { C }$ .
In this way $C ^ { \prime } / \Gamma$ and $C / \Gamma$ are dual integral affine manifolds, which suggests that the duality between the corresponding cusps is a form of mirror symmetry.

To resolve the cusp singularities of $Y _ { C }$ , say, one considers the convex hull $\Xi$ of integral points in $C$ , and let $v _ { i }$ , $i \in \mathbb Z$ , be the integral points in $\partial \Xi$ , listed so that $v _ { i - 1 }$ and $v _ { i + 1 }$ are the integral points adjacent to $v _ { i }$ on $\partial \Xi$ .
Let $\tilde { \Sigma }$ be the infinite fan with twodimensional cones generated by $v _ { i - 1 } , v _ { i }$ for $i \in \mathbb Z$ .
Note that $T$ acts on $\Xi$ , and thus acts by translation $T ( v _ { i } ) = v _ { i + n }$ for some integer $n$ , which we can take to be positive by reversing the ordering of the $v _ { i }$ if necessary.
Then $X _ { \tilde { \Sigma } }$ is a toric variety with an infinite chain of $\mathbb { P } ^ { 1 }$ ’s, and $T$ acts on $X _ { \tilde { \Sigma } }$ .
There is a tubular neighbourhood $N$ of this infinite chain of $\mathbb { P } ^ { 1 }$ ’s on which the group generated by $T$ acts properly discontinuously, see [AMRT75], p.
48. Then $N / \Gamma$ is a minimal resolution of singularities of a neighbourhood of the singularity of $Y _ { C }$ .
Note the exceptional divisors $D _ { i }$ , $i$ taken modulo $n$ , with $D _ { i }$ corresponding to the ray of $\tilde { \Sigma }$ generated by $v _ { i }$ , satisfy (1.2).
Thus if there is a Looijenga pair $( Y , D ^ { \prime } )$ with $D ^ { \prime } = D _ { 1 } ^ { \prime } + \cdot \cdot \cdot + D _ { n } ^ { \prime }$ and $( D _ { i } ^ { \prime } ) ^ { 2 } = D _ { i } ^ { 2 }$ for each $i$ , the corresponding affine manifold with singularities is precisely $B = | \tilde { \Sigma } | / \Gamma$ by Example 1.10, and $B _ { 0 } = C / \Gamma$ .

In fact, the dual cusp singularity can be described directly from the cone $C$ and the polyhedron $\Xi$ :

Lemma 7.3. Let $T , { \overline { { C } } } , { \Xi }$ and the $v _ { i }$ be as above, giving a cusp singularity $p \in Y _ { C }$ .
Let $Z$ be the toric variety (only locally of finite type) associated to $\Xi$ .
Let $E \subset Z$ be the toric boundary of $Z$ , an infinite chain of smooth rational curves corresponding to the boundary of $\Xi$ .
Then there exists a tubular neighbourhood $E \subset N \subset Z$ such that the $\Gamma$ action on $\Xi$ induces a properly discontinuous $\Gamma$ action on $N$ .
Let $F \subset { \tilde { X } }$ denote the quotient of $E \subset N$ by $\Gamma$ .
So $F ^ { \prime }$ is a cycle of smooth rational curves.
Then $F \subset { \tilde { X } }$ can be contracted to a singularity $p ^ { \prime } \in X$ , which is a copy of the dual cusp $p ^ { \prime } \in Y _ { C ^ { \prime } }$ .
Moreover, $\tilde { X }$ is obtained from the minimal resolution of $p ^ { \prime } \in X$ by contracting all the $\left( - 2 \right)$ -curves.

Proof.
Let $\widetilde { \Sigma } ^ { \prime }$ be the normal fan for the polytope $\Xi$ and $\overline { { C } } ^ { \prime \prime }$ the closure of its support.  
We observe that $\overline { { C } } ^ { \prime \prime }$ coincides with the dual of $\overline { C }$ , together with the induced $\Gamma$ -action.  
By Remark 7.2, it follows that $\tilde { X }$ is a partial resolution of a copy of the dual cusp.

The surface $\ddot { X }$ has Du Val singularities of type $A$ .
Indeed, $v _ { i }$ is a vertex of $\Xi$ iff $m _ { i } : = - D _ { i } ^ { 2 } > 2$ .
The corresponding point of $Z$ is smooth if $m _ { i } = 3$ and a singularity of type $A _ { m _ { i } - 3 }$ if $m _ { i } > 3$ (by direct calculation using $v _ { i - 1 } + v _ { i + 1 } = m _ { i } v _ { i }$ ).
Also $K _ { \tilde { X } }$ is relatively ample over $X$ by Lemma 7.4 below.
Indeed, the vectors $u _ { i } : = v _ { i } - v _ { i - 1 }$ are the primitive integral vectors in the direction of the edges of $\Xi$ , and

$$
u _ { i + 1 } - u _ { i } = v _ { i + 1 } + v _ { i - 1 } - 2 v _ { i } = ( m _ { i } - 2 ) v _ { i } ,
$$

so the lines $v _ { i } + \mathbb { R } \cdot ( u _ { i + 1 } - u _ { i } ) = \mathbb { R } \cdot v _ { i }$ all meet at the origin.
We deduce that $\tilde { X }$ is obtained from the minimal resolution of $X$ by contracting all $\left( - 2 \right)$ -curves as claimed.
$\boxed { \begin{array} { r l } \end{array} }$

Lemma 7.4. Let $P \subset \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 }$ be a rational convex polygon and $X$ the associated toric surface.
Fix an orientation of the boundary of $P$ and let $e _ { 0 } , e _ { 1 } , e _ { 2 }$ denote oriented consecutive edges of the boundary of $P$ .
Let $C \subset X$ denote the component of the toric boundary associated to the bounded edge $e _ { 1 } \subset P$ .
Let $v _ { 0 } = e _ { 0 } \subset { } 1 e _ { 1 }$ and $v _ { 1 } = e _ { 1 } \lor e _ { 2 }$ be the vertices of $e _ { 1 }$ .
Let $u _ { 0 } , u _ { 1 } , u _ { 2 } \in \mathbb { Z } ^ { 2 }$ denote the primitive integral vectors in the direction of $e _ { 0 } , e _ { 1 } , e _ { 2 }$ .
Then $K _ { X } \cdot C > 0$ if and only if the lines $v _ { 0 } + \mathbb { R } ( u _ { 1 } - u _ { 0 } )$ and $v _ { 1 } + \mathbb { R } ( u _ { 2 } - u _ { 1 } )$ meet on the opposite side of $e _ { 1 }$ to $P$ .

Proof.
Write $M = \mathbb { Z } ^ { 2 } \subset \mathbb { R } ^ { 2 }$ for the lattice of characters of the torus of $X$ .
Choose an orientation $\textstyle \bigwedge ^ { 2 } M \simeq \mathbb { Z }$ and use it to identify $M$ with its dual lattice $N$ .
Let $U \subset X$ denote the union of the two toric affine open subsets corresponding to the vertices $v _ { 0 }$ and $v _ { 1 }$ of $P$ .
Then $U$ is a toric open neighbourhood of $C \subset X$ .
Then, under this identification and up to a sign, the fan $\Sigma$ of $U$ in $N _ { \mathbb { R } }$ consists of the two cones $\langle u _ { 0 } , u _ { 1 } \rangle _ { \mathbb { R } _ { \geq 0 } }$ , $\langle u _ { 1 } , u _ { 2 } \rangle _ { \mathbb { R } _ { \geq 0 } }$ and their faces.
The condition on the lines $v _ { 0 } + \mathbb { R } ( u _ { 1 } - u _ { 0 } )$ and $v _ { 1 } + \mathbb { R } ( u _ { 2 } - u _ { 1 } )$ in the statement is equivalent to the condition that the primitive generator $u _ { 1 }$ of the central ray of the fan $\Sigma$ lies on the same side of the affine line spanned by the primitive generators $u _ { 0 }$ and $u _ { 2 }$ of the two outer rays of $\Sigma$ as the origin $0 \in N$ .
Now by [R83], 4.3, this condition is equivalent to $K _ { X } \cdot C > 0$ .


Because this quotient construction is analytic we will have to deal with convergence issues to show that our construction extends to this analytic situation.

7.2. Cusp family.
In this subsection, we fix the following.
Let $( Y , D )$ be a rational surface with anti-canonical cycle, now over the field $\Bbbk = \mathbb { C }$ .
We obtain $( B , \Sigma )$ , with $\Sigma$ having one-dimensional cones $\rho _ { i }$ and two-dimensional cones $\sigma _ { i , i + 1 }$ as usual.

We assume that the intersection matrix $( D _ { i } \cdot D _ { j } ) _ { 1 \leq i , j \leq n }$ is negative definite.
Let $f \colon Y \to Y ^ { \prime }$ be the contraction (in the analytic category) of $D \subset Y$ to a cusp singularity $q \in Y ^ { \prime }$ .
We assume that $f$ is the minimal resolution of $Y ^ { \prime }$ , that is, $D _ { i } ^ { 2 } \leq - 2$ for all $i$ .
We further assume that $n \geq 3$ to avoid additional technical issues of the flavour dealt with in §6.2.
The case of Looijenga’s conjecture with $n \leq 2$ is in fact trivial, see the proof of Theorem 7.13.

Let $L$ be a nef divisor on $Y$ such that

$$
\mathrm { N E } ( Y ) _ { \mathbb { R } _ { \geq 0 } } \cap L ^ { \perp } = \langle D _ { 1 } , \dots , D _ { n } \rangle _ { \mathbb { R } _ { \geq 0 } } .
$$

Here the subscript $\mathbb { R } _ { \geq 0 }$ denotes the real cone in $A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { R } )$ generated by the given elements or set.
(Indeed, if $Y ^ { \prime }$ is projective we can take $L = h ^ { * } A$ for $A$ an ample divisor on $Y ^ { \prime }$ .
In general, let $A$ be an ample divisor on $Y$ .
There exist unique $a _ { i } \in \mathbb { Q }$ such that $\begin{array} { r } { L : = A + \sum a _ { i } D _ { i } } \end{array}$ is orthogonal to $D _ { j }$ for each $j$ .
By negative definiteness of $\langle D _ { 1 } , \ldots , D _ { n } \rangle$ , we have $a _ { i } > 0$ for each $i$ .
It follows that $L$ is nef.)

Let $\sigma _ { P } ~ \subset ~ A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { R } )$ be a rational polyhedral cone containing $\mathrm { N E } ( Y )$ , $P = \sigma _ { P } \cap$ $A _ { 1 } ( Y , \mathbb { Z } )$ the associated toric monoid.
We assume that $\sigma _ { P }$ is strictly convex and $\sigma _ { P } \cap L ^ { \perp }$ is a face of $\sigma _ { P }$ .
Let ${ \mathfrak { m } } = P \setminus \{ 0 \}$ and $J = P \setminus P \cap L ^ { \perp } \subset P$ , the radical monomial ideal associated to the face $\sigma _ { P } \cap L ^ { \perp }$ of $\sigma _ { P }$ .
We will write $S = \operatorname { S p e c } \mathbb { k } \lfloor P \rfloor$ , and for any monomial ideal $I$ , we write $S _ { I } = \operatorname { S p e c } \mathbb { k } [ P ] / I$ .

We take the multivalued piecewise linear function $\varphi$ as usual to have bending parameter $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi } = \vert D _ { \rho } \vert \in P$ .
We wish to build a deformation of the $n$ -vertex over $S _ { I }$ with $\sqrt { I } = J$ .
However, this is already a problem over $S _ { J }$ because none of the $\kappa _ { \rho , \varphi }$ lie in $J$ .
Thus we can’t apply the results of §2 directly as the standard open sets $U _ { \rho , J }$ will not glue compatibly because of issues involving triple intersections.
To deal with this, we need to shrink these open sets.
This procedure is carried out as follows.

Theorem 7.5. Fix $R > 1$ .
There exists an analytic open neighbourhood $S _ { J } ^ { \prime }$ of $0 \in S _ { J }$ and an analytic flat family $f _ { J } \colon X _ { J } \to S _ { J } ^ { \prime }$ together with a section $s \colon S _ { J } ^ { \prime } \to X _ { J }$ satisfying the following properties:

(1) The general fibre $X _ { J , t }$ of $f _ { J }$ is a Stein analytic surface with a unique singularity $s ( t ) \in X _ { J , t }$ isomorphic to the dual cusp to $q \in Y ^ { \prime }$ .

(2) For each ray $\rho _ { i } ~ \in ~ \Sigma$ there is an open analytic subset $V _ { \rho _ { i } , J } ~ \subset ~ X _ { J }$ and open analytic embeddings

$$
V _ { \rho _ { i } , J } \subset \{ ( X _ { i - 1 } , X _ { i } , X _ { i + 1 } ) \in U _ { \rho _ { i } , J } \mid | X _ { i - 1 } | < R | X _ { i } | , | X _ { i + 1 } | < R | X _ { i } | \} \subset U _ { \rho _ { i } , J }
$$

where

$$
U _ { \rho _ { i } , J } : = V ( X _ { i - 1 } X _ { i + 1 } - z ^ { [ D _ { \rho _ { i } } ] } X _ { i } ^ { - D _ { \rho _ { i } } ^ { 2 } } ) \subset \mathbb { A } _ { X _ { i - 1 } , X _ { i + 1 } } ^ { 2 } \times ( \mathbb { G } _ { m } ) _ { X _ { i } } \times S _ { J }
$$

such that

(b) $V _ { \rho , J } \cap V _ { \rho ^ { \prime } , J } = \emptyset$ unless $\rho = \rho ^ { \prime }$ or $\rho$ and $\rho ^ { \prime }$ are the edges of a maximal cone $\sigma \in \Sigma$ .
